Tunisian Laws — Comprehensive Legal Reference

A navigable compendium of the Republic of Tunisia's constitutions, codes, statutes, and regulations

Version 4.119 — July 6, 2026 (Run 119)  |  Sources: WIPO Lex, NYU GlobaLex, JurisiteTunisie, ConstitutionNet, ILO NATLEX, JORT (via 9anoun.tn), Assemblée des Représentants du Peuple, ICNL, CPJ, RSF, HRW, Amnesty International, FIDH, Carnegie Endowment, TIMEP, Business News, La Presse de Tunisie, African Manager, Kapitalis, Managers.tn, Le Temps News, Nawaat, LPA Legal, allAfrica, InforMEA, Office des Terres Domaniales, IACE, L'Economiste Maghrébin, EuroMed Rights, MERIP, Arab Reform Initiative, JURIST, Webmanagercenter, Tustex, Business News Tunisia, legislation-securite.tn (DCAF), FAOLEX, Tunisie Numérique, WebManagerCenter, RTCI, Le Monde, BCT payment-systems & monetary bulletins, europarl.europa.eu, Mondafrique, webdo.tn, urbanisme.fr, TAP (Agence Tunis Afrique Presse), Inkyfada, printempsarabe.info, OECT.org.tn, Autonomia.org, Espace Manager, Tunisie Tribune, CKF.com.tn, Jeune Afrique, IFJ, CFJustice.org, Gnet News, INS.tn (Institut National de la Statistique), Leaders.com.tn, tunisienumerique.com

Legal Family
Tunisia's legal system is predominantly civil law-based, derived from the French legal tradition with elements of Islamic law (particularly in personal status). The unified secular judiciary was established in 1956 upon abolition of Shari'a courts. The current constitution is the 2022 Constitution (adopted July 25, 2022 by referendum; published August 18, 2022), which supersedes the 1959 and 2014 constitutions. Official legal texts are published in the Journal Officiel de la République Tunisienne (JORT).
v4.0 update (June 30, 2026): Added the Finance Law 2026, the pending Foreign Exchange Code reform (draft 115/2025), the December 2023 local council elections that seated the National Council of Regions and Districts, the 2025 draft Personal Data Protection Organic Law, and developments on civil-society restrictions (LTDH/ATFD/FTDES suspensions) and Decree-Law 2022-54 prosecutions. Refreshed status notes on the Constitutional Court, INLUCC/anti-corruption institutions, and the investment code reform bill. Five new entries added (187 total); several existing entries revised for currency.
v4.1 update (June 30, 2026, Run 2): Cross-checked v4.0 against fresh research; it already captured nearly all major 2023–2026 developments tracked this run (Labor Law reform, FY2026 Finance Law, Foreign Exchange Code bill, CNRD seating, NGO reform bill, Personal Data Protection bill). Four genuine gaps were found and closed: (1) the 2024 Commercial Code bad-cheque reform (Law No. 2024-41), entirely absent; (2) the September 27, 2024 Electoral Law amendment transferring electoral-dispute jurisdiction from the Administrative Court to the Court of Appeal of Tunis, not previously noted; (3) the 1969 agricultural-land foreign-ownership restriction (Law No. 69-56) and the 2025–2026 pending state ("domanial") land regularization bills, an uncovered area; (4) updated the whistleblower-protection and electoral-law entries' descriptions in place. Two new standalone entries added (189 total); two existing entries revised for currency.
v4.2 update (June 30, 2026, Run 3): Cross-checked v4.1 against a broad parallel research sweep (Finance Laws, Penal/Labor/Central Bank/Health/Social Security legislation, post-2021 decree-laws, and pending-bill trackers). v4.1 already captured nearly all major 2023–2026 developments. Nine genuine gaps were closed: (1) the Finance Law 2023 (Decree-Law No. 2022-79), entirely absent, plus corrected placeholder law numbers on the Finance Law 2024 and 2025 entries (now Law No. 2023-13 and Law No. 2024-48, confirmed against JORT-mirroring sources); (2) the February 2024 Central Bank financing-authorization amendment (Law No. 2024-10), not previously noted on the Central Bank Law entry; (3)–(4) the two Saied-era judicial decree-laws underlying the Judicial Statute entry's "changed by presidential decree" reference (Decree-Law No. 2022-11 creating the Provisional Supreme Judicial Council, and Decree-Law No. 2022-35 granting magistrate-dismissal power), now cited with their own entries; (5)–(6) the criminal-reconciliation and anti-speculation decree-laws of March 2022 (Decree-Law Nos. 2022-13 and 2022-14), distinct from the 2017 Administrative Reconciliation Law already present; (7) the July 2025 Penal Code amendment to Articles 96/98 on abuse of function (Law No. 2025-14); (8) Tunisia's February 2024 accession to the Budapest Convention on Cybercrime (Organic Law No. 2024-9), and a correction to the cybersecurity decree-law entry noting the ANSI→ANCS agency rename it enacted; (9) the June 2024 patient-rights/medical-responsibility law (Organic Law No. 2024-32) and the October 2024 social-protection decree-law for female agricultural workers (Decree-Law No. 2024-4), both absent from the Health and Social Security sections respectively. The CNSS entry was also updated with the 2024–2025 contribution-amnesty decrees and the still-undrafted CNSS–CNRPS merger discussion. Nine new standalone entries added (198 total); three existing entries revised for currency.
v4.3 update (July 1, 2026, Run 4): Cross-checked v4.2 against a further research sweep focused on the narrow June 30 – July 1, 2026 window plus a scan of structurally plausible gaps (competition, mining, water, copyright, and public-debt legislation). v4.2 already captured nearly all major developments; three genuinely new standalone items and several refinements were found: (1) the April 30, 2026 wage-increase package (Decrees No. 2026-66, 2026-67 and 2026-68) setting SMIG/SMAG and general salary scales for 2026–2028, entirely absent; (2) the Presidential Decree No. 2026-108 (JORT, June 8, 2026) ratifying a US$500 million Afreximbank financing facility supporting the FY2026 budget, not previously noted; (3) the Decree-Law No. 2025-3 (JORT, October 3, 2025) framework for "sociétés communautaires" (community enterprises), a wholly uncovered area of Kais Saied's "new social project" economic legislation, distinct from the Social and Solidarity Economy Law already implied elsewhere. Six existing entries were refined for currency: the Finance Law 2026 entry now cites the wealth-tax basis (Art. 88) with its exemptions and June 2026 Note Commune n°13 implementing guidance; the Constitutional Court entry now notes pending Organic Law Proposal No. 100/2025; the Copyright Law entry now notes pending Bill Proposal No. 2024-47; the Competition Law and Mining Code entries now note preparatory "radical revision" and code-modernization efforts respectively; the Water Code entry now notes a reported (unconfirmed) new "Code de l'eau" in preparation; and the civil-society-suspensions entry now includes the May 5, 2026 suspension of Avocats Sans Frontières Tunisia. Three new standalone entries added (201 total); six existing entries revised for currency.
v4.4 update (July 1, 2026, Run 5): Cross-checked v4.3 against a fresh research sweep, with adversarial verification of every candidate finding before inclusion — one claim (that a "Loi n° 011-2025/ALT" had reformed the Associations Law) was investigated and confirmed to be a hallucinated Burkina Faso statute misattributed to Tunisia; it was discarded, and the existing entry (Decree-Law No. 2011-88 still unamended, reform bills pending since 2023) is confirmed accurate with no change needed. Three genuine, independently verified items were added or refined: (1) a new standalone entry for Proposition de loi n° 42/2024, a pending 49-article ARP bill regulating social-media/online commerce sales (cahier des charges requirement), in committee as of June 2026; (2) the Insurance Code entry now notes a March 29, 2026 bill by 14 ARP deputies to raise road-accident compensation caps, pending; (3) the Road Traffic Code entry now notes the "violence routière" reform package — automatically confirmed as a draft approved by the Council of Ministers on July 28, 2025 (correcting a since-discarded claim that it was already enacted as law on that date) and still awaiting ARP examination as of December 2025. One new standalone entry added (202 total); two existing entries revised for currency.
v4.5 update (July 1, 2026, Run 6): Cross-checked v4.4 against a fresh two-pass research sweep (broad gap scan, then independent verification of every candidate before inclusion). v4.4 already captured essentially all enacted 2023–2026 legislation; the remaining gaps were three genuinely uncovered pending bills, all confirmed across multiple independent sources: (1) a draft "Code du Numérique" (Digital Code) — a ~258-article ministerial text to replace the 2001 Telecommunications Code entirely, deregulating MVNO/ISP/data-operator entry and restricting licensing to scarce resources (spectrum, submarine cables, satellite slots); previously only alluded to in passing on the Cybercrime Law entry ("pre-dates the current Digital Code effort") with no standalone entry — now added; (2) a gambling/betting law reform effort, an entirely uncovered area: the baseline Decree-Law No. 74-20 of October 24, 1974 (games of chance/lotteries) was not previously documented at all; a 23-deputy reform bill (filed January 19, 2026, referred to committee January 29, 2026) to criminalize unlicensed online gambling, plus a parallel Ministry of Youth & Sports sports-betting oversight bill awaiting Council of Ministers review, are now documented — note that specific penalty figures reported by some outlets (prison terms, fine amounts) could not be independently confirmed against a primary text and were deliberately omitted; (3) the Tax Adviser Organic Law (proposition de loi organique n° 2023-013), a deputy-initiated bill to regulate the "conseiller fiscal" profession, under ARP Commission de la Législation Générale review with sessions confirmed for June 3 and June 13, 2026. All three are pending, not-yet-enacted bills, documented as such. No changes were made to the Personal Data Protection or Constitutional Court entries — a candidate refinement (specific bill-proposal numbers) could not be corroborated on a second independent pass and was excluded rather than risk an unverified citation. Three new standalone entries added (205 total); no existing entries altered.
v4.6 update (July 1, 2026, Run 7): Cross-checked v4.5 against a fresh research sweep spanning constitutional, criminal, labor, tax, financial, digital, land, transport, and energy law, followed by independent citation verification before inclusion. v4.5 already captured essentially every enacted and pending 2023–2026 development tracked this run (Finance Law 2026, the Constitutional Court's continued non-establishment, the July 2025 Penal Code corruption amendment, the May 2025 anti-subcontracting labor law, the September 2024 electoral-law amendment, 2026 SMIG/SMAG figures, the pending Code des Changes and Investment Code revisions, the pending Personal Data Protection bill, and the Mining/Hydrocarbons/Road Traffic Code entries) — in several cases with more precise, independently-verified citations than this run's own initial research turned up (e.g., this run's draft finding of a "May 2025" electoral amendment and a "Law No. 2025-16" labor statute were both superseded by v4.5's already-correct September 27, 2024 and Law No. 2025-9 citations, and were discarded rather than introducing a regression). One genuine gap was found and closed: a dedicated Expropriation Law entry (Law No. 2016-53 of July 11, 2016, as amended by Decree-Law No. 2022-65 of October 19, 2022) was entirely absent from the Land & Property section — previously only implied via the Investment Law's Art. 21–25 reference to protection against expropriation. An initial draft citation (based on this run's own research) mis-dated the base law to "August 11, 2016" and the pending Dec. 2024 Hedhili working session to "December 2025"; both were corrected after independent verification against InforMEA and the Office des Terres Domaniales before publication. One new standalone entry added (206 total); no existing entries altered.
v4.7 update (July 1, 2026, Run 8): Cross-checked v4.6 against a fresh research sweep, then independently re-verified each candidate finding via targeted follow-up searches before inclusion. Several candidate findings turned out to already be captured near-identically by v4.5/v4.6 (the Digital Code's 258-article structure, the Personal Data Protection bill's 123-article/6-title structure, the gambling-reform bill's mechanics, the Tax Adviser Organic Law's committee timeline, and the Code des Changes bill's summer-2026 vote target) and were left unchanged rather than duplicated. Three genuine items were confirmed across multiple independent sources and added: (1) the NGO reform entry was refined with the April 2026 organic law proposal's concrete structure (45 articles/8 chapters) and its Article 26 foreign-funding pre-authorization mechanism, plus the ARP Rights and Freedoms Committee's June 24, 2026 report deadline; (2) a new standalone entry for the Unified Electoral Code proposal (29 deputies, May 2026) — a broader codification effort distinct from the narrower September 2024 amendment already documented, aiming to consolidate all electoral and referendum rules into a single text; (3) a new standalone entry for Consumer Credit & Household Over-indebtedness — a wholly uncovered area, documenting the dormant December 2020 Council-of-Ministers bill and the March 2026 IACE policy note reviving calls for its promulgation, with the "113/2020" bill number explicitly flagged as sourced from secondary reporting rather than a primary JORT record. Lower-confidence candidates (a nascent Health Code initiative, a sports-governance organic law, and a National Council of Tunisians Abroad reform) were investigated but excluded for insufficient independent corroboration or missing bill citations, consistent with this project's practice of omitting unverifiable claims rather than risking inaccurate citations. Two new standalone entries added (208 total); one existing entry revised for currency.
v4.8 update (July 1, 2026, Run 9): A targeted follow-up check identified one genuine, previously overlooked gap despite this project's extensive coverage of criminal and constitutional law: Organic Law No. 2018-50 of October 23, 2018 on the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination was entirely absent from the artifact, even though it is one of Tunisia's most internationally documented statutes (the first anti-racial-discrimination criminal law in the MENA region). The new entry cites the law's adoption/promulgation dates, Article 2's definition of racial discrimination, the criminal penalties and legal-entity fines it establishes, and the Article 11 National Commission against Racial Discrimination, and adds a 2024–2026 implementation note — drawn from EuroMed Rights, MERIP, and Arab Reform Initiative analysis — that enforcement has been limited, alongside the case of Mnemty president Saadia Mosbah, whose 8-year sentence on separate money-laundering/illicit-enrichment charges was upheld on appeal in June 2026 and which Amnesty International and other rights groups describe as ordinary criminal law being used against a leading anti-racism advocate. One new standalone entry added (209 total); no existing entries altered.
v4.9 update (July 1, 2026, Run 10): Cross-checked v4.8 against a fresh research sweep spanning labor, migration/asylum, and treaty ratifications, then independently re-verified each candidate finding before inclusion. Most candidates duplicated existing entries (the May 2025 subcontracting reform, the September 2024 electoral-jurisdiction amendment, and the Decree-Law 2022-54 press-freedom entry were already documented, in v4.8's case with a more precise citation than this run's own initial research turned up). Three genuine items were confirmed across independent sources and added: (1) Law No. 2024-44 of August 12, 2024 (Maternity and Paternity Leave Reform), entirely absent — expands paternity leave from a pre-existing 1-day (private sector) / 2-day (civil servants) baseline to 7–10 days at full pay and restructures maternity leave into prenatal/postnatal components; (2) the June 2024 UNHCR suspension of asylum registration and status-determination processing in Tunisia, confirmed via HRW's 2025 and 2026 World Report chapters, added to the Entry and Residence of Foreigners entry; (3) Decree No. 23 of 2025 (JORT Issue No. 4, January 11, 2025) ratifying the North Western Sahara Aquifer System tripartite groundwater consultation mechanism with Algeria and Libya, a wholly uncovered treaty. Also fixed a structural bug: the judicial tag class was used on 5 entries but had no corresponding CSS color rule or custom property, rendering those tags uncolored — added --tag-judicial and its .tag.judicial rule. Two new standalone entries added (211 total); two existing entries revised for currency; one CSS bug fixed.
v4.10 update (July 1, 2026, Run 11): Cross-checked v4.9 against a fresh research sweep focused on climate, AI governance, and a re-verification of the labor, tax, criminal, digital, and electoral entries closed in earlier runs (all confirmed already accurate and left unchanged, avoiding duplication). Nearly every 2025–2026 development already appeared in the artifact by this point — the two genuine refinements found were updates to existing entries rather than uncovered gaps: (1) the Paris Agreement / NDC entry was updated with Tunisia's third Nationally Determined Contribution (NDC 3.0), published end of April 2026 and presented at a Gammarth seminar (May 16–17, 2026) to 200+ non-state actors — raising the carbon-intensity reduction target to 62% by 2035 (2010 baseline) and estimating US$55 billion in financing needs for 2026–2035; (2) the Personal Data Protection entry was given a brief, deliberately hedged AI-governance note: Tunisia's 2026–2030 National Development Plan references finalizing and launching a national AI strategy (building on an earlier 2018 strategy), but no decree or law number could be identified for it as of mid-2026, so it is documented as a policy-level initiative rather than as a standalone legal entry — consistent with this project's practice of not asserting a legal citation that cannot be independently confirmed. No new standalone entries added (211 total remains accurate); two existing entries revised for currency.
v4.11 update (July 1, 2026, Run 12): Cross-checked v4.10 against an independent, broad research sweep spanning post-2021 institutional decree-laws, the Finance Laws for 2025/2026, wage decrees, cybersecurity, data protection, foreign exchange reform, and previously-uncovered codes (transport, maritime, aviation, mining, hydrocarbons, PPP, legal professions). Essentially every candidate finding from this sweep was already captured — in most cases with more precise citations than this run's own initial research produced (e.g., this run's draft "Loi n° 62-13 du 24 avril 1962" for the maritime code and "Decree-Law 2011-79" for the bar association were both superseded by the artifact's already-correct Law No. 62-36/March 27, 1962 and Law No. 87-79/December 29, 1987 citations, and were discarded rather than introducing a regression). One genuine, independently corroborated gap was found and closed: the dissolution of the National Authority for Access to Information (INAI) in early August 2025 — confirmed across ARTICLE19, the IDEA Global State of Democracy tracker, and regional press (The New Arab, APAnews) — had not been reflected anywhere in the artifact despite the Access to Information entry otherwise being current; the entry now documents the dissolution and its effect of removing the law's independent enforcement mechanism. The State of Emergency entry was also refined with the specific end-of-January 2026 renewal decree extending the emergency through December 31, 2026 (the tenth consecutive year), replacing a more generic "extended for years" description. No new standalone entries added (211 total remains accurate); two existing entries revised for currency.
v4.12 update (July 1, 2026, Run 13): A broad multi-source research sweep (Finance Laws, local governance, wage decrees, cybercrime enforcement, migration, nationality, pensions) found that nearly every development had already been captured by prior runs, in most cases with equal or better precision (e.g., this run's own research separately re-derived the SMIG/SMAG decrees, the Finance Law 2026 wealth-tax overhaul, the pending Foreign Exchange Code and Personal Data Protection bills, and the 2025 civil-society suspensions — all already present and, on comparison, more precisely sourced than this run's initial drafts). Four genuine gaps were closed, all existing-entry refinements rather than new standalone cards: (1) the Local Government Code entry now notes that Organic Law No. 2025-4 of March 12, 2025 repealed its region/district provisions (and Organic Law 89-11 of 1989), replacing the elected-region model with decree-organized councils — a legislative change with no prior mention in the artifact; (2) the Nationality Code entry now notes a pending February 27, 2026 bill (36 deputies) that would tighten naturalization to 7 years' legal residence while easing rules for stateless persons and creating a skilled-foreigner track; (3) the Decree-Law 2022-54 (press) entry now cites the specific February 3, 2025 Cour de Cassation ruling (Criminal Chamber 29, Sonia Dahmani case) limiting the decree's scope, replacing a vague "2024 court ruling" reference, and notes Dahmani's subsequent April 2026 conviction under a separate case showing inconsistent application; (4) the CNSS entry now cites two specific pending pension bills (Proposition de loi 16/2026 on retirement deferral; Projet de loi 104/2025 on women's early retirement) previously described only in general "merger discussed" terms. No new standalone entries added (211 total remains accurate); four existing entries revised for currency.
v4.13 update (July 1, 2026, Run 14): Two parallel research sweeps (recent 2023–2026 legislation, and entirely uncovered legal-code areas) initially surfaced roughly 30 candidate items across tax, labor, transport, energy, culture, health, and institutional topics — cross-checking against the artifact showed essentially all of them were already present, in most cases with more precise citations than this run's own initial drafts (e.g., this run's draft citations for the Sport Law, Cinema Law, Blood Transfusion Law, and Organ Transplant Law were all superseded by the artifact's already-correct Law No. 1995-11, Law No. 93-40, Law No. 91-72, and Law No. 91-22 entries, and were discarded rather than introducing a regression; a draft claim that Finance Law 2023/2024 taxed telecommunications operators directly was also investigated and found to be inaccurate — that levy applied only to banks, financial establishments, and insurers, per the official Note Commune n°7/2024 — and was not added). Three genuine gaps were closed after independent verification: (1) Décret n° 2025-165 of March 20, 2025 (JORT No. 2025-033) appointing Sarra Zaafrani Zenzri as Head of Government in place of Kamel Madouri — the fourth such change since 2021 — entirely absent from the artifact despite its extensive institutional coverage; (2) the Investment Law entry now cites Décret n° 2024-182 of April 4, 2024, which amended the law's implementing regulation (Décret gouvernemental n° 2017-389 of 2017, not the 2016 law itself) on incentive-claim deadlines and eligible agricultural services; (3) the Finance Law 2026 entry now notes that the bank/insurer conjunctural contribution was reportedly made permanent and extended to telecommunications operators and auto dealers, sourced to two independent secondary outlets (ilBoursa, InFirst Auditors) with an explicit caveat that the specific implementing article could not be confirmed against primary JORT text. Also fixed a version-string bug: the page's <title> tag had been left at "v4.11" for two prior runs while the header/footer correctly read "v4.12" — now synchronized. One new standalone entry added (212 total); two existing entries revised for currency; one version-string bug fixed.
v4.14 update (July 1, 2026, Run 15): This run initially started from a stale local checkout of the artifact's v1.0 baseline (87 entries) rather than current main; the mistake was caught before merging by fetching and diffing against origin/main (v4.13, 212 entries), and the branch was reset to match current main rather than risk a large regression — consistent with how prior runs (see the discarded PRs preceding v2.0 and v4.10) have handled the same failure mode. A subsequent, narrower gap-check confirmed that virtually every 2023–2026 development surfaced by this run's initial research (Finance Laws 2024–2026, Law No. 2025-9, Law No. 2024-41, Law No. 2024-10, CNRD establishment, Constitutional Court status, the pending Personal Data Protection and NGO-funding bills) was already present, in most cases with more precise citations. Three genuine gaps were closed: (1) the Higher Education Law entry now notes a pending 27-deputy bill to amend Article 15, replacing elected university presidents with ministry-appointed ones (opposed by FGESRS, under debate as of February 2026); (2) the Judicial Officers (Huissiers) entry now notes a pending modernization bill containing a provision allowing mutual-consent divorce before a huissier-notaire without court involvement, criticized by women's rights groups as weakening Personal Status Code safeguards; (3) a new standalone entry for the 2026–2030 National Development Plan bill, examined by all ARP standing committees June 29–30, 2026 but not yet put to a plenary vote — the framework expected to underpin the AI strategy and NDC 3.0 climate plan already noted elsewhere in this document. Also added a missing .tag.pending CSS rule (used by these three entries but previously undefined). One new standalone entry added (213 total); two existing entries revised for currency; one CSS gap fixed.
v4.15 update (July 1, 2026, Run 16): This run also began from a stale local checkout (a v1.0-era baseline, 87 entries) rather than current main; per the same failure mode documented in the v4.14 entry above, this was caught before merging by fetching origin/main (v4.14, 213 entries) and resetting the branch to match it. A subsequent research sweep spanning finance/tax, the Foreign Exchange Code bill, the 2026–2030 Development Plan, the Water Code, renewable energy, e-commerce, and health-insurance (CNAM) reform found no genuine gaps: every lead either (a) duplicated an existing entry with equal or better precision (the Finance Law 2026 wealth-tax and e-invoicing provisions, the May 2025 anti-subcontracting labor law, the IFAD Thirteenth Replenishment ratification, the Code des Changes committee's June 17, 2026 status — commission at ~90% of hearings, weighing a vote without the Central Bank's input — already matched what this run's own research turned up almost verbatim), (b) remained an unconfirmed draft-stage measure not yet traceable to enacted JORT text (preliminary PLF 2026 press reports of additional levies on phone recharges, "cahier des charges" fees, and large-retailer invoices could not be confirmed against the final Law No. 2025-17 text and were not added), or (c) was misattributed foreign content on inspection — a purported "Office des Changes" digital platform launch and an "auto-production d'électricité Law No. 82-21" both resolved to Morocco's Office des Changes and Moroccan legislation respectively, not Tunisia, and were discarded. No new standalone entries added (213 total remains accurate); no existing entries altered. Consistent with the project's stated practice (task item 5), this run confirms the artifact is current as of July 1, 2026; see "Areas for Future Expansion" below for topics to prioritize once genuinely new legislation emerges.
v4.16 update (July 1, 2026, Run 17): This run correctly started from current origin/main (v4.15, 213 entries) rather than a stale checkout. A fresh research sweep targeted the seven items listed in "Areas for Future Expansion" plus a general scan for newly enacted JORT text since the v4.15 confirmation run earlier the same day. No genuinely new legislation, standalone gap, or enacted bill was found — consistent with v4.15's conclusion that the artifact is current — but four existing pending-bill entries were refined with more precise detail confirmed across independent sources: (1) the Foreign Exchange Code entry now cites the bill's 91-article count, notes hearings additionally covering the Ministry of Finance and Customs Directorate, and the economists' estimated 1–1.5 percentage-point GDP growth impact; (2) the 2026–2030 National Development Plan entry now cites the plan's specific growth targets (4.2% average annual growth vs. 2.4% previously, 5.1% by 2030), names Minister of Economy and Planning Samir Abdelhafidh's June 30, 2026 hearing, and clarifies the remaining procedural steps (synthesis report → ARP Bureau → plenary → Conseil National des Régions et des Districts); (3) the Proposition de loi n° 42/2024 (online commerce/social-media marketing) entry now notes the bill's consumer-protection chapter, including a three-day right of withdrawal (droit de rétractation) and a bar on non-disclosure of a seller's identity and licensing details; (4) the CNAM (Law No. 2004-71) entry now documents the fund's 2026 liquidity crisis (a theoretical ~TND 900 million surplus alongside acute cash shortfalls) and the January 2026 tripartite payment-guarantee agreement with pharmacists, while noting no CNAM reform bill has yet reached the ARP. No new standalone entries added (213 total remains accurate); four existing entries revised for currency.
v4.17 update (July 1, 2026, Run 18): Verified this branch was reset to current origin/main (v4.16, 213 entries, 19 merged runs) before starting, rather than a stale local checkout — the failure mode documented repeatedly in prior run notes above. A fresh research sweep re-checked the seven "Areas for Future Expansion" items (Foreign Exchange Code, Development Plan bill, Personal Data Protection & AI bill, Associations Decree reform, Water Code replacement, gambling reform, CNAM reform) and found no enactments or plenary votes — all remain at the same committee stage previously documented, confirming this artifact's existing detail is current. Two genuine standalone gaps were identified and closed, both previously undocumented pending bills: (1) a new Draft Law on Combating Financial Exclusion — a government (Ministry of Finance) bill built on the National Financial Inclusion Strategy, heard by the Finance & Budget Committee on June 26, 2026, addressing Tunisia's low banking/financial-service penetration (~60% of adults hold any bank/postal account; single-digit uptake of mobile finance and voluntary insurance); (2) Proposition de loi n° 83/2025 — a July 2025 parliamentary bill creating a dedicated criminal framework (6 months–10 years' imprisonment depending on the act) for stadium and sports-facility violence, under review by the ARP's Education Commission. Both were independently corroborated across multiple sources before inclusion. Two new standalone entries added (215 total); the "Areas for Future Expansion" watchlist was refreshed accordingly.
v4.18 update (July 1, 2026, Run 19): This run's local checkout again began from the stale v1.0 baseline (87 entries) rather than origin/main; per the failure mode documented repeatedly above, this was caught immediately (before any content was drafted) by checking git log against GitHub's PR history, fetching origin/main (v4.17, 215 entries, 20 merged PRs), and resetting the branch to match it. A structural audit found no regressions: all 215 law-card ids unique, all `.tag` classes styled, all internal anchors resolving, balanced HTML tags. A fresh research sweep independently re-verified the seven "Areas for Future Expansion" items via web search and source fetches rather than relying on the prior run's notes alone. Most leads confirmed the existing detail was already accurate or more precise than newly-found sources (e.g., the Personal Data Protection bill's "123 articles" and the nationality bill's "36 co-sponsors, February 27, 2026" both matched independently). This is a new false-positive pattern for future runs to watch for, alongside the cross-country-mislabeling (Run 74), search-engine-summary hallucination (Run 76), and database-timestamp (Run 74) risks already flagged above: a shared colloquial nickname ("loi 52," "loi 115," etc.) between two unrelated statutes can cause a search summary to attribute one bill's content to the wrong law entirely — always verify the law number against a direct quote from a primary or near-primary source before adding or amending an entry on this basis.
