Enacted: December 30, 1989 | In force: January 1, 1990 | Amended: annually in Finance Laws
Governs personal income tax (IRPP) and corporate income tax (IS) in Tunisia. Personal income tax is progressive with rates from 0% to 35%. Corporate income tax standard rate is 15%, with higher rates for banks and telecoms (35%) and reduced rates for SMEs and certain sectors. Amended annually through the Finance Law.
Key rates (as of 2024)
  • Personal income tax: 0% up to 5,000 TND, 26% bracket, 35% top rate
  • Corporate income tax: 15% standard rate
  • Banks, financial institutions, telecoms: 35%
  • SMEs: reduced rate of 10% (conditions apply)
  • Withholding tax on dividends: 10%
  • Capital gains tax: included in income tax
TaxAmended

VAT Code — Law No. 88-61 of June 2, 1988Code de la Taxe sur la Valeur Ajoutée

Enacted: June 2, 1988 | In force: January 1, 1989 | Amended: annually
Establishes value-added tax in Tunisia. Standard rate 19%; reduced rates of 13% (certain services) and 7% (basic goods, agricultural products). Exports are zero-rated. Registration required for businesses with turnover exceeding 100,000 TND/year (services) or 200,000 TND (goods).
TaxAmended

Tax Procedures Code — Law No. 2000-82 of August 9, 2000Code des Droits et Procédures Fiscaux

Enacted: August 9, 2000 | In force: 2001 | Amended: annually
Unifies and governs all tax administration procedures including filing obligations, tax audits, assessment, payment, penalties, and dispute resolution. Establishes taxpayers' rights during tax audits. Provides for administrative appeals before tax committees and judicial appeals before administrative courts.
Tax

Customs Code — Law No. 2008-77 of December 22, 2008

Enacted: December 22, 2008 | In force: 2009 | Replaced: 1955 Customs Code
Governs importation, exportation, transit, and customs duties. Establishes the legal framework for customs declarations, inspections, duty rates, and customs offenses. Aligns Tunisia's customs procedures with international standards (WCO Revised Kyoto Convention). Governs free zones and customs warehouses.
TaxCommercial

Local Taxes Law — Law No. 97-11 of February 3, 1997

Enacted: February 3, 1997 | In force: 1997 | Amended: multiple times
Regulates taxes and fees collected by municipalities including property taxes (TFP — taxe foncière sur les propriétés bâties et non bâties), tourism taxes, advertising taxes, and local business taxes (TCL — taxe sur les établissements à caractère industriel, commercial ou professionnel).
TaxAdministrative

Decree-Law No. 2022-79 of December 22, 2022Finance Law 2023

Enacted: December 22, 2022 | Published: JORT No. 141, December 23, 2022 | In force: January 1, 2023
Annual budget law, exceptionally issued as a presidential decree-law rather than an ordinary parliamentary statute because the Assembly of People's Representatives had not yet resumed full legislative activity following the December 2022 legislative elections. Set state expenditure at approximately TND 54 billion against TND 46.4 billion in revenue (deficit of approximately TND 7.5 billion). Introduced a real-estate wealth tax on individuals' property holdings worth TND 3 million or more, and temporary customs/VAT relief on EV charging equipment.
Tax

Law No. 2023-13 of December 11, 2023Finance Law 2024

Adopted by ARP: December 10, 2023 (116 for, 4 against, 26 abstentions) | Enacted: December 11, 2023 | Published: JORT, December 12, 2023 | Fiscal year: 2024
First annual Finance Law passed by the elected Assembly of People's Representatives since 2021. Key changes: Corporate Income Tax (CIT) increased from 15% to 20% for general companies effective January 1, 2024. Bank and insurance company CIT rate increased from 35% to 40%. New 2% conjunctural contribution on FY2023 taxable income for companies with turnover ≥ TND 20 million. Standard deduction for rental income raised from 20% to 25%. New 4-year CIT/IRPP exemption for enterprises filing investment declarations in 2024–2025. Raised import duties on solar panels from 10% to 30% effective January 1, 2025.
TaxAmended

Law No. 2024-48 of December 9, 2024Finance Law 2025

Adopted by ARP/CNRD: December 2 & 4, 2024 | Enacted: December 9, 2024 | Published: JORT No. 149, December 10, 2024 | Fiscal year: 2025
Key measures: VAT reduced to 7% (from 13%) on low-voltage electricity for domestic consumers using ≤300 kWh/month. New conjunctural budget contribution (2% on FY2024 taxable income) for large companies (turnover ≥ TND 20M). Additional tax incentives for R&D-intensive companies. Community enterprises exempt from VAT under new suspension regime. Reinforces tax inspection powers for real estate valuations. Raises low pensions to TND 260 with roughly 7–7.5% increases for retirees, plus income-tax relief for pensioners, and begins phasing in mandatory electronic invoicing for an expanded range of service providers.
TaxAmended

Law No. 2025-17 of December 12, 2025Finance Law 2026

Adopted: ARP December 4, 2025 (89 for/23 against/12 abstain); Council of Regions and Districts December 7, 2025 | Published: JORT No. 148, December 12, 2025 | Fiscal year: 2026
State budget sets revenues at TND 52.56 billion and expenditures at TND 63.575 billion, with tax receipts of TND 47.773 billion as the dominant funding source. Key measures: Art. 88 establishes a new annual "wealth tax" (impôt sur la fortune) on Tunisian-resident individuals' worldwide net patrimony (non-residents taxed only on Tunisian-situs assets, subject to double-tax treaties), at 0.5% on net wealth between TND 3–5 million and 1% above TND 5 million, assessed as of January 1 each year including minor children's assets held in trust; exempts the taxpayer's principal residence, professional assets recorded on a business balance sheet, home-savings and investment-savings accounts, life insurance/capitalization contracts, and non-utility vehicles rated ≤12 fiscal horsepower; declared electronically by end of June each year, first applying to the 2026 tax year; implementing guidance issued via Note Commune n°13 (DGELF, June 11, 2026) and n°15. Art. 56 expands mandatory e-invoicing from goods transactions to the entire services sector (~380,000 professionals affected), effective January 1, 2026 — invoices must be issued through the "El Fatoora" platform operated by Tunisie TradeNet (TTN) in the standardized TEIF/XML format and bear a qualified electronic signature issued by TunTrust/ANCE; penalties run 100–500 TND per invoice (capped at 50,000 TND/year) for invoices wrongly issued outside the electronic system where e-invoicing is mandatory, and 250–10,000 TND (doubled for repeat offenses) for non-compliant electronic invoices; tax incentives for new/used vehicle imports for resident families; state-subsidized interest bonuses (up to 3 points) on investment credits taken out in 2026–2027; salary and pension increases staged across 2026–2028 by decree; VAT exemption on packaging inputs for locally produced olive oil; VAT reduced to 7% on EV charging equipment and lithium battery manufacturing inputs; authorization for farmers to import and use agricultural drones. A parliamentary amendment adopted December 2, 2025 (69–17–17) additionally authorizes Tunisian residents to open and hold foreign-currency bank accounts for the first time — see Exchange Control Law entry. Tunisian financial-press sources (ilBoursa, InFirst Auditors) also report that the FY2024–2025 "conjunctural contribution" of 4% on taxable profit — previously limited to banks, financial establishments, and insurers (see the Finance Law 2024 entry) — was made permanent under this law and extended to telecommunications network operators and automobile dealers/concessionaires; this extension is corroborated by two independent secondary sources but the specific implementing article number could not be confirmed against a primary JORT text during this research pass, so it is noted here with that caveat. Art. 83 (added Run 109): revises tax and customs advantages for Tunisians residing abroad (TRE) — full customs-duty exemption on electric and plug-in-hybrid vehicle imports (VAT reduced to 7%, customs-service fees to 3%), a once-per-five-years equipment/materials import benefit (plus a separate single-truck allowance under tariff heading 8704) for TRE-backed investment, extension, or participation projects in Tunisia, and exemption from filing a Tunisian tax return for TRE who present a foreign tax-residency certificate — corroborated across Managers.tn, Tunisie Numérique, La Presse de Tunisie, and InFirst Auditors' commentary on the law; see also the National Council for Tunisians Residing Abroad entry (Nationality section). Pending amendment (added Run 118): the law's core assumptions have been overtaken by an oil-price shock — the budget was set on a reference Brent price of USD 63.3/barrel, versus actual prices approaching USD 110/barrel by June 2026 (each additional dollar costing roughly TND 160 million; a financing gap estimated at TND 5–8 billion by year-end). ARP Finance and Budget Commission president Maher Ktari stated on radio June 9, 2026 that recourse to a "loi de finances rectificative" (or, per some economists' competing proposal, a more flexible scenario-based "loi de finances d'urgence") in September or October 2026 is now "inevitable," while criticizing the Finance Ministry for not yet engaging the commission on the amendment's substance; a ministerial meeting to examine the discrepancy was scheduled for July 2026. No amending bill text had been filed with the ARP as of this run — corroborated across La Presse/allAfrica, Business News, Kapitalis, Tustex, and webdo, but treat as a forecast/committee-pressure signal, not yet a pending bill with a registry number.
TaxAmendedPending

Proposition de Loi Organique n° 2023-013Statute of the Tax Adviser Profession

Filed: 2023 | ARP Commission de la Législation Générale review ongoing: article-by-article vote October 23, 2025; disputed-article sessions June 3 and June 18, 2026 | Not yet finalized | Not yet enacted
A deputy-initiated organic bill (proposition de loi organique n° 2023-013, also cited as 13/2023) to create and regulate a licensed "conseiller fiscal" (tax adviser) profession in Tunisia, defining qualification requirements, a professional order, and the rights and duties of practitioners advising on tax matters. Reviewed by the Assembly of People's Representatives' Commission de la Législation Générale since at least mid-2025; the Ministries of Finance and Justice raised objections concerning overlap with the Code des Droits et Procédures Fiscaux and existing rules governing the legal (lawyer) profession. The commission held an article-by-article vote on the full bill text on October 23, 2025, but a residual set of "disputed articles" (fasl khilafiya) remained unresolved. Correction: examination was not finalized by early June as previously tracked — a June 3, 2026 session (following an earlier study day) specifically reviewed those remaining disputed articles, and the commission resumed again on June 18, 2026 to continue that same review and set its forward work program; no finalization or vote has been confirmed since. Status as of July 2026: still in committee review, not yet scheduled for plenary, not yet adopted.
TaxAdministrative

🏦 Financial & Banking Law

Banking Law — Law No. 2016-48 of July 11, 2016

Enacted: July 11, 2016 | In force: 2016 | Replaced: Law No. 2001-65
Regulates credit institutions, banking activities, and financial services in Tunisia. Establishes licensing requirements for banks and microfinance institutions. Strengthens the supervisory powers of the Central Bank of Tunisia (BCT). Introduces new categories of banking licenses and regulates Islamic banking activities.
Key provisions
  • Licensing: exclusive reserve of banking activities for licensed credit institutions
  • Capital requirements: minimum TND 25 million for universal banks
  • Supervision: BCT has powers to inspect, sanction, and revoke licenses
  • Islamic banking: participatory banks regulated under specific provisions
  • Deposit guarantee: Fund for Deposit Guarantee created
  • Resolution: framework for failing banks established
Financial

Central Bank Law — Law No. 2016-35 of April 25, 2016

Enacted: April 25, 2016 | In force: 2016 | Replaced: Law No. 58-90 (1958) | Amended: 2024, 2026
Establishes the Banque Centrale de Tunisie (BCT) as an independent institution responsible for monetary policy, exchange rate management, payment systems oversight, and banking supervision. Grants operational independence to the BCT Governor. Article 25 originally prohibited direct central bank financing of the state budget deficit. This prohibition was exceptionally suspended by Law No. 2024-10 of February 7, 2024 (JORT No. 2024-021, adopted by the ARP 92 votes to 41), authorizing the BCT to lend the Treasury up to TND 7,000 million, interest-free, repayable over 10 years with a 3-year grace period, to help cover the 2024 budget deficit — a measure Carnegie Endowment and TIMEP analyses describe as eroding central bank independence. Update (Run 40): Finance Law 2026 Art. 12 authorizes a further, separate interest-free BCT advance to the Treasury of up to TND 11,000 million (up from TND 7,000 million in 2024/2025). Independent of fiscal financing, the BCT cut its policy rate in three steps from 8% to 7% between late 2025 and March 2026 (held at 7% at its March 30, 2026 meeting); a March 26, 2026 circular (No. 2026-4) newly restricts bank financing of "non-priority" imports, generally requiring 100% importer-funded deposits; and in January 2026 the BCT granted OFT Tunisie (Ooredoo's fintech arm) a definitive payment-institution license (JORT No. 65/2026).
FinancialAmended

Insurance Code — Law No. 92-24 of February 9, 1992

Enacted: February 9, 1992 | In force: 1992 | Amended: 1997, 2002, 2008, 2014
Governs all insurance and reinsurance activities in Tunisia. Mandatory insurance categories include third-party motor liability, employer liability, and professional liability for certain professions. Regulates insurance contract terms, claims procedures, and policyholder protections. Insurance companies supervised by the Insurance General Committee (CGA). Pending reform: on March 29, 2026, 14 ARP deputies filed a bill to raise road-accident compensation caps under the motor-liability provisions introduced by amending Law No. 2005-86: proposed indexation to 5× annual income for a surviving spouse, 10× the SMIG for victims without stable income, a 3-year SMIG supplement for breadwinners, and triennial inflation-linked revision of caps. Not yet enacted as of June 2026; the exact proposition number was not independently confirmed.
Financial

Microfinance Law — Law No. 2011-117 of November 5, 2011

Enacted: November 5, 2011 | In force: 2011
Liberalizes and regulates the microfinance sector, allowing private and associative organizations to offer microcredit services beyond the former state monopoly (Banque Tunisienne de Solidarité). Establishes the Supervisory Authority for Microfinance (ACM). Maximum loan amounts and interest rate caps specified.
FinancialSocial

Financial Market Law — Law No. 94-117 of November 14, 1994

Enacted: November 14, 1994 | In force: 1995 | Amended: 1999, 2005, 2016
Governs the Tunisian stock exchange (Bourse des Valeurs Mobilières de Tunis — BVMT), securities regulation, and capital markets. Creates the Financial Market Council (CMF — Conseil du Marché Financier) as the securities regulator. Regulates public offers, disclosure requirements, insider trading, and market manipulation.
FinancialCommercial

Law No. 2006-19 of May 2, 2006Deposit Guarantee Fund

Enacted: May 2, 2006 | In force: 2006
Creates the Tunisian Deposit Guarantee Fund (FGTD — Fonds de Garantie des Dépôts Tunisiens). Provides protection for bank depositors up to TND 20,000 per depositor per institution. Funded by mandatory bank contributions. Administered under BCT supervision. Coverage extended under the 2016 Banking Law reforms to include savings banks and electronic money institutions.
Financial

BCT Circular 2011-06 and Law 2001-65 amendmentsFinancial Leasing Regulation

Circular: 2011 | Framework updated: 2016 Banking Law
The BCT regulates leasing companies as financial institutions. Capital requirements, prudential ratios, and provisioning rules apply. The 2016 Banking Law (No. 2016-48) updated the regulatory framework for specialized financial institutions including leasing and factoring companies.
Financial

Law No. 76-18 of January 21, 1976 (as amended by Finance Law 2026)Exchange Control Law

Enacted: January 21, 1976 | Amended: multiple times, most recently Finance Law 2026 (Dec. 2025)
Governs foreign exchange transactions, capital movements, and currency restrictions; historically prohibited Tunisian residents from holding foreign currency. A parliamentary amendment to the 2026 Finance Law, adopted December 2, 2025 (69 for/17 against/17 abstain) — after a near-identical proposal was narrowly rejected in November 2024 (51 against/48 for) — for the first time authorizes resident individuals and businesses to open and fund foreign-currency bank accounts, ending a 50-year-old restriction. This 2025 amendment is a stopgap pending the wholesale replacement of the 1976 law by the new Foreign Exchange Code (Draft Law 115/2025) — see separate entry.
FinancialCommercialAmended

Draft Law No. 115/2025 (pending)New Foreign Exchange Code (Code des Changes)

Registered with ARP: 2025 | Finance & Budget Committee hearings: ~90% complete as of June 17, 2026 | Status: not yet enacted as of July 1, 2026
Comprehensive replacement of the 1976 Exchange Control Law and its implementing decrees — the first wholesale rewrite of Tunisia's foreign-exchange regime in 50 years, composed of 91 articles. Would broaden the definition of foreign exchange operations, formally recognize crypto-assets, create a new "licensed exchange operator" status, authorize private exchange offices for the first time, enable a regulated framework for international digital payment platforms, and guarantee immediate and unrestricted repatriation of profits, dividends, and asset-sale proceeds. The Finance and Budget Committee has held extensive hearings (including with CONECT, which submitted 41 proposed amendments, plus the Ministry of Finance and the Directorate General of Customs) and as of mid-June 2026 was weighing whether to proceed to a committee vote without the Central Bank of Tunisia's opinion, after the BCT failed to respond to repeated hearing requests. Update (Run 36): that posture has since softened rather than hardened — a June 23, 2026 report on the committee's continuing work (alongside separate STEG financing-agreement hearings) has Ktari instead calling continued BCT consultation "essential" before final adoption, with the BCT still not having formally responded as of that date; the committee's earlier openness to voting around the BCT's silence should therefore be read as superseded by this more recent, more cautious stance, not as the operative plan. Economists heard by the committee estimated the reform could add 1–1.5 percentage points to GDP growth. Committee chair Maher Ktari has reiterated parliament's aim to pass the bill before the summer 2026 recess, with additional listening sessions continuing into late June before transmission to plenary — the committee also heard the Chamber of Commerce (mixed chambers) on May 14, 2026. However, a July 1, 2026 report on the separate Development Plan debate notes MPs were still publicly urging "acceleration" of this Code's adoption that same day, indicating the bill had not yet passed and Ktari's summer-recess target should be treated as unconfirmed rather than on track. A related but separate track — Bill No. 58/2025 on amnesty for past exchange violations (see entry below) — was sent back to committee in April 2026 with reporting suggesting the two efforts are being harmonized rather than passed separately. Update (Run 70): a July 3, 2026 IACE report named this reform Tunisia's single highest enactment priority among sixteen pending economic reforms, arguing the underlying 1976 exchange-control framework is obsolete against digital-economy payment flows and contemporary cross-border finance; unlike the Investment, Water, Environment, and Hydrocarbons codes IACE separately flagged as stalled pre-ARP transmission (see those entries), this Code is already before the Finance and Budget Committee, so IACE's "priority" framing here concerns urgency of a floor vote rather than executive-stage delay.
FinancialDigital

Bill No. 58/2025 (pending)Regularization ("Amnesty") of Past Exchange-Control Violations

Filed: late May 2025 (Independent National Bloc) | Plenary examination: April 14, 2026 | Sent back to Finance Committee: April 14, 2026 (54 for / 3 against / 2 abstain) | Status: not yet enacted as of July 2026
Distinct from the wholesale Foreign Exchange Code rewrite (Draft Law No. 115/2025, above), this narrower bill would let residents holding undeclared foreign-currency assets or funds held abroad regularize their situation through repatriation and a sworn declaration, in exchange for a one-off "liberatory contribution": reported rates of 15% of value for foreign real estate/financial assets, and 7% of the dinar-equivalent for repatriated sums (reduced to 4% if fully converted to dinars). Payment would extinguish both the underlying exchange-control and tax violations. Brought to a plenary vote on April 14, 2026 after a first committee pass, but following hours of debate and a wave of proposed amendments, deputies voted 54–3–2 to send the text back to the Finance Committee for further work rather than adopting or rejecting it outright — some press coverage described this as the amnesty being "postponed indefinitely," though the bill was not formally withdrawn or defeated. Critics (including deputy Abderrazak Houas) argued the draft was insufficiently rigorous on the source-of-funds and money-laundering questions raised by an amnesty of this kind. Reporting since December 2025 indicates the Finance Committee is now working to harmonize this amnesty mechanism with the broader Foreign Exchange Code reform and Tunisia's anti-money-laundering framework rather than advancing it as a standalone text — watch the Foreign Exchange Code entry above for developments, as the two efforts may ultimately merge.
FinancialTaxPending

Draft Law No. 023/2024 (pending)Combating Financial Exclusion

Filed by the Presidency of the Republic: March 6, 2024 | Referred to Finance & Budget Committee: March 7, 2024 | Committee hearing: June 26, 2026 | Status: not yet enacted as of July 2, 2026
A government-drafted bill to overhaul Tunisia's financial-inclusion framework, developed as the legislative centerpiece of the National Financial Inclusion Strategy. Motivated by a Ministry of Finance diagnosis showing only around 60% of Tunisians over 18 interact with a bank or postal financial institution at all, with far lower uptake of modern financial services (roughly 17% use non-cash payment methods, 3% use mobile financial services, under 2% hold voluntary insurance). Structured around four axes: expanding access to and use of financial services; consolidating sectoral oversight; consumer protection and financial-literacy promotion; and rationalizing financial flows. Correction (Run 43): the "n° 38/2026 and n° 39/2026" numbering attributed to this bill in a prior run was in error — those numbers in fact belong to the unrelated STEG IBRD loan-guarantee bills approved by the same committee two days after this bill's hearing (see standalone entry above). This bill's number is confirmed via the ARP's own official bill registry (arp.tn/loi/project/4016) as Draft Law No. 023/2024 ("مشروع قانون عدد 023 لسنة 2024 يتعلّق بمكافحة الإقصاء المالي"), filed by the Presidency of the Republic on March 6, 2024 and pending in committee since March 7, 2024 — the June 26, 2026 session was a committee hearing on this long-pending 2024 bill, not a newly filed 2026 text. The Finance and Budget Committee hearing was chaired by committee president Maher Ktari with vice-president Dhafer Sghiri and deputies including Amal Moueddeb, Mohamed Ben Hassine, Mustapha Boubakri, and Mohamed Zied El Maher, with officials from the Ministry of Finance, the Microfinance Supervision Authority, and the General Insurance Committee. A central sticking point has been a proposed "banque postale" (postal bank) — now identified as a related but formally separate proposition de loi, n° 82/2025, filed by 21 deputies, which would offer expanded banking-type services through La Poste Tunisienne's network under Central Bank of Tunisia supervision without converting La Poste into a full bank; on June 28, 2026 the Ministry of Finance publicly "temporized" on this specific proposal, describing it as a "strategic option" requiring further legal and economic study and broader stakeholder consensus. Distinct from the pre-existing Microfinance Law (2011) and Banking Law (2016), which the bill would supplement rather than replace. Not yet put to a committee vote or enacted as of this update.
FinancialSocialPending

Proposition de loi n° 82/2025 (pending)Creation of a "Banque Postale" (Postal Bank)

Filed: 2025, by 21 co-sponsoring deputies | Status: not yet enacted as of July 2, 2026
A multi-party member's bill (proposition de loi), distinct from the government's financial-exclusion bill (Draft Law No. 023/2024, above) though addressing an overlapping policy goal, that would authorize La Poste Tunisienne to offer expanded banking-type services — savings, credit, and payment products — through its extensive nationwide branch network under Central Bank of Tunisia supervision, without converting La Poste into a full-fledged licensed bank. Proponents frame it as a financial-inclusion tool reaching underbanked rural and low-income populations already served by La Poste's existing savings-account and payment infrastructure. The Ministry of Finance publicly "temporized" on the proposal on June 28, 2026, describing it as a "strategic option" requiring further legal and economic study rather than something to be resolved within the parallel financial-exclusion bill, signaling likely continued delay. Not yet examined to a committee vote as of this update.
FinancialPending

Law No. 88-108 of August 18, 1988Chartered Accountants (Experts Comptables) (Run 54 addition)

Enacted: August 18, 1988 | Published: JORT No. 56, August 26, 1988 | Superseded: Law No. 82-62 of June 30, 1982
"Portant refonte de la législation relative à la profession d'expert comptable" — creates the Ordre des Experts Comptables de Tunisie (OECT) as a body with legal personality, under Ministry of Finance supervision. Defines the "expert comptable" profession: organizing, verifying, and certifying company accounts, and — subject to conditions — acting as statutory auditor (commissaire aux comptes) referenced in the Companies Code entry above. Sets diploma/qualification requirements for admission to the Order and its disciplinary framework. Wholly absent from this artifact despite 53 prior research runs; verified directly against oect.org.tn.
FinancialAdministrative

Law No. 2002-16 of February 4, 2002Accounting Profession (Compagnie des Comptables de Tunisie) (Run 54 addition)

Enacted: February 4, 2002 | Amended: Law No. 2004-88 of December 31, 2004 (Art. 28 transitional registration deadlines)
"Portant organisation de la profession des comptables" — creates the Compagnie des Comptables de Tunisie, a distinct civil-law professional body (Art. 4) for non-expert accountants performing bookkeeping and general accounting services, who may also perform statutory-auditor functions for commercial companies under specified conditions. Complements, without merging into, the chartered-accountants' Order (OECT, Law No. 88-108, above). Wholly absent from this artifact despite 53 prior research runs.
FinancialAdministrative

Draft Text No. 113/2020 (unpromulgated)Household Over-Indebtedness (Surendettement) (Run 83 addition)

Adopted in principle: Council of Ministers, December 2020 | Status: still unpromulgated as of March 2026 (5+ years)
A wholly new standalone gap identified via a March 2026 IACE report ("Le surendettement des ménages : une urgence nationale," March 27, 2026) and corroborated by L'Economiste Maghrébin and WebManagerCenter (both March 27–28, 2026): Tunisia has no legal definition of household over-indebtedness, no dedicated observatory, no centralized credit bureau, and no specialized adjudication commission, despite household debt reaching roughly TND 32 billion — described as a "critical over-indebtedness threshold." Draft text n°113/2020, adopted in principle by a Council of Ministers in December 2020, would define over-indebtedness as the debtor's manifest inability to honor non-professional debts within a reasonable time, coupled with a good-faith criterion, but remains unpromulgated more than five years later. IACE's 2026 proposal builds on this dormant text with binding prudential debt-to-income ratio caps (40% for consumer credit, 50% for mortgage credit), a per-governorate over-indebtedness commission offering a seven-year recovery plan, partial debt write-off with a reinsertion commitment, or total write-off with a three-year credit ban, plus a national observatory (TND 500,000 first-year budget) and a centralized credit bureau (TND 7 million over three years, funded 50% BCT / 30% international donors / 20% banking sector). None of this has reached the ARP as a filed bill as of this run — the gap is the absence of any consumer-over-indebtedness framework, not a pending-bill status to track under the standard watchlist.
FinancialSocialDraft

📈 Investment & Business Environment

Investment Law — Law No. 2016-71 of September 30, 2016

Enacted: September 30, 2016 | In force: April 2017 | Replaced: Investment Code of 1993
Tunisia's primary investment statute, replacing the 1993 Investment Code. Applies to both domestic and foreign investment. Guarantees freedom of investment, equal treatment of foreign and domestic investors, protection against expropriation (with compensation), and transfer of profits and capital. Establishes the Investment Authority (FIPA — Foreign Investment Promotion Agency) and the Investment Development Agency (IDI). Creates investment incentives including tax benefits for priority sectors and regions. Implementing update: Décret n° 2024-182 of April 4, 2024 (JORT No. 2024-047) amended the executive regulation on financial incentives, Décret gouvernemental n° 2017-389 of March 9, 2017 — not the 2016 law itself — setting a 2-year deadline from the investment declaration certificate date to submit incentive claims with a feasibility study, requiring exceptional deadline-extension requests to be filed 6 months before expiry, and clarifying which agricultural services (milk collection, grain storage, seed conditioning, hay storage) qualify for regional-development incentives.
Key provisions
  • Freedom of establishment for foreign investors (with exceptions in certain sectors)
  • Art. 7: Equal treatment of foreign investors
  • Art. 21–25: Protection against nationalization and expropriation
  • Art. 26: Freedom to transfer capital, profits, and salaries of foreign staff
  • Incentive framework: preferential tax rates for investment in priority sectors
  • Regional development incentives for investments in interior regions
  • Foreign ownership: up to 100% in most sectors; limited in certain strategic sectors
  • Single window (guichet unique) for investment approvals
CommercialFinancial

Free Zones Law — Law No. 92-81 of August 3, 1992

Enacted: August 3, 1992 | In force: 1993 | Amended: 2007, 2009, 2019
Creates special economic zones (free zones) offering customs and tax advantages for export-oriented businesses. Free zone companies are exempt from import duties, VAT on local purchases, and income tax for initial periods. Currently active free zones include Bizerte Economic Activity Park. Requires minimum 70% export of production.
CommercialTax

Startup Act — Law No. 2018-20 of April 17, 2018

Enacted: April 17, 2018 | In force: August 2018
Innovative legislation creating a special legal and fiscal framework for innovative startups. Eligible startups receive a "Startup" label granting advantages including: salary coverage for founders for 1 year, exemption from employer social security contributions for 3 years, preferential tax treatment, facilitated foreign currency access, and streamlined administrative procedures. One of Africa's first dedicated startup laws.
Key provisions
  • "Startup" label granted by the Startup Tunisia Committee
  • Founder salary covered by the state for up to 1 year
  • 3-year exemption from employer social security contributions
  • 5-year corporate tax holiday
  • Foreign currency account allowed for startups
  • Fast-track work permits for foreign talent
  • Crowdfunding and convertible notes facilitated
CommercialDigital

Arbitration Code — Law No. 93-42 of April 26, 1993

Enacted: April 26, 1993 | In force: 1993
Governs domestic and international commercial arbitration in Tunisia. Based on the UNCITRAL Model Law. Provides for arbitration agreements, constitution of tribunals, conduct of proceedings, and recognition and enforcement of awards. Tunisia ratified the New York Convention on the Recognition and Enforcement of Foreign Arbitral Awards in 1967.
Commercial

Law No. 2015-49 of November 27, 2015Public-Private Partnership Law

Enacted: November 27, 2015 | In force: 2015
Creates the legal framework for PPP contracts between the state and private operators. Defines PPP modalities: concessions, affermage (lease-operate), and BOT contracts. Establishes the General Committee for Public-Private Partnerships under the Ministry of Finance. Requires competitive tendering and feasibility studies. Enables private participation in infrastructure (ports, airports, highways, water, energy).
CommercialAdministrative

Law No. 92-81 and Investment CodeOffshore (Totally Exporting) Companies Framework

Established: 1992 | Updated: Investment Code 2016
Regime for 100% export-oriented foreign investment: exemption from all customs duties, exemption from VAT on purchases, tax holiday for first 10 years, then 50% reduction. Must export ≥70% of production. May employ up to 30% of workforce from Tunisia. Separately regulated from free zones; offshore companies may locate anywhere in Tunisia.
CommercialTax

Decree-Law No. 2022-15 (as substantially amended by Decree-Law No. 2025-3 of October 3, 2025)Sociétés Communautaires (Community Enterprises)

Original framework: 2022 | Major reform: Decree-Law No. 2025-3, published JORT October 3, 2025
Part of President Kais Saied's "new social project" economic program, this framework creates a distinct legal form — the "community enterprise" — intended as a third pillar of the economy alongside the public and private sectors, oriented toward participatory local and regional economic development. Decree-Law 2025-3 substantially overhauled the original 2022 framework: it distinguishes "local" community enterprises (participants from a single delegation, minimum 10 members) from "regional" ones (participants from multiple delegations within a governorate, minimum 15 members); sets minimum capital at TND 5,000 (local) and TND 10,000 (regional); grants a 10-year total exemption from taxes and duties from formation, VAT suspension on certain operations, preferential bank-loan interest rates, National Guarantee Fund coverage for financing, and priority long-term leasing (up to 40 years, with a 5-year rent exemption) of state-owned agricultural and non-agricultural land; creates a National Register of Community Enterprises conferring legal personality and a unique identifier; and sets governing-board composition at 3–10 members elected for renewable 3-year terms with conflict-of-interest rules. The Finance Law 2025 separately extended VAT-suspension treatment to community enterprises (see Finance Law 2025 entry).
CommercialTax

Law No. 89-9 of February 1, 1989Public Enterprises & Privatization Framework

Enacted: February 1, 1989 | Amended: Laws No. 94-102, 96-74, 99-38, 2001-33, 2006-36
Tunisia's foundational statute governing state holdings ("participations"), public enterprises (wholly state-owned companies, and those where the state, local authorities, or public establishments jointly or individually hold over 50%), and public establishments. Defines the legal mechanisms for restructuring these entities — including share sales or exchanges, mergers, divisions, and asset sales — providing the general framework that enables privatization (transfer of state-owned shareholdings to the private sector). Privatization decisions are administered by CAREPP (Commission d'Assainissement et de Restructuration des Entreprises à Participation Publique), created under this law and detailed by Decree No. 97-410. Distinct from the narrower 2011 Confiscation Law entry above, which targets only assets seized from Ben Ali-clan associates via the separate Tunisian Confiscation Commission/Privatisation Authority.
CommercialFinancial

Draft Investment Code Reform Bill (pending)Proposed Overhaul of Law No. 2016-71

Approved in principle: Restricted Ministerial Council, December 31, 2025 | Under committee review: ARP Strategic Planning Commission (article-by-article vote stage) | Status: not enacted as of July 1, 2026
A government-sponsored bill to revise the 2016 Investment Law, launched at a Conseil ministériel restreint on December 31, 2025 and alongside the related Foreign Exchange Code reform (Draft 115/2025), as part of a broader push to modernize Tunisia's business and capital-flow environment. As of late May 2026, the ARP Strategic Planning Commission — chaired by Saber Jelassi — had moved to article-by-article voting after incorporating several amendments, following expert hearings and consultations with business organizations (Conect, Utica, Utap). Goals reported by officials and business groups include streamlining incentive eligibility, easing the single-window approval process, and aligning investment guarantees with the liberalized exchange regime. Reporting as of late June 2026 describes the amended text replacing the current prior-authorization regime with a prior-declaration principle for most investments, coupled with a positive (tacit-approval) administrative-silence rule under strict response deadlines for the administration, plus a proposed "licence premium" fast-track status to attract larger investors; this description was independently repeated by officials at the 22nd Tunisia Investment Forum (June 25–26, 2026), a second source corroborating the reform direction — but none of these provisions could be confirmed against a primary committee text as of this update, so they remain noted as reported reform direction rather than settled article language. Update (Run 38): Tunisian financial press (Tustex, late May/early June 2026) explicitly frames the reform as tied to the 2026–2030 National Development Plan's investment targets, citing a 3.3% GDP growth objective for 2026 specifically and an approximately TND 29.9 billion total public-and-private investment target for the year — figures presented alongside, not yet reconciled against, the Development Plan bill's own five-year targets documented in that entry's standalone card. As of July 1, 2026 the 2016 Investment Law remains unchanged and fully in force; the reform bill had not yet reached a plenary vote. Tracking note: a follow-up search also surfaced a separate, MP-initiated proposition n°26/2024 amending the 2016 law under the same Strategic Planning Commission, distinct from the government-driven track described above; it remains unclear from open sources whether these are the same initiative described differently or two parallel bills before the same commission — flagged for clarification in a future run rather than merged into a single narrative here. Update (Run 70): a July 3, 2026 IACE report ("cartographie" of Tunisia's pending economic-code reforms), independently corroborated by African Manager, La Presse de Tunisie, and Tustex, offers a partial resolution of that ambiguity: it classifies the Investment Code among five priority reforms — alongside the Water, Environment, and Hydrocarbons codes — that are "technically finalized and validated by the Council of Ministers but never transmitted to the ARP," attributing the bottleneck to unfinished interministerial arbitration and sectoral resistance rather than to parliamentary delay. Read together with the ARP Strategic Planning Commission's own article-by-article voting activity documented above, this suggests the government-driven track (approved in principle December 31, 2025) may be the one still stalled pre-transmission, while the committee voting already underway concerns the separate MP-initiated proposition n°26/2024 — a plausible but not yet independently confirmed reconciliation of the two tracks. IACE separately named the Investment Code one of its top three enactment priorities for the remainder of 2026, citing a decline in the overall investment rate from 19.3% of GDP (2016) to 16% (2024) and average FDI inflows down 25% (US$728M/year in 2019–2023 vs. US$974M/year in 2014–2018), and recommended a Prime Ministry-based coordination unit with binding milestones to move all sixteen tracked reforms forward.
CommercialFinancial

Law No. 2020-30 of June 30, 2020Social and Solidarity Economy Law (Run 81 addition)

Enacted: June 30, 2020 | Published: JORT N°63, July 3, 2020 (pp. 1399–1403)
A wholly new standalone entry — this artifact's own Run 4 changelog previously described this law as "already implied elsewhere," but a full-text search confirmed no standalone citation for it actually existed; only the distinct 2025 "sociétés communautaires" decree-law (see above) was documented. Confirmed via direct reading of the scanned Journal Officiel text (JORT N°63, hosted by ILO NATLEX). The first framework law of its kind in the Maghreb (Morocco and Algeria had no equivalent text at the time of enactment, per leaders.com.tn), giving legal status and a governance structure to the "économie sociale et solidaire." Article 2 defines the sector as an economic model of social-purpose activities whose primary goal is not profit distribution; Article 3 lists covered entity types — cooperatives, agricultural/fisheries development groups, mutual associations, microfinance associations, mutual insurance companies, Decree-Law 2011-88 associations conducting social-purpose economic activity, and certain qualifying companies/economic-interest groupings. Creates a Conseil supérieur de l'économie sociale et solidaire (Art. 7) and an Instance tunisienne de l'économie sociale et solidaire (Art. 8). Secondary sourcing indicates the law remained largely unimplemented roughly ten months after passage pending its application decrees — an implementation gap, not a citation issue.
CommercialSocial

💡 Intellectual Property Law

Law No. 2009-33 of June 23, 2009Literary and Artistic Property (Copyright)

Enacted: June 23, 2009 | In force: 2009 | Replaced: Law No. 94-36
Governs copyright and related rights in Tunisia. Protects literary, artistic, and musical works, software, databases, and audiovisual productions. Protection term: author's life + 70 years. Covers moral rights (inalienable) and economic rights (transferable). Aligns with WIPO Copyright Treaty and TRIPS Agreement obligations. Enforced by the Tunisian Copyright Protection Office (OTPDA). Pending reform: Bill Proposal No. 2024-47, amending and supplementing the copyright framework to address digital-era exploitation of works, is under review by the ARP's Rights and Freedoms Committee; a June 24, 2026 parliamentary study day examined the draft's likely impact on the protection regime. Not yet enacted as of July 2026.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–10: Scope — literary, artistic, musical, audiovisual, photographic works; software
  • Art. 15: Moral rights (right of integrity, right of attribution) — inalienable and perpetual
  • Art. 16–22: Economic rights — reproduction, distribution, public performance
  • Art. 24: Duration — life of author + 70 years
  • Art. 45–55: Related rights for performers, producers, broadcasters
  • Art. 60–75: Exceptions and limitations (private use, education, libraries)
  • Art. 87–100: Civil and criminal remedies for infringement
IP

Law No. 2000-84 of August 24, 2000Industrial Property (Patents, Trademarks, Designs)

Enacted: August 24, 2000 | In force: 2001 | Amended: 2007
Governs patents, trademarks, geographical indications, industrial designs, and utility models in Tunisia. Administered by the Tunisian Intellectual Property Office (INNORPI). Tunisia is a member of WIPO, the Paris Convention, the Patent Cooperation Treaty (PCT), and the Madrid Protocol on trademarks.
Key provisions
  • Patents: 20-year protection from filing date; requires novelty, inventive step, industrial application
  • Trademarks: 10-year protection, renewable; registration at INNORPI required
  • Industrial designs: 5-year protection, renewable up to 4 times (25 years total)
  • Geographical indications: protection for Tunisian products with geographic origin
  • Compulsory licenses: available for public health or national emergency
IP

Law No. 99-42 of May 10, 1999Plant Variety Protection

Enacted: May 10, 1999 | In force: 1999
Provides plant breeders' rights (PBR) over new plant varieties. Tunisia is a member of the International Union for the Protection of New Varieties of Plants (UPOV). Protection term: 20 years (25 years for vines and trees). Administered jointly by INNORPI and the Ministry of Agriculture. Protects commercially registered varieties from unauthorized reproduction, conditioning, sale, or export.
IPAgriculture

COC Arts. 83–100 and Decree No. 2018-417Trade Secret and Unfair Competition

COC base: 1906 | Regulatory update: 2018
Tunisian law protects trade secrets through COC provisions on unjust enrichment and breach of confidence. The 2018 regulatory decree reinforces protection against industrial espionage and unauthorized disclosure of commercial secrets. Penal Code Art. 254 criminalizes breach of professional secrecy. Unfair competition (dénigrement, parasitism, misleading origin) actionable under COC Art. 83 and the Competition Law.
IPCommercial

Law No. 2001-21 of February 6, 2001Protection of Semiconductor Topographies

Enacted: February 6, 2001 | In force: February 2001
Protects original topographies (layout designs) of integrated circuits (semiconductors) against unauthorized copying and commercial exploitation. Implements Tunisia's obligations under the TRIPS Agreement (WTO, Art. 35–38) and the Washington Treaty on Intellectual Property in Respect of Integrated Circuits (1989). Protection lasts 10 years from the date of first commercial exploitation or 15 years from creation, whichever ends first. Registration with INNORPI (Institut National de la Normalisation et de la Propriété Industrielle) is required for protection. Reverse engineering and independent creation are permitted; exhaustion of rights applies to legitimately marketed products.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–5: Definitions: topography, integrated circuit, layout design
  • Art. 6–10: Originality requirement; rights conferred on creator
  • Art. 11–15: Registration with INNORPI; filing requirements
  • Art. 16–20: Duration of protection (10 years from first commercial use)
  • Art. 21–26: Permitted acts: private use, research, teaching, reverse engineering
  • Art. 27–32: Infringement remedies: civil damages, seizure, destruction of infringing articles
  • Art. 33–36: Criminal sanctions: up to 2 years + fines for willful infringement
IP

Law No. 2009-38 of June 30, 2009National Standardization System

Enacted: June 30, 2009 | Repealed: Law No. 82-66 of August 6, 1982
Establishes the general legal framework for standardization in Tunisia, distinct from the trademark/patent registration functions covered by the Industrial Property Law above, though both are administered by the same institute. Confirms the National Institute of Standardization and Industrial Property (INNORPI — Institut National de la Normalisation et de la Propriété Industrielle) as the body responsible for managing the national standardization system: developing, publishing, and periodically revising Tunisian standards (normes tunisiennes) through a consensus-based process grounded in impartiality and independence, and providing normative monitoring (veille normative) to inform economic operators and the public of applicable standards and technical documents. States standardization's policy purposes — strengthening the national economy, facilitating trade, improving product/service quality and competitiveness, protecting consumer health and safety, protecting the environment, and supporting sustainable development. Tunisian standards are voluntary by default, but all or part of a standard can be rendered mandatory by a joint order of the minister in charge of industry and the sector-competent minister (technical regulations); Art. 8 provides for temporary exemptions from mandatory-standard compliance where an operator faces genuine difficulty applying it.
IPCommercial

🌿 Environmental Law

Law No. 88-91 of August 2, 1988Framework Environmental Law

Enacted: August 2, 1988 | In force: 1988 | Amended: 1992, 2001
Tunisia's foundational environmental statute, creating the National Environment Protection Agency (ANPE). Establishes environmental impact assessment (EIA) requirements for major projects. Governs pollution prevention, waste management, and natural resource protection. Establishes the principle of "polluter pays." Pending consolidation (added Run 73): the Ministry of Environment has been drafting a wholesale replacement — a unified "Code de l'Environnement" of roughly 400 articles consolidating this framework law with the Waste Management, Marine Pollution, EIA-decree, and related sectoral texts below — described by the Minister as fully rewritten and finalized (environnement.gov.tn; TAP, July 2026). A July 3, 2026 IACE report on stalled economic reforms lists this Environment Code, alongside the Investment, Water, and Hydrocarbons codes (see those entries), as "technically finalized and validated by the Council of Ministers" but "never transmitted to the ARP" — an executive-stage bottleneck shared with the other three. No draft article text or ARP registry number has been published; Law No. 88-91 remains the operative statute pending transmission.
Environmental

Water Code — Law No. 75-16 of March 31, 1975

Enacted: March 31, 1975 | In force: 1975 | Amended: 1987, 2001
Classifies all water resources (surface and groundwater) as public domain. Governs water use permits, irrigation rights, water conservation, and protection from pollution. Establishes the National Water Authority and regional water committees. The 2022 Constitution (Art. 48) enshrines the right to safe drinking water as a fundamental entitlement. Emergency water restriction orders issued in 2023 following severe drought; a summer-2026 emergency water program (81 projects, TND 58 million) was launched to secure supply. Reported pending reform (unconfirmed): government officials referenced preparation of a new, comprehensive "Code de l'eau" as of early 2025; reported structure would comprise seven chapters (guiding principles; identification and preservation of the water public domain; governance; use and exploitation; water-service provision; risk management; and criminal protections). Update (Run 51): the source and date for this reported structure are now precisely identified — Agriculture, Water Resources & Fisheries Minister Ezzeddine Ben Cheikh presented the draft to a Conseil ministériel restreint on February 25, 2025, with the ministry instructed to amend it in coordination with the government's legal and legislative advisory services before finalizing a version for submission to the Council of Ministers and then parliament (La Presse de Tunisie, Webmanagercenter, L'Economiste Maghrébin, allAfrica, all dated March 11, 2025). A single, separate and still-uncorroborated report describes the draft as having since been submitted to the Prime Ministry (Présidence du Gouvernement) for review ahead of that transmission, but this remains unconfirmed by a second source or a confirmed date. No public draft text or enactment had been confirmed as of mid-2026, and the 1975 Code remains in force. What has been confirmed and enacted, distinct from the rumored wholesale replacement, is an incremental amendment: Article 65 of the Finance Law 2026 created a "Fonds de l'Eau" (Water Fund) financed by a tariff surcharge on bottled-water companies — a targeted funding mechanism layered onto the existing 1975 framework rather than a new code. Update (Run 70): a July 3, 2026 IACE report, corroborated across African Manager, La Presse de Tunisie, and Tustex, sharpens the "submitted to the Prime Ministry" claim considerably: it states the new Water Code is one of five pending codes (with Investment, Environment, and Hydrocarbons) "technically finalized and validated by the Council of Ministers" but "never transmitted to the ARP" — a specific, now multiply-sourced procedural status, though still short of a primary government or JORT confirmation naming this Water Code draft by number or article count.
Environmental

Forestry Code — Law No. 88-20 of March 13, 1988

Enacted: March 13, 1988 | In force: 1988
Classifies and protects Tunisian forests and natural parks. Regulates logging permits, forest fires (criminalized), afforestation, and pastoral use of forest land. Creates the Directorate General of Forests under the Ministry of Agriculture. Pending reform advocacy (Run 112): a January 2026 FTDES report describes the code as unreformed since 1988 and calls for a dedicated "Forests and Climate" chapter to unlock REDD+ climate-financing mechanisms; no bill or Council of Ministers text has been filed as of mid-2026.
Environmental

Waste Management Law — Law No. 96-41 of June 10, 1996

Enacted: June 10, 1996 | In force: 1996 | Amended: 2007
Governs waste prevention, collection, transport, treatment, and disposal. Creates the National Agency for Waste Management (ANGed). Establishes the waste hierarchy (prevention, reuse, recycling, recovery, disposal). Special provisions for hazardous waste, medical waste, and industrial effluents.
Environmental

National Sanitation Office (ONAS) — Law No. 74-73 of August 3, 1974

Enacted: August 3, 1974 | Amended: Law No. 93-41 of April 19, 1993 | In force: ongoing
Creates the Office National de l'Assainissement (ONAS), the public utility responsible for wastewater collection, treatment, and sanitation infrastructure across Tunisia's urban network. Law No. 93-41 (1993) expanded ONAS's originally urban-only mandate to industrial-effluent pretreatment oversight and broader rural/peri-urban sanitation coverage, and strengthened its tariff-setting and financing authority (the sanitation surcharge collected via SONEDE water bills). ONAS operates alongside SONEDE (drinking-water distribution) and ANPE/the Ministry of Environment (pollution enforcement — see the Framework Environmental Law and Marine Pollution entries above) within Tunisia's water-sector regulatory architecture. (Added Run 82 — a genuine standalone gap outside the pending-bill watchlist: the sanitation utility's own governing statute, distinct from the Water Code and Marine Pollution Law, had been absent from this document through 81 prior runs.)
EnvironmentalAdministrative

Law No. 2017-49 of July 3, 2017Paris Agreement Ratification

Ratified: July 3, 2017 | COP21 Agreement: December 12, 2015
Tunisia ratified the Paris Agreement on Climate Change. Nationally Determined Contribution (NDC) commits Tunisia to a 41% reduction in carbon intensity by 2030 (conditional on international support). Updated NDC submitted in 2021 with binding targets for renewable energy, energy efficiency, and transport emissions. Tunisia also ratified the Kyoto Protocol (2002) and the Vienna Convention on ozone depletion. NDC 3.0 (2026 update): Tunisia published its third NDC update in late April 2026 and presented it to over 200 non-state actors at a Gammarth seminar on May 16–17, 2026. NDC 3.0 raises the carbon-intensity reduction target to 62% by 2035 (from a 46.4%-by-2030 trajectory), against the 2010 baseline, and estimates financing needs of roughly US$55 billion for 2026–2035 (about 53% for adaptation — led by water and food security — and 47% for mitigation). It builds on a national climate finance taxonomy adopted in 2025 and is intended to be integrated into the state budget and the 2026–2030 national development plan rather than implemented as standalone legislation.
EnvironmentalConstitutional

Law No. 95-73 of July 24, 1995Maritime Public Domain and Coastal Protection

Enacted: July 24, 1995 | In force: 1995 | Decree No. 2004-2727 (implementing)
Defines and protects the maritime public domain (50 metres from high-tide line). No construction permitted without prior authorization from APAL (Agency for Coastal Protection and Planning — Agence de Protection et d'Aménagement du Littoral). Governs beach access, coastal setbacks, and seaside urbanization restrictions. Strengthened environmental impact controls on port and tourist developments along coastlines.
EnvironmentalLand

Decree No. 2005-1991 of July 11, 2005Environmental Impact Assessment

Enacted: July 11, 2005 | In force: 2005 | Amended: 2012
Requires Environmental Impact Assessment (EIA) studies for listed major projects (Annex I: mandatory EIA; Annex II: simplified environmental notice). Covered projects include industrial plants, mining operations, tourism facilities, infrastructure, and large-scale agriculture. ANPE reviews EIA dossiers and issues environmental authorization before the building permit. Public consultation required for Annex I projects.
EnvironmentalAdministrative

Law No. 96-29 of June 3, 1996Marine Pollution Prevention and Control

Enacted: June 3, 1996 | In force: 1996
Tunisia's primary statute on marine environmental protection and pollution prevention along its 1,300 km Mediterranean coastline. Prohibits the discharge of hydrocarbons, sewage, waste, and noxious substances into Tunisian territorial waters and the exclusive economic zone. Establishes emergency response obligations for ships and port operators in case of accidental spills. Implements MARPOL 73/78 (the International Convention for the Prevention of Pollution from Ships) obligations in domestic law. Assigns the Merchant Marine Directorate and the National Guard (maritime units) enforcement responsibilities. Criminal sanctions: up to 5 years imprisonment and significant fines for deliberate discharges.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–6: Scope — territorial sea (12 nm), contiguous zone (24 nm), and EEZ (200 nm)
  • Art. 7–15: Prohibition of discharge of hydrocarbons and harmful substances; exceptions
  • Art. 16–22: Ship waste reception facilities in ports; waste manifests
  • Art. 23–28: Emergency response plans (POLMAR plans) for port operators and major vessel traffic
  • Art. 29–35: Aerial and maritime surveillance; inspection of vessels
  • Art. 36–42: Enforcement: confiscation of vessels; criminal sanctions for ship masters
  • Art. 43–48: Coastal industrial discharge limits; ANPE monitoring of industrial outfalls
EnvironmentalTransport

Draft Law on Reducing Single-Use Plastic Bag Consumption (pending)

Filed by ARP deputies, reported: July 5, 2026 | Status: newly submitted, not yet referred to committee
A proposition de loi that would mandate a progressive phase-out of single-use plastic bags in favor of reusable or biodegradable alternatives, and set technical standards to ensure the quality and effectiveness of those substitutes. Cites Tunisia's estimated 4.2 billion single-use plastic bags consumed annually (roughly 400 per resident), generating about 188,000 tonnes of plastic waste, of which some 60% is improperly managed — distinct from, and narrower than, the still-untransmitted "Code de l'Environnement" consolidation project noted in the Framework Environmental Law entry above. Just submitted to the ARP as of this run; not yet referred to committee. (Added Run 109 — a wholly new standalone entry outside the pending-bill watchlist, filed the same day as this run's research cutoff.)
EnvironmentalPending

📰 Press, Media & Freedom of Expression

Decree-Law No. 2011-115 of November 2, 2011Press and Publication Code

Enacted: November 2, 2011 | In force: 2011 | Replaced: Press Code of 1975
Post-revolution statute guaranteeing freedom of the press and abolishing prior government control over media. Abolished the requirement for prior authorization to publish newspapers. Criminalized defamation but with strong protections for press freedom. Created the High Authority for Audio-Visual Communication (HAICA — Haute Autorité Indépendante de la Communication Audiovisuelle).
Press

Decree-Law No. 2011-116 of November 2, 2011HAICA (Audio-Visual Regulator)

Enacted: November 2, 2011 | In force: 2011
Establishes the High Authority for Audio-Visual Communication (HAICA) as an independent regulatory body for radio and television broadcasting. HAICA grants broadcasting licenses, sets content standards, and monitors compliance. Replaced the previous state-controlled broadcasting authority.
Press

Law No. 90-49 of May 7, 1990National Broadcasting Establishment (ERTT)

Enacted: May 7, 1990 | Predecessor: RTT (created by decree, April 25, 1957) | Split into separate radio/TV establishments: 2007
Converted Radio Télévision Tunisienne (RTT) — previously a service administratively and financially dependent on the Ministry of Information — into the Établissement de la Radiodiffusion-Télévision Tunisienne (ERTT), a public establishment with administrative and financial autonomy operating national radio and television. Following a November 2006 presidential announcement, ERTT was split, effective August 31, 2007, into separate Établissement de la Radio Tunisienne and Établissement de la Télévision Tunisienne entities. Governs only the public broadcaster's own corporate/administrative status — sector-wide licensing and content-standard oversight sits with HAICA below (and, if enacted, its proposed replacement). (Run 90 addition)
PressAdministrative

Organic Law No. 2016-22 of March 24, 2016Access to Information

Enacted: March 24, 2016 | In force: 2017 | Enforcement authority dissolved: August 2025
Comprehensive right-to-information law creating an independent National Authority for Access to Information (INAI — Instance Nationale d'Accès à l'Information). Any person may request information from public bodies; response within 20 days required. Narrow exceptions for national security, privacy, and commercial confidentiality. Considered one of the strongest RTI laws in the MENA region when enacted. Status as of 2026: Tunisian authorities dissolved INAI in early August 2025, eliminating the independent body responsible for enforcing access-to-information requests and adjudicating appeals against public-body refusals. ARTICLE19 and the IDEA Global State of Democracy tracker both logged the move as removing the last independent oversight mechanism created during the post-2011 transition; rights groups and Tunisian civil society condemned the dissolution as a further blow to transparency. The underlying statutory right to request information nominally remains in the text of Organic Law No. 2016-22, but without a functioning independent authority to enforce it.
PressAdministrative

Decree-Law No. 2022-54 of September 13, 2022Combating Information Technology Offenses

Enacted: September 13, 2022 | In force: September 2022 | Still in active use as of 2026
Criminalizes "false information" spread online that "harms third parties, public order, or national defense" (Art. 24: up to 5 years imprisonment and TND 50,000 fine, doubled for statements about public officials). Criticized by RSF, CPJ, Amnesty International, Access Now, and ICJ for vague language enabling prosecution of journalists, bloggers, and critics. Passed by presidential decree-law without parliamentary scrutiny. New prosecutions of journalists and online activists were brought in January 2026 — including radio hosts Mourad Zeghidi and Borhen Bsaies, each sentenced to a further 3.5 years on January 22, 2026, partly on related money-laundering charges illustrating a pattern of layered prosecutions — and CPJ reported in May 2026 on additional measures beyond Decree 54 used to pressure the press. RSF's May 2026 World Press Freedom Index ranked Tunisia 137th globally amid what the organization called a 25-year global low for press freedom. Courts have at times narrowed the decree's application: on February 3, 2025, the Cour de Cassation's Criminal Chamber 29 ruled, in the case of lawyer/journalist Sonia Dahmani, that the decree's scope does not extend to opinions expressed by journalists in the written or audiovisual press, limiting it to offenses committed through or using information/communication systems — though lower courts have not consistently followed this precedent, and Dahmani was nonetheless convicted and sentenced to 18 months in a separate Decree 54 case in April 2026. Prosecutions continued into mid-2026: in June 2026, Tumedia director and journalist Khaoula Boukrim was sentenced to four years' imprisonment over Facebook posts critical of public policy, prompting condemnation from the National Union of Tunisian Journalists (SNJT); the Committee for Justice separately reported further cumulative prison sentences against Dahmani in unrelated cases during the same period. As of 2026, no legislative review or repeal has occurred, though the ARP's General Legislation Commission announced in December 2025 that it would examine amendment bills to the decree during 2026, with hearings planned for journalists' and lawyers' unions.
PressCriminalDigital

Decree-Law No. 2022-17 of March 10, 2022HAICA Amendment

Enacted: March 10, 2022 | In force: 2022
Amended HAICA's organic statute under post-July 2021 decree-law powers. Modified the appointment procedure for HAICA members, increasing presidential influence over the regulator. Criticized by media freedom organizations and the Venice Commission for weakening HAICA's independence. HAICA continued operations but faced political pressure over license renewals for channels critical of the government.
PressConstitutional

Draft Organic Law on Audiovisual Communication (pending, filed February 25, 2025)Proposed Replacement of HAICA

Filed: February 25, 2025, by 16 co-sponsoring deputies | Under review: ARP Rights & Freedoms Commission | Status: not yet enacted as of July 1, 2026
A 99-article draft organic law across five chapters — organized around sector principles, the organization and functioning of a new regulatory authority, organization of the audiovisual media sector, offenses and sanctions, and final/transitional provisions — that would repeal Decree-Laws No. 2011-115 and No. 2011-116 and replace HAICA entirely with a new permanent regulatory instance. Sponsors argue the change is needed because HAICA, as constituted, is not a constitutional body under the 2022 Constitution and its members' activity has effectively been frozen. As of May 20, 2026, the ARP Rights and Freedoms Commission was still debating which committee holds proper jurisdiction over the bill — General Legislation, which received the original April 2025 referral, had asked Rights and Freedoms for an advisory opinion — before substantive examination could begin; Commission vice-president Aymen Ben Salah subsequently announced that hearings on the bill were planned to begin in June 2026, though no independent confirmation that those hearings occurred or concluded was found as of this update. Update: at its June 4, 2026 meeting, the ARP Bureau reaffirmed its original April 10, 2025 ruling — the bill (numbered 14/2025 in this source) stays with the Commission de Législation Générale as lead committee, with Rights and Freedoms limited to an advisory opinion — resolving the jurisdictional question in the same direction as before rather than newly, and no confirmation of substantive hearings on the bill's content has surfaced. Media professionals and press-freedom groups have called for a new independent authority but disagree over the bill's specific design. Not yet enacted; HAICA (as amended by Decree-Law No. 2022-17) remains the operative regulator.
PressPending

💻 Digital Law & ICT

Law No. 2004-63 of July 27, 2004 (organic reform bill pending, 2025–2026)Personal Data Protection

Enacted: July 27, 2004 | In force: 2004 | Amended: 2013 | Reform bill: introduced 2025, under ARP Rights & Freedoms Committee review as of 2026
Tunisia's data protection statute — one of the first in Africa. Creates the National Authority for Personal Data Protection (INPDP — Instance Nationale de Protection des Données Personnelles). Requires prior authorization from INPDP before processing sensitive personal data. Establishes data subject rights (access, rectification, deletion) and obligations on data controllers. A comprehensive Fundamental Bill on the Protection of Personal Data — Proposition n°95/2025 per the ARP's own bill tracker (referred to committee July 16, 2025) — is pending before the Assembly of People's Representatives' Rights and Freedoms Committee. The bill comprises 132 articles across six titles (general provisions; processing principles; data-subject rights; processing systems; a new Personal Data Protection Authority replacing INPDP; penalties/transitional measures), per the ARP tracker, Espace Manager, and Nessma, which independently corroborate 132; a "123 articles" figure circulating in some secondary summaries appears to conflate the bill's article count with Article 123 of the ARP's internal rules of procedure (the provision under which the bill was referred to committee) — treat 132 as the correct figure absent a primary JORT text. The bill aligns Tunisian law with the EU GDPR and Council of Europe Convention 108+, addressing biometric data, facial recognition, and algorithmic profiling, and introducing GDPR-style turnover-based administrative fines. Adoption is partly aimed at securing EU "adequacy" status for cross-border data transfers. Committee sessions ran February–May 2026, then June 3 and June 25, 2026 (most recent as of this run); as of the June 3 session the committee was still awaiting written opinions from several ministries and had not yet scheduled civil-society hearings — no committee report or vote as of July 1, 2026. Not yet enacted; the 2004 law remains in force. AI governance note: Tunisia has no dedicated artificial-intelligence statute; the 2026–2030 National Development Plan references completing an executive plan for a national AI strategy (building on an initial 2018 AI strategy) and launching its implementation, but as of mid-2026 this remains a policy-level initiative without an identified decree or law number — AI-specific processing (profiling, biometric/facial recognition) would instead fall under this pending Personal Data Protection bill once enacted.
Key provisions
  • Mandatory registration of personal data processing with INPDP
  • Prior authorization for sensitive data (health, biometric, political, religious)
  • Data subject rights: access, rectification, objection, deletion
  • Restrictions on cross-border data transfers
  • Security obligations for data controllers
  • Criminal sanctions for violations: up to 2 years + fines
Digital

Law No. 99-89 of August 2, 1999 (as amended)Cybercrime Law

Enacted: August 2, 1999 | In force: 1999 | Amended: 2004, 2010
Criminalizes unauthorized access to computer systems, interception of electronic communications, introduction of malicious code, and computer fraud. Establishes penalties of 2 to 5 years imprisonment for basic offenses; up to 8 years for attacks on critical infrastructure. Pre-dates the current Digital Code effort.
DigitalCriminal

Telecommunications Code — Law No. 2001-1 of January 15, 2001

Enacted: January 15, 2001 | In force: 2001 | Amended: 2002, 2008, 2013
Liberalizes the telecommunications sector and establishes the independent regulator (INT — Instance Nationale des Télécommunications). Governs licensing of operators, frequency management, interconnection, universal service obligations, and quality standards. Opened mobile and internet markets to competition. Article 86 — criminalizing "knowingly harming others or disturbing their peace" via public telecommunications networks, up to 2 years' imprisonment — has been repeatedly used against journalists and commentators alongside Decree-Law No. 2022-54 (see Criminal Law section). Update (Run 63): journalist Zied El Heni was sentenced to one year in prison under Article 86 on May 7, 2026 (arrested April 24, 2026 after sharing a social-media post criticizing the treatment of journalist Khalifa Guesmi); the Tunis Court of Appeal confirmed the sentence on June 26, 2026. Reporters Without Borders (RSF), the International Federation of Journalists, and the Committee for Justice condemned the ruling as an abusive use of the telecom-harassment provision to suppress press criticism.
DigitalPress

Postal Code — Law No. 98-38 of June 2, 1998

Enacted: June 2, 1998 | Published: JORT N°44, June 2, 1998 | Amended: Law No. 2007-40
Sets the conditions for exercising postal activity in Tunisia and guarantees public access to basic postal services while protecting correspondence confidentiality. Governs licensing of postal operators — including the state operator, Office National des Postes (La Poste Tunisienne) — prohibited items, and sanctions for postal offenses. Unlike the telecommunications sector's independent regulator (INT), no standalone postal-sector regulatory authority has been identified; oversight remains with the Ministry of Communication Technologies and La Poste Tunisienne itself.
DigitalAdministrative

Draft "Code du Numérique" (Digital Code)Proposed Replacement of the 2001 Telecommunications Code

Ministerial draft publicly discussed 2025–2026 | Not yet submitted to the ARP as of July 2026
A comprehensive ~258-article draft code, prepared under the Ministry of Communication Technologies, intended to entirely replace the Telecommunications Code (Law No. 2001-1 of January 15, 2001). As publicly previewed by the Ministry, the draft would deregulate market entry for mobile virtual network operators (MVNOs), internet service providers, and data-center/cloud operators; impose "dig-once" shared-infrastructure obligations on network buildout; and restrict formal licensing requirements to genuinely scarce resources (radio spectrum, submarine-cable landing rights, satellite orbital slots), removing licensing for activities not dependent on such resources. It is also intended to consolidate cybersecurity, data-hosting, and digital-services rules currently scattered across the ICT Security Law, cybersecurity decree-laws, and sector-specific texts. The existing Cybercrime Law (Law No. 99-89) is expressly described by commentators as pre-dating this effort. As of July 2026 the draft remains a ministerial/preparatory text and has not been formally tabled before the Assembly of People's Representatives; the 2001 Telecommunications Code remains in force. Update (Run 66): within the existing (unamended) 2001 Code, the government nonetheless completed a full 5G rollout: a roadmap was approved and a licensing committee formed on June 13, 2024, licensing conventions were signed with Tunisie Telecom, Ooredoo, and Orange on November 30, 2024, and amending decrees granting each operator 5 MHz (700 MHz band) and 100 MHz (3.5 GHz band) for 15-year terms were published in JORT No. 8 of January 23, 2025; commercial 5G launched February 14, 2025.
Digital

Starlink / Satellite Internet Authorization (pending)

SpaceX request filed: January 2023 | Pilot test: Tunis, Ariana, Gabès (late 2023) | INT feasibility study: March–May 2025 | Status: unauthorized as of mid-2026 (Run 66 addition)
A wholly new standalone entry: Tunisia has neither authorized nor banned SpaceX's Starlink satellite-internet service, leaving a multi-year regulatory process stalled under the existing Telecommunications Code. Following SpaceX's January 2023 authorization request and a three-month pilot in Tunis, Ariana, and Gabès, the national regulator (INT — Instance Nationale des Télécommunications) issued a call for proposals in March 2025 for a feasibility study on satellite very-high-speed internet access, publishing results around May 5–6, 2025 reportedly recommending licensing of at least two LEO satellite operators under 5-year terms. As of early-to-mid 2026, Starlink's own coverage map lists Tunisia as "available from 2026" and takes refundable pre-order deposits, but no commercial license had been granted; the reported sticking point is INT's demand for local ground-station/national IP routing versus SpaceX's standard direct-to-gateway model. The long-pending "Code du Numérique" (see entry above) would eventually restrict licensing specifically to scarce resources including satellite orbital slots, potentially reshaping this process once enacted.
DigitalPending

Law No. 2004-5 of February 3, 2004 & Decree No. 2015-1032ICT Security and National Cybersecurity Agency

Law: February 3, 2004 | ANSI Decree: August 4, 2015
The 2004 ICT Security Law mandates security audits and certification for sensitive IT systems connected to public networks. Decree No. 2015-1032 created the National Cybersecurity Agency of Tunisia (ANSI — Agence Nationale de la Sécurité Informatique), responsible for cybersecurity policy, incident response (CERT-TN), and certification of secure IT systems for public entities and critical infrastructure operators.
DigitalSecurity

BCT Circular No. 2018-02 (January 25, 2018)Cryptocurrency Regulatory Ban

Issued: January 25, 2018 | Status: In force as of June 2026
The Central Bank of Tunisia explicitly banned all cryptocurrency transactions (purchase, holding, trading, mining, exchange) via Circular 2018-02. Violations subject to Exchange Control Law penalties. Despite a 2021 Finance Minister statement favoring decriminalization and a 2022 parliamentary proposal, no legislation has been enacted to legalize cryptocurrencies as of 2026. The BCT maintains a blockchain research sandbox (Digital Dinar experiments) separate from the ban.
DigitalFinancial

BCT Fintech Regulatory Sandbox — Circular No. 2020-03

Established: 2020 | Operative: 2020–present
The Central Bank of Tunisia established a regulatory sandbox for fintech innovations including mobile payments, digital wallets, peer-to-peer lending platforms, and open banking services. Applications reviewed by BCT's Innovation Committee. Sandbox participants may test services for up to 24 months before full licensing requirements apply. Part of BCT's Digital Finance Strategy 2025 targeting financial inclusion and payment modernization.
DigitalFinancial

Law No. 2000-83 of August 9, 2000Electronic Commerce and Digital Signatures

Enacted: August 9, 2000 | In force: 2000 | Amended: 2004
First statute to legally recognize electronic contracts and digital signatures in Tunisia. Establishes the legal equivalence of electronic documents and handwritten signatures. Governs online commercial transactions, electronic payment, and online offers. Establishes the legal framework for certification authorities issuing digital certificates.
