Foundation for Tunisia's e-commerce sector and e-government services.
DigitalCommercial

Proposition de loi n° 42/2024Regulation of Online Commerce & Social Media Marketing (Pending)

Deposited: May 2024, by 88 co-sponsoring deputies | ARP committee sessions through June 2026, examination ongoing | Status: not enacted as of July 1, 2026
A 49-article member's bill, deposited May 2024 by 88 co-sponsoring deputies, under review by the ARP's Committee on Administrative Organization, Development, Digitalization, Governance and Anti-Corruption, aimed at regulating commercial sales and marketing conducted through social media platforms (Facebook, TikTok, Instagram, etc.), a fast-growing informal segment of Tunisia's e-commerce sector. As proposed, it would require online sellers to obtain a "cahier des charges" (specifications file) from the Ministry of Commerce before operating, include a dedicated chapter on consumer-rights and personal-data protection (barring non-disclosure of a seller's identity, phone contacts, licenses, or commercial-registry details), and grant buyers a three-day right of withdrawal (droit de rétractation) to exchange or obtain a refund without justification. The Ministry of Commerce submitted written observations arguing that existing commercial law already partly covers online sales and separately announced its own e-commerce development strategy running through 2030, while the committee weighed whether to replace the mandatory cahier des charges with a simpler electronic declaration system. Correction: examination was not finalized on June 2, 2026 as previously tracked — reporting instead indicates the committee decided to form a dedicated working group at the end of an early-June session, suggesting unresolved points on the cahier-des-charges-vs-declaration debate remained open; the exact date of that session was not clearly confirmed in available sources. Update (Run 39): a June 24, 2026 WebManagerCenter piece characterized the working group's text as having reached a "near-final" shape, but this is a single secondary source rather than a confirmed committee vote or report, so it should be read as directional progress, not resolution of the underlying cahier-des-charges-vs-declaration question. Distinct from, and would supplement, the 2000 Electronic Commerce Law above, which does not address social-media-specific sales. Not yet adopted.
DigitalCommercial

Law No. 2000-57 of June 13, 2000Electronic Signatures and Certification Authorities

Enacted: June 13, 2000 | In force: 2000 | Complemented by: Decree No. 2000-2333
Establishes the legal framework for electronic signature services in Tunisia, predating the more general e-commerce law. Creates the Agence Nationale de Certification Électronique (ANCE — now part of the ANSI framework) as the root certification authority. Defines qualified electronic signatures, simple electronic signatures, and their respective legal values. Authorizes accredited certification service providers (PSC — prestataires de services de certification) to issue digital certificates. The implementing decree sets technical standards (PKI architecture, key lengths, certificate validity periods).
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–4: Definitions of electronic signature; equivalence with handwritten signature when qualified
  • Art. 5–9: Requirements for qualified electronic signatures (linked to signatory, capable of identifying signatory, created by controlled means)
  • Art. 10–14: Obligations of certification service providers; liability for certificate errors
  • Art. 15–18: Accreditation of PSCs by ANCE; ongoing audit requirements
  • Art. 19–22: Certificate suspension and revocation procedures
  • Art. 23–27: Penal sanctions for fraudulent use of certificates
DigitalCommercial

Decree-Law No. 2023-17 of March 11, 2023National Cybersecurity Framework & Cloud Computing Regulation

Enacted: March 11, 2023 | In force: March 2023 | Implementing decrees: 2024
Landmark post-2022 cybersecurity decree establishing a comprehensive national framework for cloud computing services and digital infrastructure security. As part of this decree, the National Agency for Computer Security (ANSI, created by Law No. 2004-5) was renamed the National Cybersecurity Agency (ANCS — Agence Nationale de la Cybersécurité). Mandates data localization requirements for "sensitive" public sector data (health, judicial, financial records must be stored on servers in Tunisia or in countries with adequate protection levels). Creates a licensing regime for cloud service providers operating for Tunisian public entities, including "secure" software/equipment labels and G-cloud/N-cloud labels for cloud providers. Strengthens the mandatory cybersecurity audit regime for critical infrastructure operators (energy, water, finance, telecom). Assigns ANCS enhanced enforcement powers including the ability to issue binding orders to address critical vulnerabilities.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–6: Definitions — cloud computing, critical information infrastructure, sensitive data
  • Art. 7–14: Data localization obligations for public entities; list of sensitive data categories
  • Art. 15–22: Licensing of cloud service providers (IaaS, PaaS, SaaS) for public sector use
  • Art. 23–30: Mandatory security audits for critical infrastructure operators (minimum annual frequency)
  • Art. 31–38: ANSI enforcement powers; right to issue binding security directives
  • Art. 39–45: Incident reporting obligations (critical incidents: 4-hour notification to ANSI/CERT-TN)
  • Art. 46–52: Sanctions: up to 500,000 TND administrative fines + criminal penalties
DigitalSecurity

Organic Law No. 2024-22 of March 11, 2024Biometric National Identity Card

Adopted by ARP: March 6, 2024 (107–3–4) | Published: JORT, March 11, 2024 | Amends: Law No. 93-27 of March 22, 1993
Modifies and supplements the 1993 national identity card law to make a biometric, chip-based national ID card mandatory for citizens from age 15 (permitted with parental authorization from age 12). The chip embeds fingerprints, photograph, and encrypted personal data with access-logging safeguards; the reform also removes the profession field, adds a sex/gender data field, and eliminates the card's prior fixed expiration date. A companion statute of the same date, Organic Law No. 2024-23, separately establishes the biometric passport. Distinct from "e-Houwiya" (Mobile ID), an administrative digital-identity service launched by the Ministry of Communication Technologies in August 2022 (via Circulaire n°16 of August 3, 2022) that complements, rather than replaces, the physical biometric card created by this law.
DigitalAdministrative

Organic Law No. 2024-9 of February 6, 2024Accession to the Budapest Convention on Cybercrime

Enacted: February 6, 2024 | Instrument deposited: March 8, 2024
Approves Tunisia's accession to the Council of Europe Convention on Cybercrime (Budapest Convention, 2001), making Tunisia the 70th state party. Enables international cooperation on cybercrime investigation and prosecution, cross-border evidence-sharing, and extradition mechanisms, complementing the domestic cybercrime framework under Decree-Law No. 2022-54.
DigitalCriminal

🏥 Health & Pharmaceutical Law

Public Health Code — Law No. 91-63 of July 29, 1991

Enacted: July 29, 1991 | In force: 1992 | Amended: 1994, 2002, 2008, 2019
Framework law for public health in Tunisia. Covers organization of the health system, health facilities (public and private), medical professions, pharmaceutical activities, and preventive health. Establishes obligations for health establishments and practitioners. Governs mandatory vaccination programs and epidemic disease control.
Health

Pharmacy Law — Law No. 85-91 of September 11, 1985

Enacted: September 11, 1985 | In force: 1986 | Amended: 1992, 2004
Governs pharmaceutical activities including manufacturing, import, distribution, and dispensing of medicines. Requires prior authorization (visa d'exploitation) for drug marketing. Regulates pharmacy ownership (restricted to licensed pharmacists). Establishes the National Drug Authority (ANCSEP — Agence Nationale de Contrôle Sanitaire et Environnemental des Produits).
Health

Mental Health Law — Law No. 92-83 of August 3, 1992

Enacted: August 3, 1992 | In force: 1992
Governs mental health care and protection of psychiatric patients' rights. Regulates voluntary and involuntary hospitalization, patient consent, and judicial oversight of psychiatric confinement. Establishes conditions for involuntary placement and patients' right to legal representation.
Health

Organic Law No. 2024-32 of June 19, 2024Patient Rights and Medical Responsibility

Adopted by ARP: June 5, 2024 (108 for) | Enacted: June 19, 2024 | Published: JORT, June 20, 2024
Guarantees patients' rights — including access to emergency care at any public or private facility, information about medical and therapeutic interventions, pain management, and protection of personal health data — and establishes a medical-error compensation framework allowing victims to obtain compensation once damage is established, without waiting for a full criminal investigation. Mandates that healthcare professionals be covered by a compensation fund financed by professional contributions, covering bodily, moral, professional, and aesthetic damage, lost income, and related costs. Originated as a 2019 bill; took five years to pass.
HealthAdministrative

Law No. 91-21 of March 13, 1991 (as amended)Medical Professions Organization

Enacted: March 13, 1991 | In force: 1991 | Amended: 2004
Governs the practice of medicine and regulates the professional orders: Ordre des Médecins, Ordre des Dentistes, and Ordre des Pharmaciens. Sets qualification requirements, ethical rules, and disciplinary procedures. Requires all physicians to register with the Ordre before practicing. Regulates private medical practice licensing, fees, and professional conduct. The 2024 parliament proposed a "brain drain" compensation law requiring doctors trained publicly to pay back if emigrating within 5 years.
HealthAdministrative

Draft Organic Law No. 24/2025General Framework/Status of the Health Sector

Filed: 2025, supported by 105 deputies | ARP Health Commission hearing: May 5, 2026
A deputy-initiated organic bill (96 articles across 4 titles) that would establish a general statute for health-sector personnel — covering staff assigned to the Ministry of Health's central administration, regional health directorates, and public health establishments — aiming to address structural staffing obstacles and guarantee continuity of care, particularly in Tunisia's interior regions. The ARP's Health, Women's Affairs, Family, Social Affairs, and Persons with Disabilities Commission heard the bill's authors on May 5, 2026. Distinct from the long-standing 1991 Public Health Code (facility/practice regulation) and the 2024 Patient Rights Law; not yet enacted as of July 2026.
HealthDraft

Law No. 2004-71 of August 2, 2004Compulsory Health Insurance (CNAM)

Enacted: August 2, 2004 | In force: 2007 (phased) | Amended: 2014
Creates the National Compulsory Health Insurance Fund (CNAM — Caisse Nationale d'Assurance Maladie), unifying previously fragmented health coverage. Covers all employees and pensioners in public and private sectors. Three coverage regimes: (1) public healthcare network (filière publique); (2) personal physician system (médecin de famille); (3) direct specialist access with reimbursement. Employee contribution: 2.75% of wages; employer contribution: 4% of wages. 2026 liquidity crisis: CNAM entered 2026 with a theoretical accounting surplus of roughly TND 900 million but an acute cash-liquidity shortfall preventing timely payment of pharmacists, physicians, and other private providers. Following talks convened under the Presidency of the Republic, a January 2026 tripartite agreement between the state, CNAM, and pharmacists' representatives guaranteed payment within fixed deadlines and preserved the third-party payer (tiers payant) system. The liquidity crisis persisted into mid-2026: CNAM's accumulated debt to public hospitals was reported at approximately TND 1.95 billion for 2025, contributing to medication shortages and delayed equipment maintenance; a fresh "engagement de paiement" (payment commitment) between CNAM and private pharmacies was reported reached July 1, 2026 to ensure continued medication dispensing, which pharmacists' representatives explicitly described as a provisional stopgap while again demanding durable financing reform. Verification note (updated, Run 30): the July 1, 2026 standoff is now resolved and independently confirmed — Business News (July 1, 2026) reports CNAM reached a payment commitment ("engagement de paiement") with private pharmacies that averted the threatened suspension of tiers-payant prescription processing, closing the unconfirmed hedge carried across Runs 26–29. Pharmacists' representatives nonetheless described the deal as a provisional stopgap pending durable financing reform, consistent with the underlying June 24–30, 2026 standoff (SPOT's threatened suspension over ~TND 80 million in unpaid 2025 claims; the pharmacy "crisis cell" formed June 30) previously documented here. Medical-analysis labs were separately reported to be considering a similar suspension from July 13, 2026. Update (Run 31): at an extraordinary general assembly on June 30, 2026, the private biologists' union voted to proceed with suspending "tiers payant" (third-party payment) with CNAM effective July 13, 2026, over more than seven months of unpaid claims; routine cases for patients registered under the family-doctor (médecin de famille) scheme would remain covered. The suspension had not yet taken effect as of this run and should be watched for confirmation or a last-minute agreement (as occurred with pharmacists on July 1). Update (Run 43): independent reporting confirms the July 1 pharmacist agreement was a narrow, provisional fix rather than a sign of broader resolution — the Ordre des Pharmaciens formed its own separate "cellule de crise" on June 30, 2026 over the continued risk of a renewed rupture with CNAM, indicating the underlying liquidity crisis extends sector-wide (pharmacists and biologists alike) rather than being isolated to the biologists' dispute; as of July 2, 2026 the biologists' July 13 suspension date stood unchanged with no reported negotiated fix. Update (Run 46): the Tunisian Union of Private Biologists confirmed July 1, 2026 that the tiers-payant suspension will proceed as planned on July 13, 2026, covering all analysis categories (ordinary and chronic-illness/APCI forms), citing over seven months of unpaid CNAM claims; patients registered under the family-doctor (médecin de famille) scheme will still receive analyses but must pay upfront and seek reimbursement afterward, while other insured categories lose direct billing entirely at participating private labs. No last-minute agreement had been reported as of this run — unlike the pharmacists' July 1 fix, this dispute reached its deadline unresolved. The Finance Law 2026 includes measures to diversify CNAM's funding sources and revise the social funds' organizational structure, but as of this run a dedicated CNAM structural-reform bill (splitting social-service management from health-insurance management) had still not been filed with the ARP — reporting as of late 2025 described it as "in elaboration" for an "early 2026" submission that has not materialized — so the reform remains at the policy-agreement stage, not yet codified in a standalone statute. Update (Run 69): SPOT (Syndicat des Pharmaciens d'Officine de Tunisie) confirmed July 2, 2026 that the tiers-payant system continues uninterrupted at CNAM-conventioned pharmacies — the pharmacists' track did not follow the biologists' onto a suspension path. At a June 3, 2026 national-council meeting SPOT had already flagged that "most pharmacies face critical financial situations" and could not tolerate further settlement delays; CNAM for its part acknowledged difficulties in honoring prior accords but committed to a "clear agreement for the coming six months" to stabilize the CNAM–pharmacy relationship, with a further meeting between SPOT and CNAM scheduled for mid-July 2026 to be chaired by the Minister of Social Affairs. This is a distinct, still-open track from the private biologists' union's confirmed July 13, 2026 tiers-payant suspension (see the Run 31/46 updates above), which stood unchanged as of this run.
Key provisions
  • Universal coverage for salaried workers and pensioners
  • Three coverage tracks: public network, family doctor, direct specialist access
  • Reimbursement rates: 70–80% of tariff for most services
  • Catastrophic illness: 100% coverage for chronic and severe conditions
  • Low-income beneficiaries covered by AMGP (gratuitous medical assistance)
  • Pharmaceuticals: reimbursed from national essential drugs list
HealthSocial

Law No. 91-22 of March 13, 1991 (amended by Law No. 99-92 of October 25, 1999)Organ Transplantation

Enacted: 1991 | Opt-out amendment: October 25, 1999
Governs organ and tissue transplantation in Tunisia. The 1999 amendment introduced the opt-out (presumed consent) system — all Tunisian citizens are deemed organ donors at death unless they have explicitly registered an objection with the National Transplant Registry. Permits live donation between relatives (kidneys, partial liver). Cadaveric organ procurement coordinated by CNPTO. Prohibits organ trafficking (criminalized under Penal Code); prohibits commercial kidney transplants.
Health

Law No. 91-72 of August 5, 1991 (as amended)Blood Transfusion and Blood Products

Enacted: August 5, 1991 | In force: 1992 | Amended: 2003, 2015
Governs the collection, processing, quality control, storage, and distribution of blood and blood products in Tunisia. Establishes the National Center for Blood Transfusion (CNTS — Centre National de Transfusion Sanguine) as the regulatory and coordinating authority. Mandates voluntary, non-remunerated blood donation; prohibits commercial blood sale. Requires testing of all donated blood for HIV, hepatitis B, hepatitis C, syphilis, and malaria (in endemic periods). Blood banks (banques de sang) in hospitals operate under CNTS quality standards.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–6: Voluntary, gratuitous, and anonymous donation as ethical principles
  • Art. 7–14: CNTS mandate; regional blood collection centers
  • Art. 15–20: Mandatory serological screening tests for all donations
  • Art. 21–26: Hospital blood bank licensing; traceability obligations
  • Art. 27–32: Prohibition of commercial blood trade; criminal sanctions
  • 2015 amendment: plasma fractionation framework for locally produced clotting factors
Health

Law No. 98-17 of February 23, 1998Prevention of the Harmful Effects of Smoking

Enacted: February 23, 1998 | Implementing decrees: 98-2248 (Nov. 16, 1998), 2009-2611 (Sept. 14, 2009)
Tunisia's principal tobacco-control statute — forbids smoking in enclosed places of collective use (schools, hospitals, government offices, public transport), prohibits sale of tobacco products to minors under 18, and bans tobacco advertising and sponsorship across audiovisual and print media. Decree No. 98-2248 (as supplemented by Decree No. 2009-2611) specifies the covered "places of collective use" and signage requirements. (Run 90 addition) A ministry-drafted amendment bill to extend the framework to e-cigarettes and other new nicotine products, aimed particularly at protecting minors, was reported under preparation in June 2026 — not yet filed with the ARP as of this run.
Health

Law No. 2015-31 of August 17, 2015Bioethics and Assisted Reproductive Technology

Enacted: August 17, 2015 | In force: January 2016
Tunisia's framework statute on bioethics — one of the most progressive in the Arab world at the time. Regulates medically-assisted reproduction (MAR/ART): in-vitro fertilization (IVF), intra-uterine insemination (IUI), and embryo freezing are permitted for married couples only. Prohibits surrogacy, egg donation to third parties, and post-mortem sperm use. Establishes the National Bioethics Committee (Comité National d'Éthique Médicale — CNEM) as advisory body. Regulates research on human embryos, embryonic stem cells (severely restricted), and pre-implantation genetic diagnosis (PGD permitted for certain hereditary diseases with CNEM authorization).
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–8: Scope and bioethical principles (non-maleficence, dignity, informed consent)
  • Art. 9–20: Permitted ART procedures for infertile married couples; authorized centers
  • Art. 21–28: Prohibition of surrogacy, gamete commerce, and posthumous reproduction
  • Art. 29–36: Embryo research — maximum 14-day rule; prohibition of reproductive cloning
  • Art. 37–42: Pre-implantation genetic diagnosis (PGD) conditions and limits
  • Art. 43–48: CNEM composition and advisory mandate
  • Art. 49–55: Criminal sanctions for violations (up to 5 years + fines)
Health

🎓 Education Law

Education Framework Law — Law No. 2002-80 of July 23, 2002

Enacted: July 23, 2002 | In force: 2002 | Replaced: Law No. 91-65
Framework law organizing the Tunisian national education system. Establishes compulsory schooling for 6–16 year olds. Organizes the three-stage system: basic education (9 years), secondary education (4 years), and higher education. Defines national curriculum objectives, teacher qualifications, and school governance.
Education

Higher Education Law — Law No. 2008-19 of February 25, 2008

Enacted: February 25, 2008 | In force: 2008
Governs universities, higher institutes, and research establishments. Introduces the LMD (Licence–Master–Doctorat) system aligned with the Bologna Process. Regulates accreditation, quality assurance, student rights, and university governance. Establishes the framework for private higher education institutions. Pending reform: a bill filed by 27 deputies to amend Article 15 would replace the current system of elected university presidents with ministry-appointed presidents (capped at two terms); under live parliamentary debate as of February 2026 and opposed by the Federal General Union of Higher Education and Scientific Research (FGESRS). Not yet enacted.
EducationPending

Vocational Training Law — Law No. 93-10 of February 17, 1993 (as amended)

Enacted: February 17, 1993 | In force: 1993 | Amended: 2008
Framework law governing vocational and professional training. Creates the National Vocational Training Agency (ATFP — Agence Tunisienne de la Formation Professionnelle). Establishes the national apprenticeship system, certification standards (CAP, BTP, BTS), and quality assurance for vocational qualifications. Employers must contribute 1% of gross payroll to the Vocational Training Fund (TFP). Covers both initial training for youth and continuing training for employed workers.
EducationLabor

Law No. 96-6 of February 1, 1996 (amended 2010, 2018)Scientific Research and Technology

Enacted: February 1, 1996 | In force: 1996 | Amended: 2010, 2018
Framework law for scientific research and technological development in Tunisia. Creates the National Research Council (CNR) and sector-based research centers. Establishes the National Research Fund (FNR) for competitive grants. Sets researcher status in public research institutions. The 2018 amendment introduced valorization mechanisms: technology transfer offices, patent filing support for public researchers, and spin-off company frameworks linked to the Startup Act (2018).
Education

🤝 Social Security & Protection

CNSS Organic Law — Law No. 60-30 of December 14, 1960 (as amended)

Enacted: 1960 | Extensively amended through 2024
Establishes the National Social Security Fund (Caisse Nationale de Sécurité Sociale — CNSS) for private sector workers. Covers contributory retirement pensions, invalidity, death benefits, occupational hazards, family allowances, and maternity. Employee contribution: 9.18%; Employer contribution: 16.57% of gross wages. A possible CNSS–CNRPS merger into a unified pension fund and a new "Identifiant Social Unique" were repeatedly discussed at ministerial-council level in early 2026 but remained at the study stage, with no bill text. Only a 2024 decree-law amnesty on late-payment penalties (Decree-Law No. 2024-503 of October 24, 2024, extended by Decree No. 2025-259 of May 22, 2025) and routine Finance Law contribution/benefit adjustments have actually been enacted. Two further bills remained under ARP review as of early 2026, neither yet adopted: Proposition de loi No. 16/2026 (filed February 21, 2026), which would let public-sector employees request a one-year (extendable to three, capped at age 70) deferral of retirement by amending Law No. 85-12 on civil/military pensions; and Projet de loi No. 104/2025 (filed January 29, 2026), which would ease early-retirement conditions for private-sector women covered by CNSS by removing the "mother of three children" requirement and setting eligibility at age 50 with at least 80 quarters of contributions. (Cross-ref, Run 115) A third, distinct retirees' bill (Proposition de loi n° 30/2026) targeting the cumul-emploi-retraite ban and retiree tariff benefits is tracked as its own standalone entry below.
SocialLabor

Decree-Law No. 2024-4 of October 22, 2024Social Protection for Female Agricultural Workers

Enacted: October 22, 2024 | Published: JORT, October 2024
Establishes a dedicated social protection regime for female agricultural workers (ouvrières agricoles), a category long excluded from effective social security coverage. Covers health insurance, old-age and disability pensions, workplace accident insurance, and maternity benefits, and includes provisions on economic inclusion and transport. Comprises 52 articles across 4 chapters; financed in part through the AMEN Social framework and subsequent Finance Laws.
SocialLabor

Social Assistance Program Law — Law No. 2019-49 of June 6, 2019AMEN Social

Enacted: June 6, 2019 | In force: 2020
Creates Tunisia's unified social protection system "AMEN Social" replacing fragmented assistance programs. Provides means-tested direct cash transfers to poor families (National Aid Program — PAN), health coverage for the poor (AMGP), and subsidized access to services. Governed by the Ministry of Social Affairs.
Social

Disability Law — Law No. 2005-83 of August 15, 2005

Enacted: August 15, 2005 | In force: 2005
Framework law for the rights and inclusion of persons with disabilities. Prohibits discrimination in employment, education, and public services. Requires accessibility in public buildings and transportation. Originally established a disability employment quota of 1% in the public sector. Creates the National Bureau of Disabled Persons (ONHA). Amended by Law No. 2016-41 of May 16, 2016: raised the quota to 2% and extended it beyond the public sector — public and private enterprises with 100+ employees must reserve 2% of positions for persons with disabilities, and enterprises with 50–99 employees must reserve at least one position, filled in a statutory priority order (Art. 30), with alternative compliance measures available where direct hiring is not feasible (Art. 31). Finance Law 2026 (Art. 32): created a dedicated Fund for the Promotion of Persons with Disabilities, a special treasury fund managed by the CNSS, financed by a 1% levy on compensation paid out for road accidents, workplace accidents, and occupational diseases (withheld by insurers, mutual funds, the Road Accident Victims Guarantee Fund, CNSS, CNRPS, and the Deposits and Consignments Fund), applicable to payouts made from January 1, 2026 onward regardless of when the underlying accident or judgment occurred; proceeds fund economic, social, cultural, and sports inclusion programs plus training, employment, and entrepreneurship support.
Social

Law No. 2002-32 of April 8, 2002 and subsequent amendmentsPublic Pension Reform (CNRPS)

Enacted: April 8, 2002 | Amendments: 2007, 2016
Reformed the public sector pension fund (CNRPS — Caisse Nationale de Retraite et de Prévoyance Sociale). Raised minimum civil service retirement age from 55 to 60. Increased contribution rates progressively. Introduced voluntary supplementary pension savings vehicles. Long-term pension system sustainability remains a fiscal challenge; further reform discussions ongoing as of 2025 amid IMF program conditionalities.
SocialLabor

Presidential Decree No. 2020-229 of May 4, 2020COVID-19 National Solidarity Fund

Enacted: May 4, 2020 | Emergency duration: 2020–2021
Established the National COVID-19 Solidarity Fund funded by state appropriations and private/diaspora donations. Provided emergency income support (TND 200/month) to informal workers, artisans, and daily wage earners who lost income during lockdowns. TND 200 million initially allocated; supplemented by IMF Rapid Financing Instrument loan (April 2020). Governed by Ministry of Social Affairs through municipal social registers.
SocialAdministrative

Law No. 2020-19 of April 12, 2020COVID-19 Enabling Act & Related Decree-Laws (Run 44 addition)

Enabling law: April 12, 2020 | Lockdown decree: March 22, 2020 | Lockdown lifted: July 3, 2020
Sits alongside the Solidarity Fund decree above as the rest of Tunisia's pandemic legal architecture, previously undocumented here. Law No. 2020-19 authorized the Head of Government, under Art. 70 of the 2014 Constitution, to issue decree-laws for 2 months to address COVID-19, each subject to later ARP ratification. Under this authority: Decree-Law No. 2020-2 (April 14, 2020) exceptionally suspended Labor Code provisions on dismissals/layoffs for force majeure; Decree-Law No. 2020-8 (April 17, 2020) suspended administrative/judicial procedural deadlines; Decree-Law No. 2020-30 (June 10, 2020) provided further economic/social relief including a pension top-up for low-income CNSS/CNRPS beneficiaries. Separately, Governmental Decree No. 2020-156 (March 22, 2020) imposed total lockdown/confinement, lifted by Governmental Decree No. 2020-411 (July 3, 2020); Presidential Decrees No. 2020-38 and 2020-54 (April 28 and May 29, 2020) invoked Tunisia's standing general state-of-emergency regime (Decree No. 78-50 of 1978) over the same period. Whether the ARP ever completed formal ratification of these decree-laws, and whether Decree-Law 2020-30's relief measures were formally repealed versus simply lapsing, could not be confirmed from available sources.
SocialLaborAdministrative

Law No. 1994-2 of January 3, 1994Elderly Persons — Rights and Protection

Enacted: January 3, 1994 | In force: 1994 | Amended: 2004
Tunisia's framework statute on the protection and rights of elderly persons (aged 60+). Recognizes the family's primary responsibility for elderly care while establishing state obligations for support. Creates licensed residential care facilities for the elderly (maisons de retraite) under Ministry of Social Affairs oversight. Establishes minimum quality standards for care facilities. Provides for home-care assistance programs for elderly persons without family support. Prohibits abandonment of elderly persons; criminalizes neglect by legal guardians or responsible family members. The 2004 amendment strengthened quality control inspections of private care facilities.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–6: Definition of elderly person; recognition of family as primary caregiver
  • Art. 7–14: State obligation to provide homes for elderly without family support
  • Art. 15–20: Licensing requirements for public and private elderly care facilities
  • Art. 21–26: Minimum standards: hygiene, nutrition, medical care, social activity
  • Art. 27–32: Prohibition of abandonment; criminal sanctions (up to 2 years)
  • Art. 33–38: Home-care assistance programs financed by CNSS/public funds
Social

Decree-Law No. 2011-97 of October 24, 2011Martyrs and Wounded of the Revolution

Enacted: October 24, 2011 | In force: 2011 | Amended: Law No. 2012-26 of December 24, 2012
Establishes benefits, pensions, and priority employment/healthcare rights for the families of persons killed and for those wounded during the 2010–2011 revolution. The 2012 amendment broadened eligibility criteria and revised the composition of the commission responsible for determining beneficiary status. Distinct from the later Transitional Justice Law (Organic Law No. 2013-53, see Judicial Organization section), which addresses truth-seeking and accountability for a much longer historical period rather than direct revolution-era victim benefits.
SocialAdministrative

Proposition de loi n° 30/2026 (pending)Retirees' Socio-Economic Benefits & End of the Cumul Emploi-Retraite Ban (Added Run 115)

Filed by ~40 ARP deputies, early 2026 | Under review: ARP Health, Women's Affairs, Family, Social Affairs & Disabled Persons Commission (examination sessions confirmed May 8, 20, and 25, 2026) | Status: not yet enacted as of July 2026
A deputy-initiated bill responding to Tunisia's rapidly aging population (aging rate risen from 6% to roughly 16.88% in 2024, projected to reach near 20% by 2030, per Commission for Development & Strategic Planning president Sabeur Jelassi). Its centerpiece, debated at length under Article 5 of the draft, would end the long-standing prohibition on combining a retirement pension with income from another professional activity ("cumul emploi-retraite"), with deputies divided at the May 20, 2026 session over whether existing law already permits this or whether a full repeal is required, and some pressing to extend the bill's benefits to all elderly citizens rather than pensioners alone; a further incentive would give employers a 30% social-contribution exemption for hiring retired experts as consultants, mentors, or trainers. A second strand grants tariff reductions — reported inconsistently across otherwise-corroborating La Presse de Tunisie coverage as either a 30% or 40% minimum reduction on urban/regional/rail public transport (plus national airline and maritime travel outside peak periods), and either 40% or a "40–50%" discount at classified hotels and tourist establishments and on festival tickets, alongside free museum and historical-monument entry — an unresolved figure discrepancy this project flags rather than resolves without a primary bill text. Also proposes priority hospital pathways (consultations, exams, surgery) and possible exemptions from certain prevention-related contributions. Benefits would be administered via a "Carte loisirs du retraité" (Retiree Leisure Card) with terms set by decree. Distinct from the CNSS entry's two already-tracked pension bills (Proposition de loi No. 16/2026, retirement-deferral for public-sector employees, and Projet de loi No. 104/2025, easing CNSS early-retirement conditions for women) and from the 1994 Elderly Persons Law's care-facility framework (see standalone entries) — this bill targets retirees' active-life benefits and income rights rather than pension mechanics or residential care. A related but separately-numbered sign-language/hearing-impairment bill was examined by the same commission on the same May 20, 2026 date, broadening protections for persons with total or partial speech loss and mandating a unified national sign language for education, public services, and public spaces; it remains too lightly sourced (no bill number or article count located) to warrant its own standalone entry as of this run.
SocialLaborPending

🏠 Land & Property Law

Real Property Registration — Law No. 65-5 of February 12, 1965 (as reformed)

Origin: 1885 (protectorate) | Major overhaul: Law No. 65-5 of February 12, 1965 | Amended: 1992, 2005
Governs land title registration (immatriculation foncière) through the Property Rights Registry (Conservation de la Propriété Foncière). Once registered, title is conclusive and indefeasible. Unregistered land may be subject to adverse possession claims. Tunisia operates a Torrens-style title system for registered properties.
LandCivil

Urban Planning Code — Law No. 94-122 of November 28, 1994

Enacted: November 28, 1994 | In force: 1995 | Amended: 2003, 2009, 2016
Governs land use planning, building permits, zoning, and urban development in Tunisia. Requires detailed urban plans (PAD — Plans d'Aménagement de Détail) for urban areas. Establishes building permit requirements, setback rules, and height restrictions. Regulates real estate development and subdivision approvals. Pending reform (Run 45): the ARP's Strategic Planning/Urban Planning Committee approved, by majority vote and in amended form, a bill reactivating and updating the separate construction-regularization statute (Law No. 2015-11) to let owners of non-compliant buildings regularize their status and obtain utility-hookup authorizations; reported June 23, 2026, the bill was headed toward a committee report ahead of a plenary vote, though its ARP registry number could not be independently confirmed and is omitted pending a primary-source citation. Detail added (Run 49): per committee-hearing coverage from earlier in its progress (the bill was first heard by the same committee on February 27, 2026, before its June 23 amended-form approval), the proposal comprises 12 articles setting out eligible categories, land-registration valuation, and the procedures for benefiting from regularization; it targets housing built without authorization or in violation of planning rules before a December 31, 2025 cutoff, with sponsors citing estimates of roughly 800,000 to one million residents across some 1,400 informal ("anarchic") neighborhoods nationwide as potential beneficiaries. Post-cutoff violations would face demolition or a fine of up to three times the land's value. These figures come from pre-committee-vote reporting (businessnews.com.tn, La Presse, African Manager) on what appears to be the same proposal tracked since Run 45 rather than a primary ARP/JORT text, and the registry number remains unconfirmed — treat the article count and beneficiary estimates as provisional until a post-vote committee report or JORT text is located. Re-confirmed (Run 96): a dedicated recheck of French- and English-language sources found no evidence that a plenary vote has occurred as of July 5, 2026 — La Presse de Tunisie, allAfrica, and African Manager's June 23, 2026 coverage all state the amended text must still proceed through a full committee report before reaching the floor, and no ARP agenda, vote outcome, or report-finalization news has surfaced since. The bill remains at the pre-report, pre-plenary stage; this is the closest watchlist item to crossing a legislative threshold and warrants rechecking again in the next run. Update (Run 83): distinct from the narrower construction-regularization bill above, a separate, broader wholesale revision of this 1994 Code itself has been underway for years — former Minister of Equipment and Housing Sarra Zaafrani Zenzri targeted completion by 2024 (announced June 20, 2023, developed via ministry workshops with UN-Habitat Tunisia, addressing climate-change resilience, energy transition, and digital-transformation planning tools), a deadline that lapsed unmet. Her successor, Minister Salah Zouari, announced in October 2025 that promulgation is now expected in 2026 (per urbanisme.fr, April 21, 2026), aiming to modernize planning tools for informal urban areas, improve state/local-authority governance coordination, and enable locally-adapted planning mechanisms — but political instability since 2021 has limited ARP debate time, creating the same institutional bottleneck reported for the Investment, Water, Environment, and Hydrocarbons codes elsewhere in this document. No draft text has been filed with the ARP as of this run.
LandAdministrative

Housing Law — Law No. 77-46 of May 30, 1977

Enacted: May 30, 1977 | In force: 1977 | Amended: 1996, 2013
Governs residential leases and tenant-landlord relations. Sets minimum lease terms, notice periods for termination, and tenant protection against arbitrary eviction. Regulates rent increases for controlled accommodation. Applies to residential leases; commercial leases are regulated separately under the COC.
LandCivil

Employee Housing Promotion Fund (FOPROLOS) — Law No. 77-54 of August 3, 1977

Enacted: August 3, 1977 | In force: 1977 | Status: in force, ongoing parafiscal levy
Creates the Fonds de Promotion du Logement pour les Salariés (FOPROLOS), financed principally by a mandatory 1% employer payroll levy on private-sector wages (collected alongside CNSS social-security contributions), dedicated to subsidized housing for salaried employees. FOPROLOS funds low-interest mortgage loans, employer/cooperative-built social housing programs, and housing-cooperative site-and-services schemes for lower- and middle-income CNSS-affiliated workers, jointly administered by the Ministry of Equipment, Housing & Territorial Planning and CNSS. Distinct from the rent-regulation framework of the Housing Law (No. 77-46, above), which governs private residential leases rather than subsidized-housing financing. (Added Run 82 — a genuine standalone gap: 81 prior runs had documented the general Housing Law but never this separate employer-levy financing statute.)
LandLaborSocial

Co-ownership Law — Law No. 77-42 of May 23, 1977

Enacted: May 23, 1977 | In force: 1977 | Amended: 1994
Governs horizontal co-ownership (apartment buildings and condominiums). Defines common areas vs. private lots, establishes the mandatory Syndicate of Co-owners (syndicat des copropriétaires), and regulates management, maintenance costs, and dispute resolution between co-owners.
LandCivil

Habous (Religious Endowments) — Decree of July 18, 1956 (as reformed)

Reform Decree: July 18, 1956 | Multiple administrative reforms since
Habous (Arabic: وقف — waqf) are religious endowments inalienably dedicated to Islamic charitable purposes. The 1956 decree reorganized habous administration under the Ministry of Religious Affairs. Public habous were progressively converted to state property for national development projects. Private habous recognized but creation of new ones restricted. Habous property cannot be sold, mortgaged, or partitioned. A historically significant category of Tunisian real property, particularly urban commercial and agricultural land.
LandCivil

Law No. 83-87 of November 11, 1983 and Law No. 2001-29 of March 19, 2001Agricultural Land Protection

Enacted: 1983, 2001 | In force: ongoing
Restricts conversion of agricultural land to non-agricultural uses. Prime agricultural land (irrigated perimeters, fertile coastal plains) protected from subdivision and urbanization without prior Ministry of Agriculture approval. The 2001 law governs management of communal pastoral lands (terres collectives): partitioning, titling, and integration into the formal land registry. Essential to food security policy.
LandAgriculture

Law No. 69-56 of September 22, 1969Restriction on Foreign Ownership of Agricultural Land

Enacted: September 22, 1969 | In force: 1969 | Status: in force, subject to pending reform proposals (2025–2026)
Reserves ownership of agricultural land to persons of Tunisian nationality, agricultural cooperatives, and public legal entities (Art. 1); foreign nationals may not acquire agricultural property, a policy framed around food-security and productive-land-resource protection. Foreigners may lease agricultural land, generally capped at short renewable terms absent special authorization. As of 2025–2026 the rule on outright ownership is unchanged, but it is being debated alongside two related, separately pending legislative proposals on state-owned ("domanial") agricultural land: Proposal No. 32/2025, examined by the ARP Agriculture Committee in February 2026, would regularize and reallocate already-exploited state land — granting title to long-term informal farmers who developed neglected state plots over decades — while a second track examines long-term leasing (rather than ownership transfer) of state land to investors, including foreign investors, without altering the 1969 ownership restriction. Neither proposal had been adopted as of June 2026.
LandAgriculture

Law No. 2016-53 of July 11, 2016Expropriation for Public Utility

Enacted: July 11, 2016 | Amended: Decree-Law No. 2022-65 of October 19, 2022 (JORT No. 114, Oct. 21, 2022) | Further revision under study since Dec. 2024
Governs expropriation of private property for public utility (utilité publique), replacing the older 1976 expropriation code. Sets the procedure for declaring a project of public interest, valuing affected property, and compensating owners, with disputes heard before the Expropriation Court. Decree-Law No. 2022-65 amended the law to create a permanent "commission de reconnaissance et de conciliation" (recognition and conciliation commission) in each governorate to speed up amicable compensation settlements. A December 16, 2024 working session chaired by the Minister of State Domains and Land Affairs, Wajdi Hedhili — held pursuant to Circular No. 27 of the Presidency of Government (November 7, 2024) on accelerating public projects — launched study of a further revision intended to speed up expropriation procedures while safeguarding owners' compensation rights; the revision remained at the project/consultation stage, not yet enacted, as of mid-2026.
LandAdministrative

Law No. 81-55 of June 23, 1981Real Estate Agent Profession

Enacted: June 23, 1981 | Amended: Law No. 2005-77 of August 8, 2005
Organizes the real-estate agent (agent immobilier) profession — brokerage and intermediation in property sales, purchases, and leases. Requires proof of professional competence (diploma or qualifying experience), a financial guarantee, and professional liability insurance for practice. Distinct from the Real Estate Development Law, which governs developers rather than brokers. (Run 90 addition)
LandCommercial

Law No. 74-46 of May 22, 1974Architects Profession Organization

Enacted: May 22, 1974 | Disciplinary code: Decree No. 83-1033 of November 1983
Organizes the architecture profession — no one may use the title "architecte" or practice architecture in Tunisia without being registered in the Table of the Ordre des Architectes de Tunisie (OAT). Sets nationality, diploma, and character requirements for registration (Art. 1) and makes OAT membership mandatory (Art. 2); Decree No. 83-1033 sets out the professional code of duties and disciplinary sanctions (suspension, removal). Parallels the Urban Planning Code's building-permit regime above and the Engineers Profession Law below. (Run 90 addition)
LandAdministrative

🏢 Associations & Civil Society

Decree-Law No. 2011-88 of September 24, 2011Associations and Non-Profit Organizations

Enacted: September 24, 2011 | In force: 2011 | Replaced: Law No. 59-154 (1959)
Post-revolution statute liberalizing civil society formation. Replaced the restrictive 1959 law. Article 12: associations are legally constituted upon submission of a notification letter — no prior government approval required. Prohibits dissolution by executive action without judicial order. Enabled a rapid expansion of civil society: over 20,000 associations registered by 2015. Pending reform, updated status: a draft organic law replacing this decree-law (originally filed October 2023) is under active examination by the ARP Rights & Freedoms Commission — not merely "with the government" as previously reported — with the parliamentary bureau setting a June 24, 2026 deadline for the commission's report; as of early June 2026 the commission was still awaiting requested government agencies' opinions. Commission chair Thabet El Abed described the amended bill's foreign-funding provisions as a "disguised authorization dressed as a declaration," tightening administrative control (via Ministries of Interior and Foreign Affairs oversight and mandatory reporting of foreign transfers to the CTAF) over associational autonomy. At its June 3, 2026 session the commission was reportedly still working from a newly-received, updated version of the bill text requiring further review, had received no official government responses to its consultation letters, and had not yet scheduled civil-society hearings (Al Chourouk, June 4, 2026) — all of which suggests the June 24 report deadline was more likely missed than met, though no independent reporting has surfaced confirming whether the commission actually submitted a report by that date or what it concluded. This outcome remains unverified and should be checked again as later coverage emerges. On May 22, 2026, commission member Fatma Mseddi sent formal correspondence to the commission's president describing the file — by then covering at least twelve hearing sessions plus a dedicated study day — as effectively "blocked" despite having reached what she called an "advanced" stage, and pressed the ARP not to delay it further; this predates, and is consistent with, the June 24 deadline subsequently slipping without a confirmed outcome. Update (Run 34): as of July 1, 2026 the bill remains alive, not abandoned — the Rights & Freedoms Commission is now studying it jointly with the pending nationality-code and personal-data-protection bills (webdo.tn), with the commission publicly calling for "large concertation avec les acteurs concernés" (broad consultation with concerned stakeholders) before any adoption; no report or vote has yet followed the lapsed June 24 deadline. Civil society groups continue to warn the reform would restrict freedoms; not yet enacted as of July 2, 2026.
Key provisions
  • Art. 3: Freedom to form associations guaranteed
  • Art. 10: Founders submit notification (not seek prior approval) to the Secretary General of Government
  • Art. 12: Association acquires legal personality upon notification
  • Art. 14: Registration certificate issued within 7 days
  • Art. 26: Dissolution only by judicial order on defined grounds
  • Art. 31: Foreign associations may operate with prior authorization
AdministrativeConstitutional

Decree-Law No. 2011-87 of September 24, 2011Political Parties

Enacted: September 24, 2011 | In force: 2011 | Amended: 2014
Governs the formation, registration, financing, and activities of political parties. Requires notification-based registration (not prior authorization). Prohibits parties based exclusively on religion, race, gender, or region. Mandates financial transparency and annual reporting. Political party financing regulated: prohibits foreign funding, limits campaign spending.
AdministrativeConstitutional

Organic Law No. 2014-16 of May 26, 2014 (as amended)Electoral Law

Enacted: May 26, 2014 | In force: 2014 | Amended: 2019, 2022, September 27, 2024
Governs elections for the President of the Republic, the Assembly of People's Representatives, and the Assembly of Regions and Districts. Establishes the Independent High Authority for Elections (ISIE) as the permanent electoral commission. Sets rules on candidacy, campaigning, vote counting, and dispute resolution. The 2022 amendments (Decree-Law 2022-22) modified ISIE membership and reduced its independence according to critics. September 27, 2024 amendment: adopted by the ARP just nine days before the October 6, 2024 presidential election, it transferred jurisdiction over electoral disputes — including candidate-eligibility appeals previously heard by the Administrative Court — to the ordinary Court of Appeal of Tunis, with a 48-hour deadline to file contested-result challenges. The change followed Administrative Court rulings reinstating several candidates ISIE had excluded, and was criticized by Human Rights Watch and other observers as weakening judicial checks on election integrity at a sensitive moment.
ConstitutionalAdministrative

Draft Law No. 027/2023 and Government Counter-Draft (2024)Proposed NGO Reform (Pending)

Parliamentary draft introduced: 2023 | Government draft reported: July 2024 | New organic law proposal: April 2026 | Status: Not enacted as of July 2026
Several competing proposals to replace Decree-Law No. 2011-88 on associations. The most advanced, presented to the ARP in April 2026, is a comprehensive organic law proposal of 45 articles across 8 chapters governing the creation, management, financing, oversight, and sanctioning of associations. Its Article 26 would ban all foreign funding of associations without prior written authorization from the Prime Minister's office (Présidence du Gouvernement), with a 2-month response deadline and implicit approval if the deadline lapses unanswered. The ARP held a dedicated study day on the bill in April 2026, and its Rights and Freedoms Committee was tasked with finalizing its report by June 24, 2026. Strongly opposed by Tunisian civil society, Article 19, ICNL, and Amnesty International as reversing the 2011 notification-based system. As of July 2026, no final law adopted; the 2011 Decree-Law remains formally in force, though see the entry below on its administrative circumvention in practice.
AdministrativeConstitutional

Proposition de loi organique (May 2026)Unified Electoral Code Proposal

Presented: May 10, 2026 (initial proposal); May 19, 2026 (expanded coverage) | Status: Under committee review, not enacted
A group of 29 Assembly of People's Representatives deputies presented an organic law proposal to consolidate Tunisia's electoral and referendum rules — currently scattered across the base 2014 Electoral Law and its many subsequent amending organic laws and decree-laws (including the 2022 ISIE reorganization and the September 27, 2024 dispute-jurisdiction amendment already documented above) — into a single unified electoral code. Transmitted to the ARP's Committee on Internal Rules, Electoral Laws, and Parliamentary Functions on May 7, 2026. As drafted, the code runs to 261 articles across three parts: (1) rules for direct elections (presidential, legislative) and referendums; (2) rules for regional/district council elections and the Conseil National des Régions et des Districts; and (3) transitional and final provisions. The proposal would centralize rules governing presidential, legislative, municipal, local, and regional elections plus referendums in one text, revise the distribution of electoral constituencies, address vacant-seat procedures in overseas electoral districts, and clarify the relationship between candidates and the Court of Accounts on campaign-finance disputes. A May 19, 2026 committee session opened debate on two contested provisions carried over from earlier electoral-law drafts: a proposed 5,000-dinar candidacy deposit (caution) and a minimum education-level requirement for candidates, both described in Tunisian press coverage as a "sensitive debate" among deputies. Distinct from, and broader in scope than, the narrower September 2024 amendment; as of July 2026 it remains under committee examination with no vote scheduled.
ConstitutionalAdministrative

Administrative Suspensions of Civil Society OrganizationsLTDH, ASF Tunisia, ATFD & FTDES (2025–2026)

ATFD & FTDES measures: 2025 | LTDH suspension: notified April 24, 2026, for one month | Avocats Sans Frontières Tunisia suspension: notified May 5, 2026 | LTDH court appeal: postponed June 10, 2026, to July 15, 2026
Rather than amending Decree-Law 2011-88 itself, authorities have increasingly relied on administrative and fiscal powers to suspend prominent civil society organizations' activities. In April 2026, the Ligue Tunisienne pour la Défense des Droits de l'Homme (LTDH) — founded 1976, one of the oldest human rights organizations in Africa and the Arab world, and a member of the National Dialogue Quartet awarded the 2015 Nobel Peace Prize — was formally notified of a one-month suspension of its activities, officially justified on grounds of regularizing its fiscal and administrative situation. LTDH filed a court appeal against the suspension; the competent chamber of the Tunis Court of First Instance held a hearing on June 10, 2026 but postponed examination of the appeal to July 15, 2026, leaving the matter unresolved as of this update. Days later, in May 2026, Avocats Sans Frontières Tunisia (ASF Tunisia) was notified of a comparable suspension. The Tunisian Association of Democratic Women (ATFD) and the Tunisian Forum for Economic and Social Rights (FTDES) faced comparable measures during 2025, part of a wave reported by rights groups as exceeding 20 NGO suspensions since July 2025. Decree-Law 2011-88, Art. 26 requires dissolution to occur only by judicial order; rights groups (FIDH, Committee for Justice, SNJT) characterize these administrative suspensions as circumventing that judicial-order requirement and warn they set a precedent for sidelining the 2011 framework without formally repealing it. Update (Run 39): Amnesty International (May 2026) and Human Rights Watch (June 9, 2026) reports independently put the wider tally at roughly 25 associations affected by suspension or comparable administrative obstruction since mid-2025, and name additional organizations beyond the four detailed above, including Aswat Nissa, the World Organisation Against Torture's Tunisia programme (OMCT-Tunisia), I Watch (the Tunisian chapter of Transparency International), and the investigative outlet Nawaat. Both organizations frame the pattern as a coordinated use of fiscal-regularization and administrative-authorization pretexts against rights, anti-corruption, and media-adjacent groups specifically, rather than an across-the-board enforcement sweep. This remains executive/judicial practice under the existing Decree-Law 2011-88, distinct from the still-pending organic-law reform of that decree tracked in the entry above.
AdministrativeConstitutional

Law No. 69-4 of January 24, 1969Public Meetings, Processions & Demonstrations

Enacted: January 24, 1969 | In force: 1969 | Replaced: colonial-era decrees of 1905 and 1936
Regulates public meetings, processions, parades, demonstrations, and gatherings (réunions publiques, cortèges, défilés, manifestations et attroupements) — the base statute governing freedom of assembly in public spaces, distinct from and predating Decree-Law No. 2011-88 above (which governs the formation of associations, not the conduct of gatherings). Requires prior declaration to governorate or delegation authorities, or to the National Security Directorate in Tunis, typically 3 to 15 days before the event; authorities may restrict or ban gatherings on public-order grounds. Frequently cited in UN Special Rapporteur communications and civil-society reporting on freedom of assembly in Tunisia. No amendment or replacement bill was identified as of July 2026.
AdministrativeSecurityConstitutional

🛡️ Security & Counter-terrorism

Organic Law No. 2015-26 of August 7, 2015Countering Terrorism and Suppression of Money Laundering (as amended)

Enacted: August 7, 2015 (corrected from a previously listed July 7, 2015) | Amended: Organic Law No. 2019-9 of January 23, 2019
Cross-referenced from the Criminal Law section (see the fuller entry there for provisions and the 2019-9 amendment). Comprehensive counter-terrorism statute enacted following the Bardo Museum and Sousse beach attacks (2015). Replaced the 2003 anti-terrorism law. Defines terrorism broadly, criminalizes financing, propaganda, recruitment, and travel for terrorist purposes. Establishes specialized anti-terrorism courts and the Financial Intelligence Unit (CTAF). Criticized by human rights groups for overly broad definitions and extended pre-charge detention.
Key provisions
  • Broad definition of "terrorist act" including threats to public order
  • Criminalization of glorification of terrorism and incitement
  • Criminalizes financing of terrorism and related financial flows
  • Prohibition on travel to conflict zones for terrorist purposes
  • Specialized Terrorism Chamber at the Court of First Instance of Tunis
  • Extended detention periods for terrorism suspects
  • Asset freezing without prior judicial authorization
SecurityCriminal

Law No. 2019-73 of December 31, 2019Internal Security Forces Statute

Enacted: December 31, 2019 | In force: 2020
Governs the organization, status, and conduct of Tunisia's internal security forces (National Police and National Guard). Defines missions, hierarchy, disciplinary procedures, and accountability mechanisms. Establishes an independent oversight body for police conduct complaints.
SecurityAdministrative

State of Emergency Decree — Decree No. 78-50 of January 26, 1978

Enacted: January 26, 1978 | In force: 1978 | Continuously renewed since November 2015
Establishes the legal framework for declaring and managing states of emergency. Grants expanded executive powers including restriction of movement, house arrest, administrative detention, and closure of meeting places. Tunisia declared a state of emergency in November 2015 (after the Bardo Museum and Sousse beach attacks) and has renewed it without interruption since — now its tenth consecutive year. The most recent renewal, Décret n° 2026-15 of January 30, 2026 (JORT), extended the nationwide state of emergency declared by Décret n° 2025-485 of December 29, 2025 through December 31, 2026.
SecurityConstitutional

Military Justice Code — Law No. 86-79 of September 9, 1986 (amended 2019)

Enacted: September 9, 1986 | In force: 1986 | Amended: 2019
Governs military offenses and the organization of military courts (tribunaux militaires). Military courts have jurisdiction over offenses by military personnel and certain offenses against national defense. The 2019 amendment restricted jurisdiction of military courts over civilians — prohibiting trial of civilians before military courts except for offenses directly related to the armed forces — a significant human rights improvement.
SecurityCriminal

Weapons Law — Law No. 69-33 of June 12, 1969

Enacted: June 12, 1969 | Published: JORT N°23, June 13, 1969 | Implementing decree: Decree No. 70-60 of February 21, 1970
Regulates the import, trade, possession, and carrying of weapons and ammunition in Tunisia. Classifies weapons into five categories: military weapons, defensive/sporting weapons, hunting weapons, historical/collectible weapons, and air rifles. No unified "Code des Armes" exists — the framework remains this single 1969 law plus implementing decrees, with no confirmed later amendment identified.
SecurityCriminal

Law No. 93-121 of December 27, 1993National Civil Protection Office (Fire & Rescue Services) (Run 81 addition)

Enacted: December 27, 1993 | Org-chart decree: Decree No. 99-2058 of September 13, 1999 | Administrative/financial-organization decree: Decree No. 94-568 of March 15, 1994
A wholly new standalone entry — despite extensive coverage of the National Police and National Guard under the Internal Security Forces Statute above, Tunisia's separate civil-defense/fire-and-rescue institution had never been documented. Creates the Office National de la Protection Civile as an établissement public à caractère industriel et commercial (EPIC) with legal personality and financial autonomy, headquartered in Tunis and placed under the supervision of the Ministry of Interior, tasked broadly with disaster and catastrophe response (Art. 2). Confirmed via legislation-securite.tn and cross-checked secondary sourcing; the primary JORT/legislation.tn text itself could not be directly reached (HTTP 503), so this entry carries medium-high rather than primary-source-verified confidence. Note: Decree No. 99-2058 only fixes the Office's organizational chart, not general implementation — Decree No. 94-568 (administrative and financial organization) is the more substantive implementing text.
SecurityAdministrative

"Sumood/Global Sumud Flotilla" Money-Laundering & Terrorism-Financing Prosecutions (Run 91 addition)

Arrests: March 6–7, 2026 | Remanded into custody: March 16, 2026
A wholly new standalone entry, entirely distinct from the "state security conspiracy" cluster above — at least seven Tunisian activists linked to the Sumood/Global Sumud Flotilla coordination unit (a pro-Palestinian, Gaza-bound aid-flotilla solidarity network), including Nabil Channoufi, Wael Naouar, Sana Msahli, Jawaher Channa, and Mohamed Amin Bannour, were arrested March 6–7, 2026 and remanded into custody March 16, 2026 after prolonged anti-terrorism-unit questioning. Prosecutors invoke Organic Law No. 2015-26 (Countering Terrorism and Suppression of Money Laundering, see entry above) alongside general money-laundering provisions, charging the group with "forming a money laundering conspiracy" and misuse of funds raised through an international crowdfunding campaign tied to the first Global Sumud Flotilla's September 2025 Gaza-aid mission. Tunisian and international rights groups (Amnesty International, Middle East Monitor) have called for the activists' release, framing the prosecution as a use of counter-terrorism/AML statutes against pro-Palestinian civic activism rather than a genuine financial-crime case; no trial date or verdict has been reported as of this run.
SecurityCriminal

🛂 Nationality, Immigration & Asylum

Nationality Code — Law No. 63-6 of February 28, 1963

Enacted: February 28, 1963 | In force: 1963 | Amended: 1975, 2002, 2010
Defines Tunisian nationality by birth, descent, naturalization, and reacquisition. Originally limited transmission of nationality through the paternal line; the 2010 amendment allows Tunisian mothers married to foreign nationals to transmit nationality to their children. Governs loss, renunciation, and deprivation of nationality. Naturalization requires 5 years of lawful residence. Pending reform: a bill filed at the ARP on February 27, 2026 by 36 co-sponsoring deputies (now identifiable as projet de loi n° 17/2026) would tighten naturalization by requiring proof of legal/regular entry into Tunisia plus 7 consecutive years of legal residence (up from 5), while easing rules for stateless persons born in Tunisia and creating an expedited "exceptional interest" naturalization track for high-skill foreign nationals. The ARP Rights and Freedoms Commission examined the bill at a session held May 7, 2026, which press coverage of that session also described as addressing a requirement that a stateless child's parents hold legal Tunisian residency status for the child's citizenship eligibility. Under committee review as of this update; not yet adopted.
Key provisions
  • Art. 6: Nationality by birth to a Tunisian father (jus sanguinis)
  • Art. 12: Naturalization requires 5-year lawful residence
  • 2010 amendment: children of Tunisian mother and foreign father may acquire nationality
  • Art. 30–35: Loss and deprivation of Tunisian nationality
NationalityCivil

Law No. 68-7 of March 8, 1968Entry and Residence of Foreigners

Enacted: March 8, 1968 | In force: 1968 | Amended: 1975, 2004
Governs the entry, stay, and expulsion of foreign nationals in Tunisia. Requires visa for most non-Maghreb nationals (bilateral visa exemptions apply). Establishes residence permit categories and procedures. Governs administrative detention pending deportation. Tunisia has no comprehensive asylum law; refugee status was determined by UNHCR under its mandate, but in June 2024 Tunisian authorities instructed UNHCR to suspend new registration, pre-registration, and refugee-status-determination processing (services to already-registered refugees continued); the government's stated rationale was an intent to establish a national asylum system, which had not been enacted as of Human Rights Watch's 2025 and 2026 World Report chapters on Tunisia. Update (Run 103): an Amnesty International report of July 2, 2026 quantified the suspension's effect for the first time in this artifact — UNHCR-registered refugees in Tunisia fell from 18,362 in June 2024 (when the undisclosed suspension order was given) to 7,515 by April 2026, a roughly 59% decline — and documented a government-organized "voluntary return" program for migrants operating since June 2025, alongside continued reporting of abuse against refugees and asylum-seekers left without a functioning status-determination process. (Added Run 106) See the separate Passports & Travel Documents Law entry below for the distinct 1975 statute whose border-crossing offenses (rather than this law's visa/residence-permit framework) have been used to prosecute refugee-aid humanitarian workers.
NationalityAdministrative

Passports & Travel Documents Law — Law No. 75-40 of May 14, 1975

Enacted: May 14, 1975 | In force: 1975 | Amended: Organic Law No. 2015-46 of November 23, 2015; Organic Law No. 2024-23 of March 11, 2024
Governs issuance of Tunisian passports and travel documents — ordinary passports, diplomatic/service passports, laissez-passer "B," and titres de voyage "C" and "D" for stateless persons and refugees. Articles 38, 39, and 41 criminalize facilitating, arranging, or concealing a person's irregular entry into or exit from Tunisian territory, and participating in an organization or agreement to do so — offenses distinct from the Irregular Migration Law's (2004-6) criminalization of the migrants themselves and from the 2016 Anti-Human Trafficking Law's exploitation-focused offenses. Most recently amended by Organic Law No. 2024-23 of March 11, 2024, the companion statute to that year's biometric national ID card reform (see the National Identity Card Law entry), which established the biometric passport; an earlier amendment, Organic Law No. 2015-46 of November 23, 2015, updated passport-issuance procedures. Genuinely new standalone entry (Run 106): human rights organizations have documented Articles 38/39/41 being used against humanitarian workers rather than smugglers. Tunisian authorities closed the Tunisian Council for Refugees (CTR) — a UNHCR-mandated organization providing asylum-application assistance and emergency care since 2016 — in May 2024, arresting founder Mustapha Djemali and project manager Abderrazek Krimi (plus four other staff) on charges under these articles for allegedly facilitating and harboring irregular border crossings through the CTR's refugee-assistance activities. The Tunis Court of First Instance convicted both men on November 24, 2025 (2 years, 6 months suspended; released the same day, having already served roughly 18 months in pretrial detention), and the Tunis Court of Appeal's criminal chamber upheld the conviction on May 14, 2026. Human Rights Watch (May 11, 2026) and Amnesty International characterize the prosecution as an abusive misuse of anti-smuggling provisions to criminalize refugee-aid work, consistent with this artifact's broader documentation of civil-society prosecutions (see the Associations and Judicial Organization sections).
NationalityCriminal

EU–Tunisia Memorandum of UnderstandingStrategic and Global Partnership

Signed: July 16, 2023
Non-binding political declaration between the EU and Tunisia covering five pillars: macroeconomic stability, trade and investment, green energy, people-to-people contacts, and — most emphasized in implementation — migration and mobility. The EU pledged roughly €105 million for border-control capacity, €150 million in direct budget support, and a conditional €900 million in macro-financial assistance tied to an IMF program Tunisia ultimately declined to conclude. Tunisia committed to combating smuggling networks, conducting search-and-rescue operations in its territorial waters, and accepting returns of third-country nationals. The MOU bypassed parliamentary ratification on both sides. In January 2025 the European Commission overhauled its disbursement mechanism following reports of abuses by EU-funded security forces against migrants; Tunisian officials have since pushed to renegotiate the arrangement toward greater reciprocity. Update (Run 43): marking the MOU's third anniversary, a joint letter dated July 1, 2026 from Human Rights Watch, Amnesty International, the International Commission of Jurists, the Committee to Protect Journalists, EuroMed Rights, and Avocats Sans Frontières was sent to European Commission President Ursula von der Leyen calling for a full human-rights review of the partnership and conditionality on further EU funding, citing continued reports of abuses against migrants in Tunisian territory. Forms the policy backdrop to the domestic 2023–2026 migrant-policy shift above and the EU's 2026 "safe country of origin" designation below.
NationalityAdministrative

EU Designation of Tunisia as a "Safe Country of Origin"EU Pact on Migration and Asylum

European Parliament vote: February 10, 2026 (408–184–60) | Council approval: February 23, 2026 | Rules in force: June 12, 2026
Not Tunisian domestic law, but an EU-law development directly affecting Tunisian nationals abroad: under the EU's Pact on Migration and Asylum, the European Parliament and Council designated Tunisia one of several countries presumed "safe" EU-wide, subjecting Tunisian asylum-seekers in the EU to accelerated, presumptive-rejection border-procedure examination of their claims from June 12, 2026 onward. Distinct from, but connected to, the domestic 2023–2026 migrant-policy shift and UNHCR-registration suspension documented above and the 2023 EU-Tunisia migration MOU above. Flagged as a single-source-class finding from this run pending independent corroboration of the exact vote tallies and in-force date against a European Parliament or Official Journal of the EU primary text.
NationalityUnconfirmed

Irregular Migration Law — Law No. 2004-6 of February 3, 2004

Enacted: February 3, 2004 | In force: 2004
Criminalizes irregular emigration by Tunisian nationals ("harraga") with penalties of up to 3 years imprisonment. Criticized by human rights organizations as criminalizing movement. Used to prosecute individuals attempting to cross to Europe irregularly. Tunisia also receives migrants from sub-Saharan Africa; their status is governed by the 1968 Immigration Law and UNHCR procedure. Note: No domestic asylum law as of 2026; draft asylum law discussed since 2013 but not enacted.
NationalityCriminal

National Council for Tunisians Residing Abroad — Law No. 2016-68 of August 3, 2016 (reform bill pending, 2024–2026)

Enacted: August 3, 2016 | In force: 2016 | Implementing decree: Government Decree No. 2019-820 of September 10, 2019 | Reform bill: n° 2024-064, under ARP committee review since 2024
Creates the National Council for Tunisians Residing Abroad (Conseil National des Tunisiens Résidant à l'Étranger), a consultative public body with legal personality and administrative/financial autonomy, seated in Tunis and attached to the ministry responsible for Tunisians abroad. Represents Tunisia's diaspora (estimated at over 1.5 million people) before public authorities and issues an annual activity report to the President, the ARP Speaker, and the Head of Government. Government Decree No. 2019-820 (September 10, 2019) set the Council's administrative and financial organization. A reform bill (projet de loi n° 2024/064) has been under continued examination by the ARP's Foreign Affairs, International Cooperation, Tunisians Abroad, and Migration Committee since 2024, with a working session held June 4, 2026 on its text, followed by a further joint hearing on June 22, 2026 with the President of the National Council for Tunisians Abroad herself. The bill would restructure the Council's general assembly, introduce individual, direct, voluntary-basis voting (including electronic voting) for diaspora representatives on a gender-parity basis — replacing the current framework's limited effective functioning since 2016 — and reposition the Council as a horizontal coordination body unifying public policy across ministries dealing with Tunisians abroad and mobilizing expatriate expertise for national economic development. Not yet adopted as of this run. (Cross-ref added Run 109): separately from this reform bill, Article 83 of the Finance Law 2026 already enacted new tax and customs advantages specifically for this Council's constituency — see the Finance Law 2026 entry (Tax & Fiscal section).
NationalityAdministrativePending

⚖️ Judicial Organization

Judicial Organization Law — Law No. 2013-13 of April 2, 2013 (as amended 2022)

Enacted: April 2, 2013 | In force: 2013 | Amended: 2022
Organizes the court system of Tunisia: Cantonal Courts (minor matters), Courts of First Instance (24, one per governorate), Courts of Appeal (3 — Tunis, Sousse, Sfax), Court of Cassation (apex civil/criminal court, Tunis), Administrative Court (apex administrative jurisdiction), and the Constitutional Court (established under 2022 Constitution). Specialized jurisdictions include the Terrorism Chamber at TFI Tunis.
Court structure
  • Cantonal Courts: minor civil matters, misdemeanor infractions
  • Courts of First Instance (24): civil, commercial, criminal chambers
  • Courts of Appeal (3 — Tunis, Sousse, Sfax): appellate jurisdiction
  • Court of Cassation (Tunis): reviews points of law only
  • Administrative Court: all public law disputes
  • Terrorism Chamber: specialized jurisdiction at TFI Tunis level
  • Constitutional Court: established under 2022 Constitution (Arts. 116–120)
AdministrativeConstitutional

Judicial Statute — Law No. 67-29 of July 14, 1967 (as amended)

Enacted: July 14, 1967 | In force: 1967 | Amended: 1997, 2013, 2022
Governs the appointment, promotion, transfer, discipline, and removal of judges. Establishes the Superior Council of the Judiciary (Conseil Supérieur de la Magistrature — CSM) as the body responsible for judicial governance. Post-2022, the CSM's composition was changed by presidential decree, significantly reducing judicial independence according to the UN Special Rapporteur on the Independence of Judges and Lawyers. Update (Run 40): tensions over judicial and legal-profession independence escalated in mid-2026. The Tunisian National Bar Association (ONAT) staged an escalating protest campaign — a May 13 Bar Council escalation vote, a symbolic May 18 red-armband action, then rallies at courts in Tunis (Bab Bnet), Bizerte, Béja, Jendouba, Le Kef, and Siliana — culminating in a nationwide lawyers' general strike on June 18, 2026 demanding reform of the profession's legal framework and stronger judicial-independence guarantees; Bâtonnier Boubaker Belthabet called it a success, but no government response had followed as of late June 2026, and on June 9, 2026 forty international legal and human-rights organizations issued a joint statement condemning an escalating campaign against lawyers, judges, and the Bar. Separately, judge Anas Hmaidi — president of the Association of Tunisian Magistrates (AMT), re-elected at its February 2026 congress despite government pressure — was sentenced to one year in prison on April 6, 2026, prompting condemnation from the International Union of Magistrates and the African Commission on Human and Peoples' Rights, both linking the prosecution to the continued absence of a permanent, independent Superior Judicial Council (see the Provisional Supreme Judicial Council entry below). Update (Run 42): the Tunis Court of Appeal's correctional chamber confirmed Hmaidi's one-year sentence on July 1, 2026 in a ruling issued by default (par défaut/in absentia), this time ordering immediate execution of the sentence — a harsher procedural posture than the first-instance judgment. TAP/Diwan FM reporting confirmed the same legal qualification (Art. 136 Penal Code, obstruction of the freedom to work) tied to his conduct at a June 13, 2022 hearing in Monastir; Hmaidi continues to dispute the legitimacy of the Provisional Supreme Council of the Judiciary body that lifted his immunity on September 20, 2022.
