Update (Run 43): ONAT's annual general assembly was reported scheduled for July 11, 2026, a potential venue for the Bar to set conditions for renewed dialogue with the government following the unresolved June 18, 2026 general strike; no government response to the strike's demands had been reported as of this run. Update (Run 46): confirmed and refined — the July 11, 2026 assembly is not an elective session (Belthabet was already elected bâtonnier for a 2025–2028 term in September 2025); Belthabet has framed its purpose as convening the Bar Council and general assembly to "evaluate the evolution of the dispute [with the authorities] and adopt consequent measures," i.e., a strategy review on whether to escalate or seek renewed dialogue. Separately, the public prosecutor at the Tunis Court of Appeal filed an appeal challenging the quorum/procedural validity of an earlier lawyers' extraordinary general assembly, with a hearing reported set for June 10, 2026 — outcome not independently confirmed. As of this run (July 2, 2026), the government still had not proposed any official or informal mediation, per Belthabet, though ONAT says it remains open to dialogue. Update (Run 60): on July 2, 2026, UN Special Rapporteur on the independence of judges and lawyers Margaret Satterthwaite publicly stated that Hmaidi's conviction "should have been overturned," a statement the AMT amplified in its own denunciation of the July 1 appeals ruling as unfair — the first UN Special Procedures reaction specifically naming this case, distinct from the broader CSM-composition criticism already cited above. Update (Run 91): a further, previously undocumented lawyer-harassment case closed a genuine gap — Ahmed Souab, a lawyer, former administrative judge, and prominent human rights defender, was arrested at his home on April 21, 2025 by anti-terrorism officers over public statements he made after representing defendants in the "state security conspiracy" case (see that standalone entry above), and was convicted at first instance to 5 years' imprisonment following a trial Amnesty International said lasted only seven minutes. On appeal, the Tunis Court of Appeal reduced his sentence to 10 months' firm imprisonment plus a 2-year suspended sentence and ordered his immediate release on February 23, 2026 (already served), also lifting the administrative-surveillance measure imposed at first instance — a rare, if partial, judicial correction amid the broader pattern of prosecutions against lawyers and judges documented in this entry.
AdministrativeConstitutional

Decree-Law No. 2022-11 of February 12, 2022Provisional Supreme Judicial Council

Enacted: February 12, 2022 | Published: JORT No. 2022-016
The specific decree-law referenced above: dissolved the elected Supreme Judicial Council (created under Organic Law No. 2016-34) and replaced it with a "Provisional Supreme Council of the Judiciary," composed predominantly of senior ex officio magistrates plus members appointed by the President from candidates submitted via the Prime Ministry. Retains three specialized sub-councils (judicial, administrative, financial). Remains the operative — still "provisional" — body as of mid-2026, with no permanent council established to replace it.
AdministrativeConstitutional

Decree-Law No. 2022-35 of June 1, 2022Magistrate Dismissal Power (Completing Decree-Law No. 2022-11)

Enacted: June 1, 2022
Grants the President of the Republic power to summarily dismiss any magistrate by simple presidential decree in cases of declared "emergency," threat to public security, or harm to "the country's superior interest," based on a report from unspecified "competent authorities." Dismissal triggers automatic criminal referral; the dismissed magistrate cannot appeal until after a final criminal judgment. Used the same day to dismiss 57 judges and prosecutors, condemned by HRW, Amnesty International, and EuroMed Rights; the Tunisian Association of Magistrates continued to denounce the dismissals as unresolved injustice as of June 2026.
AdministrativeConstitutionalCriminal

Organic Law No. 2013-53 of December 24, 2013Transitional Justice

Enacted: December 24, 2013 | In force: 2014
Establishes Tunisia's transitional justice framework including the Truth and Dignity Commission (IVD — Instance Vérité et Dignité). Covers: truth-seeking, reparations, institutional reform, and accountability for human rights violations committed between July 1, 1955 and December 31, 2013. IVD operated from 2014–2019; issued final report in 2019 documenting systematic torture, political persecution, and economic crimes.
AdministrativeConstitutionalCriminal

Organic Law No. 2013-43 of October 2013National Authority for the Prevention of Torture (INPT) (Run 66 addition)

Adopted by NCA: October 9, 2013 | Enacted: October 21 or 23, 2013 (sources diverge on the exact day; JORT No. 85/2013) | Members elected by ARP: March 29–30, 2016
A wholly new standalone entry — despite 65 prior research runs and the extradition entry below referencing "torture risk" as a ground for refusal, no dedicated entry existed for Tunisia's National Preventive Mechanism against torture. Implements Tunisia's OPCAT obligations (ratified June 2011) via the Instance Nationale pour la Prévention de la Torture (INPT), the first such mechanism in the MENA region: 16 members elected by Parliament from human-rights associations, magistrates, doctors, and child-protection specialists, holding legal immunity and empowered to conduct unannounced visits to any place of deprivation of liberty; authorities may refuse a visit only for narrow, written, reasoned reasons. The INPT remains active as of mid-2026, distinct from the (separately documented) Truth and Dignity Commission above, which addressed historical rather than ongoing torture: it publicly criticized severe prison overcrowding in November 2024 (population risen from ~23,000 to over 32,000–33,000 against ~17,000 capacity), published a "Guide to Detainees' Rights" in November 2025, and issued a "zero impunity" statement for the UN International Day in Support of Victims of Torture on June 27, 2026. No confirmed reports of INPT defunding or leadership removal were found for 2024–2026.
JudicialCriminalConstitutional

"State Security Conspiracy" Prosecutions and Related 2025–2026 Convictions

Arrests from: February 2023 | Case 1 sentences: April 19, 2025 | Case 1 appeal: November 27–28, 2025 | Case 2 appeal: February 2, 2026 | "Secret Apparatus" case verdict: June 2, 2026 | Bensedrine sentenced: June 26, 2026 | Smara appeal: July 2, 2026
A mass prosecution of dozens of opposition politicians, lawyers, and activists on charges of conspiracy against state security, prosecuted under general Penal Code state-security provisions (Arts. 72–85, see Criminal Law section) rather than a dedicated statute — added here as a standalone entry given its significance to Tunisia's post-2021 rule-of-law trajectory, previously undocumented in this artifact despite 39 prior research runs. Case 1 (defendants including Khayam Turki, Ridha Belhaj, Ghazi Chaouachi, Jaouhar Ben Mbarek, Issam Chebbi, and Abdelhamid Jelassi) produced severe sentences on April 19, 2025, described by Human Rights Watch as a "sham trial"; on appeal (Nov 27–28, 2025), 34 of 37 sentences (5–45 years) were upheld and 3 defendants acquitted (Lazher Akremi, Hattab Ben Slama, Noureddine Boutar), with several sentences increased (Chebbi, Ben Mbarek, Chaouachi, and Belhaj raised from 18 to 20 years) while businessman Kamel Ltaief's sentence was reduced from 66 to 45 years, remaining the heaviest in the case. A related but separate Case 2, focused on Ennahda party leadership, produced an appeals ruling on February 2, 2026 sentencing roughly 20 defendants to 3–35 years; Ennahda leader Rached Ghannouchi's sentence was raised from 14 to 20 years (56 years cumulative across his cases), and co-defendants Kamel Guizani, Rafik Abdessalem, Lotfi Zitoun, and Mouadh Ghannouchi received 35 years in absentia — Amnesty International described the ruling as deepening a "rule-of-law crisis." Separately, Sihem Bensedrine — journalist and former president of the Truth and Dignity Commission (IVD, see the Transitional Justice entry above) — was sentenced to 25 years in prison on June 26, 2026, among the most severe individual sentences of a rights defender since 2021. Opposition politician Abir Moussi (Free Destourian Party leader, detained since October 2023) also had a Tunis Court of Appeal ruling reported around March 2026, condemned by Amnesty International as unjust. See the Judicial Statute entry above for the related prosecution of AMT president judge Anas Hmaidi, whose one-year sentence was confirmed on appeal (with immediate execution ordered) on July 1, 2026 — Run 42 update. Update (Run 60): a related but distinct terrorism-chamber case opened July 3, 2026: the Tunis Court of First Instance's terrorism chamber rejected pre-trial release requests (June 30, 2026) for Ennahda secretary-general Ajmi Lourimi and co-defendant Mossaâb Gharbi, prosecuted under the Anti-Terrorism Law (Law No. 2015-26, see Criminal Law section) for failing to report a terrorism-related offense; no verdict has been reported as of this run. Update (Run 63): a further related but distinct prosecution — former MP Sahbi Smara, charged not with joining the conspiracy but with fabricating one to incriminate political opponents ahead of the October 2024 presidential election — had his 11-year first-instance sentence reduced to approximately nine and a half years on appeal, July 2, 2026 (Kapitalis, Business News, Tunisie Numérique, Gnet News). Smara was separately sentenced in March 2026 to 6 months' imprisonment for assaulting Abir Moussi inside Parliament while both were sitting deputies, illustrating how this cluster of prosecutions has widened to include cases arising from, rather than merely alongside, the original conspiracy proceedings. Update (Run 81): Case 1 defendant Khayam Turki carries two further, entirely distinct convictions that should not be conflated with his conspiracy sentence above: a April 21, 2026 Tunis Court of First Instance ruling of 14 years for fraud, money laundering, and aggravated breach of trust, arising from a UAE company's complaint over a North African land-acquisition project (Kapitalis, Businessnews); and a July 3, 2026 first-instance conviction of 4 years on strictly tax-related offenses tied to his business-portfolio management, distinct from both prior cases (La Presse de Tunisie, Businessnews, allAfrica). Three separate Turki dockets are now on record: the conspiracy case (above), the April 2026 money-laundering case, and the July 2026 tax case. Update (Run 101): a wholly distinct, considerably larger and more severe case — the "affaire de l'appareil secret" ("Secret Apparatus" case) — reached a first-instance verdict June 2, 2026 from the specialized anti-terrorism criminal chamber of the Tunis Court of First Instance, and should not be conflated with the Case 1/Case 2 conspiracy prosecutions above: it was opened in 2022 following a complaint by lawyers for the families of assassinated leftist politicians Chokri Belaïd and Mohamed Brahmi (killed 2013), alleging that a clandestine Ennahda security structure — directly supervised, per the prosecution, by Rached Ghannouchi — orchestrated political assassinations, conducted espionage, and infiltrated state institutions; the 35 defendants were charged under the Anti-Terrorism Law (Law No. 2015-26, see Criminal Law section) with constituting a terrorist organization, rather than under the Penal Code Arts. 72–85 state-security-conspiracy provisions underlying Case 1/Case 2. Sentences: Ghannouchi received life imprisonment plus 30 additional years; alleged mastermind Mustapha Khedher received life imprisonment plus 96 additional years (the case's heaviest sentence); former Prime Minister Ali Laarayedh received 42 years; eleven further defendants received life sentences plus additional terms, and thirteen others received 10–48 years. Ennahdha denounced the sentences as "scandaleuses" and politically motivated; rights groups (DAWN) and Ghannouchi's defense contested the verdict's legitimacy (Al Jazeera, Bloomberg, La Presse de Tunisie, businessnews.com.tn, Le Courrier de l'Atlas, Kapitalis, Middle East Eye, Middle East Monitor, all June 1–6, 2026). No appeal outcome has been reported as of this run.
CriminalConstitutional

Affaire Habib Haouas / SOREAL — Organized Money Laundering Conviction (Run 85 addition)

Arrest warrant: November 17, 2023 | Customs reconciliation agreement: June 22, 2026 | Verdict: June 29, 2026
A wholly new standalone entry, entirely distinct from the Mabrouk/Chahed/Turki dockets above — this is an ordinary (non-political) organized-crime financial case, not part of the "state security conspiracy" cluster. Businessman Habib Haouas, former director of metal-recycling firm SOREAL Tunisie (founded 1997) and resident abroad since at least 2017, was sentenced on June 29, 2026 by the specialized financial-corruption criminal chamber of the Tunis Court of First Instance to 22 years' imprisonment (a co-defendant received 24 years) for organized-group money laundering, abusive exploitation of company mechanisms, and property damage, with customs, exchange-control, and tax-fraud offenses also charged; the underlying investigation dates to a November 17, 2023 arrest warrant from the Economic and Financial Judicial Pole. A June 22, 2026 reconciliation agreement with the Customs Directorate-General (~12 million TND) was presented to the court by defense counsel but did not alter the sentence (Businessnews, Kapitalis, La Presse de Tunisie, L'Economiste Maghrébin, all June 30–July 1, 2026).

Affaire Makram Jlassi — Embezzlement Conviction (Run 87 addition)

Dismissed from Justice Ministry post: effective July 10, 2024 (JORT) | First-instance sentence: March 11, 2026 (5 years) | Appeal ruling: July 4, 2026 (raised to 8 years)
A wholly new standalone entry, distinct from both the "state security conspiracy" cluster and the Haouas/SOREAL case above — an ordinary embezzlement/breach-of-trust prosecution of a former Justice Ministry official. Makram Jlassi, a former mission officer (chargé de mission) in the Justice Minister's cabinet dismissed from that post effective July 10, 2024, was convicted by the Tunis Court of First Instance's criminal chamber on March 11, 2026 and sentenced to 5 years' imprisonment for embezzlement and breach of trust, following a complaint filed by the Tunisian Association for the Prevention of Road Accidents. On appeal, the Tunis Court of Appeal's criminal chamber confirmed the underlying conviction but increased the sentence to 8 years on July 4, 2026 (reported same-day by WebManagerCenter/Directinfo, webdo, African Manager, Réalités, and Kapitalis).

Affaire Taoufik Mekacher — Unsecured Public-Bank Loans Conviction (Run 92 addition)

Related ex-bank-official first-instance sentence: February 1, 2026 (2 years) | Mekacher first-instance verdict: July 3, 2026 (6 years)
A wholly new standalone entry, distinct from the Haouas/SOREAL and Jlassi cases above — another ordinary (non-political) financial-crime prosecution from the same specialized financial-corruption criminal chamber of the Tunis Court of First Instance that has produced several of this cluster's convictions in the same window (see the Run 81 Mabrouk/Chahed and Turki updates on the Anti-Terrorism/AML and State Security Conspiracy entries above). Businessman Taoufik Mekacher — previously convicted in July 2021 to 5 years for uncovered checks and forgery, a separate matter — was sentenced on July 3, 2026 to 6 years' imprisonment for obtaining substantial loans from a public bank without sufficient guarantees, causing the institution financial harm; the court entered a non-lieu (discontinuance) for a co-defendant bank official in this same ruling. The case is part of a broader "affaire des prêts bancaires sans garantie" implicating some 31 suspects including a former minister (Samir Saïed, ordered kept in provisional detention as of a January 2025 ruling), within which a separate ex-bank-official defendant had already been convicted to 2 years on February 1, 2026 for the same underlying loan-approval practice (La Presse de Tunisie, Businessnews/Mkacher variant spelling, allAfrica, all July 3, 2026; Kapitalis and Webdo for the broader case background).
CriminalFinancial

Affaire Taïeb Rached — "Cassations Sans Renvoi" Judicial Corruption Case (Run 103 addition)

Unauthorized chambers created: 2019 | First-instance verdict: October 27–28, 2025 | Appeal verdict: May 23, 2026
A wholly new standalone entry — distinct from the Haouas/SOREAL, Jlassi, and Mekacher financial-crime cases above, and the first prosecution of a judge of this rank in Tunisian judicial history. In 2019, as First President of the Court of Cassation, Taïeb Rached created two chambers without legal basis (Nos. 35 and 36) and appointed magistrates Abderrazek Bahouri and Marouane Tlili to sit on them; those chambers issued "cassation sans renvoi" rulings (cassation without remand for retrial) that benefited businessmen Nejib Ismaïl and Fethi Jenayah, shielding them from further prosecution and causing the Tunisian state an estimated one billion TND in losses. The Tunis Court of First Instance's specialized financial-corruption chamber convicted Rached on October 27–28, 2025 to 30 years' imprisonment, with Bahouri and Tlili (both since revoked from the magistracy) sentenced to 20 years each, alongside civil damages ordered against the judges and businessman Fethi Jenayah. On appeal, the Tunis Court of Appeal's specialized financial-corruption chamber reduced all three judges' sentences to 10 years each on May 23, 2026, while confirming roughly 935 million TND in civil damages against the three judges and Jenayah; other businessman co-defendants in the case include Adel Jenayah, Kamel Taboubi, and El Wardi Nouisri. Independently corroborated across Kapitalis, L'Économiste Maghrébin, La Presse de Tunisie, Directinfo/WebManagerCenter, African Manager, and allAfrica.
CriminalJudicialFinancial

Mediation Law — Law No. 2002-82 of August 3, 2002

Enacted: August 3, 2002 | In force: 2002
Establishes the legal framework for civil and commercial mediation as an alternative dispute resolution (ADR) mechanism. Mediators must be registered and trained. A mediation agreement, once signed by parties and approved by the competent court, is enforceable as a court judgment. The Startup Act (2018) encourages mediation for commercial disputes involving labeled startups. Reduces court caseloads and offers faster, confidential dispute resolution.
JudicialCivil

Notaries Law — Law No. 94-60 of May 23, 1994(Run 44 correction)

Enacted: May 23, 1994 | Predecessor: Law No. 88-27 of July 12, 1988
Run 44 correction: prior runs cited this statute as "Law No. 67-39 of June 8, 1967" — no such law could be found in any source searched (NATLEX, jurisitetunisie.com, legislation-securite.tn), and it appears to have been a fabricated citation. The verified currently-operative statute, confirmed directly from jurisitetunisie.com's full-text reproduction and cited in the preamble of the 2011 decree-law reorganizing the lawyer profession, is Law No. 94-60 of May 23, 1994 — 65 articles across 8 chapters (registration on the professional roll; powers; legal status; rights/obligations; discipline; regional chambers and national association; transitional provisions). It separated the notarial function from that of court bailiff (huissier de justice), a split not present in the probable predecessor, Law No. 88-27 of July 12, 1988. Notaries are public officers authenticating deeds, contracts, inheritance declarations, and real property transactions; notarial acts are enforceable without court order, and all real property transfers, mortgage deeds, and company-formation documents must be notarized. Supervised by regional chambers of notaries and a national professional association.
JudicialCivil

Law No. 87-79 of December 29, 1987 (as amended)Lawyers Statute (Barreau de Tunisie)

Enacted: December 29, 1987 | In force: 1988 | Amended: 1999, 2009
Governs the legal profession and bar association in Tunisia. The Bar Association (Ordre National des Avocats de Tunisie — ONAT) is the self-regulatory body for all lawyers admitted to the Tunisian bar. Sets qualification requirements (law degree + 3-year pupillage), bar admission conditions, and continuing education obligations. Regulates fee structures, professional conduct, confidentiality (avocat-client privilege), conflict of interest rules, and disciplinary procedures. Guarantees lawyers' immunity from prosecution for oral or written statements made in the exercise of their functions before courts.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–10: Bar membership; qualification requirements; pupillage (stage) period
  • Art. 11–20: Professional rights — audience before all courts; freedom of speech in court
  • Art. 21–28: Inviolability of lawyer-client communications; prohibition of office searches without ONAT involvement
  • Art. 29–36: Professional conduct rules; prohibition of conflicts of interest
  • Art. 37–44: Fee arrangements; prohibition of contingency fees as primary arrangement
  • Art. 45–52: ONAT governance — National Council, regional bars (Tunis, Sousse, Sfax, etc.)
  • Art. 53–60: Disciplinary council; sanctions ranging from warning to permanent disbarment
JudicialAdministrative

Law No. 2002-52 of June 3, 2002Legal Aid (Aide Judiciaire) (Run 44 correction)

Enacted: June 3, 2002 | Amended: Law No. 2007-27 of May 7, 2007
Run 44 correction: prior runs cited this statute as "Law No. 2002-42 of April 3, 2002" — no such law could be verified in any source searched, and it appears to have been a fabricated citation. The verified statute, confirmed via legislation-securite.tn and jurisitetunisie.com's "bureau d'aide judiciaire" page, is Law No. 2002-52 of June 3, 2002, amended by Law No. 2007-27 of May 7, 2007 (extending eligibility to criminal cassation appeals). Establishes Tunisia's legal aid system for civil and criminal matters: any natural person lacking sufficient income to cover court/enforcement costs without compromising basic necessities may apply, at any stage of proceedings; non-profit legal persons and foreigners (subject to reciprocity) also qualify. Applications are decided by a Legal Aid Bureau attached to each Court of First Instance, chaired by the Republic Prosecutor with a Finance Ministry representative and a lawyer as members; decisions are not subject to appeal, though a new application may be resubmitted. A separate, older track — Decree No. 74-882 of September 26, 1974, replaced by Organic Law No. 2011-2 of January 3, 2011 — governs legal aid specifically before the Administrative Court.
JudicialSocial

Law No. 66-49 of July 2, 1966 (as amended)Judicial Officers (Huissiers de Justice)

Enacted: July 2, 1966 | In force: 1966 | Amended: 1995, 2002, 2011
Regulates the profession of judicial officers (huissiers de justice) in Tunisia — officers of the court responsible for serving legal process, executing judgments, and conducting judicial auctions (ventes aux enchères). Huissiers are public officers appointed by the Ministry of Justice; they hold a monopoly on service of process and enforcement of court judgments. Regulated by the National Chamber of Huissiers (Chambre Nationale des Huissiers de Justice). The 2011 amendment introduced electronic service of process as a supplementary method. Pending reform: a modernization bill (digitized archives, revised access conditions to the profession, expanded legal protections for huissiers) is under review, supported by the professional order; a provision within it would additionally allow mutual-consent divorce to be finalized before a huissier-notaire without court involvement, which women's rights groups have criticized as weakening Personal Status Code judicial safeguards for wives. Not yet enacted.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–8: Status of huissier as public officer; appointment by Ministry of Justice
  • Art. 9–16: Territorial jurisdiction; offices by judicial circuit
  • Art. 17–24: Service of process procedures (summons, notifications, judgments)
  • Art. 25–32: Enforcement of civil judgments (seizure of assets, evictions, expulsions)
  • Art. 33–38: Conduct of judicial auctions of seized movable property
  • Art. 39–44: Fee schedule (tariff set by ministerial decree)
  • Art. 45–50: Professional discipline; liability for service errors
JudicialCivilPending

Extradition & Mutual Legal Assistance — Code of Criminal Procedure, Book IV, Ch. VIII (Arts. 308–335)

Base code: Law No. 68-23 of July 24, 1968 | Extradition chapter amended by Law No. 93-113 of November 22, 1993
Tunisia has no standalone extradition or mutual-legal-assistance statute — the current cybercrime entry elsewhere in this artifact notes only in passing that the Budapest Convention "complement[s]" extradition mechanisms, without a dedicated entry for the underlying domestic framework. Extradition of foreigners is instead governed entirely by Book IV, Chapter VIII of the Code of Criminal Procedure (conditions, Arts. 308–315; procedure, Arts. 316–328, including a chambre d'accusation review and presidential decree under Art. 324; effects, Arts. 329–335), supplemented by bilateral and multilateral treaties. Tunisian nationals cannot be extradited; political offenses and torture risk are recognized grounds for refusal. Update (Run 66): beyond the 1972 France convention, dedicated bilateral extradition treaties are in force with Belgium (1990), Malta (2006), Portugal (1998), Turkey (1983), Syria (2007), and Germany (1969); a new Algeria–Tunisia judicial-cooperation and extradition convention was signed December 15, 2021 and ratified by Tunisia via Presidential Decree No. 2022-312 of April 8, 2022 (Algeria's ratification reported February 2025). No dedicated extradition treaty was confirmed with Italy, Libya, Egypt, Morocco, the UK, or the US. Multilaterally, Tunisia is party to the UN Convention against Transnational Organized Crime/Palermo Convention (ratified June 19, 2003) and its Trafficking Protocol (July 14, 2003), UNCAC (ratified September 23, 2008), and the 1998 Arab Convention on the Suppression of Terrorism (ratified via Law No. 99-10 of February 15, 1999). A 2025–2026 test case saw the Paris Court of Appeal reject Tunisia's extradition request for Halima Ben Ali, daughter of the late president, in April 2026, reportedly over inadequate fair-trial guarantees.
JudicialCriminal

Key International Conventions Ratified by Tunisia

Various dates
Tunisia is party to numerous international conventions forming part of domestic law upon ratification (monist approach per Art. 20 of the 2022 Constitution).
Major conventions

  • ICCPR — ratified March 18, 1969

  • Arab Charter on Human Rights — ratified 2008

  • Paris Agreement on Climate Change — ratified July 3, 2017

Withdrawal of the Article 34(6) DeclarationProtocol to the African Charter on Human and Peoples' Rights on the Establishment of an African Court

Reported: March 2026
Tunisia reportedly withdrew its declaration under Article 34(6) of the Ouagadougou Protocol establishing the African Court on Human and Peoples' Rights, which had permitted individuals and NGOs to file complaints directly with the Court without first exhausting proceedings before the African Commission. If confirmed, the withdrawal would close that direct-access route for Tunisian complainants going forward, mirroring earlier withdrawals by Rwanda, Tanzania, Benin, and Côte d'Ivoire, and would mark a rollback of one of Tunisia's post-2011 international human-rights-law commitments (Tunisia ratified the underlying African Charter in 1983 and the Protocol establishing the Court separately). Flagged as a single-source finding from this run's research pass, pending stronger corroboration (e.g., an African Court press release or JORT/Ministry of Foreign Affairs text) in a future run — consistent with this artifact's practice of noting unverified leads rather than silently omitting or overstating them.

EU-Tunisia Association Agreement — Law No. 97-37 of July 1, 1997

Signed: July 17, 1995 | Ratified by Tunisia: July 1, 1997 | In force: March 1, 1998
Association agreement between Tunisia and the European Union — the first Euro-Mediterranean Partnership agreement. Established a free trade area for manufactured goods, progressively eliminating tariffs by 2008. Includes provisions on services, capital, intellectual property, and political dialogue. Supplemented by Action Plans under the European Neighbourhood Policy. DCFTA (Deep and Comprehensive Free Trade Area) negotiations launched 2016, not yet concluded as of 2026.
CommercialConstitutional

Arab Maghreb Union Treaty — 1989 (Marrakesh Treaty)

Signed: February 17, 1989 | Ratified: 1989
Treaty establishing the Arab Maghreb Union (UMA) among Tunisia, Algeria, Morocco, Libya, and Mauritania. Aims for eventual economic integration and free movement. In practice largely dormant due to Algeria-Morocco tensions. Bilateral agreements within the AMU framework remain in force, including free movement protocols for Maghreb nationals within the region.
CommercialConstitutional

Decree No. 23 of 2025Ratification of the North Western Sahara Aquifer System Consultation Mechanism

Underlying agreement signed: April 24, 2024 (Algeria, tripartite agriculture-ministers meeting) | Ratifying decree published: JORT Issue No. 4, January 11, 2025
A two-article decree: Art. 1 ratifies the tripartite agreement establishing a consultation mechanism ("mécanisme de concertation") for shared management of the North Western Sahara Aquifer System (SASS/NWSAS), a transboundary fossil groundwater resource shared by Tunisia, Algeria, and Libya and critical to southern Tunisia's water supply; Art. 2 covers publication. Algeria ratified the same agreement in parallel.
EnvironmentalConstitutional

UNCLOS Ratification — April 24, 1985

Ratified: April 24, 1985 | Convention in force: November 16, 1994
Tunisia ratified the United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea (UNCLOS). Defines maritime zones: 12-nautical-mile territorial sea, 24 nm contiguous zone, and 200 nm Exclusive Economic Zone (EEZ). Bilateral maritime delimitation agreements concluded with Italy (1971, continental shelf) and Libya (boundary established by ICJ ruling in 1982). Tunisia's EEZ in the Mediterranean is significant for fisheries and hydrocarbon exploration rights.
ConstitutionalTransport

WTO Membership and Bilateral Trade Agreements

WTO membership: March 29, 1995 | Ongoing bilateral and regional agreements
Tunisia is a WTO member and party to multiple bilateral and regional trade agreements: Pan-Arab Free Trade Area (PAFTA, 1997); EU Association Agreement (1998); Agadir Agreement with Egypt, Jordan, and Morocco (2007, implementing diagonal cumulation of origin); EFTA-Tunisia Free Trade Agreement (2005). DCFTA talks with the EU and negotiations for African Continental Free Trade Area (AfCFTA) integration remain active as of 2026.
CommercialConstitutional

Agadir Agreement — Law No. 2004-20 of February 24, 2004 (ratification) | Operational: 2007

Agreement signed: February 25, 2004 | Ratified by Tunisia: February 24, 2004 | Operational: March 2007
The Agadir Agreement on the establishment of a free trade zone between Tunisia, Morocco, Egypt, and Jordan — four countries with EU Association Agreements. Eliminates tariffs and non-tariff barriers on trade in goods between member states. A key feature is the application of diagonal cumulation of origin with the EU, allowing products manufactured from inputs originating in any member country to benefit from EU preferential tariffs. Managed by the Agadir Technical Unit (ATU) based in Amman. Tunisia's exports to Egypt and Jordan are primarily industrial goods, olive oil, and chemicals. The agreement fits within the Euro-Mediterranean Partnership framework and enhances supply chain integration between North Africa and the Middle East.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–5: Establishment of the free trade zone; objectives
  • Art. 6–10: Tariff elimination schedule for industrial goods (immediate for most)
  • Art. 11–15: Rules of origin: Pan-Euro-Mediterranean cumulation rules apply
  • Art. 16–20: Agricultural goods: partial liberalization with tariff-rate quotas for sensitive items
  • Art. 21–25: Trade facilitation: simplified customs procedures, single document
  • Art. 26–30: Dispute resolution mechanism; role of ATU
  • Art. 31–35: Safeguard clauses; anti-dumping provisions
CommercialConstitutional

AfCFTA Ratification — Law No. 2021-19 of June 7, 2021

Agreement adopted: March 21, 2018 | Tunisia ratified: June 7, 2021 | Trading began: January 1, 2021 (for earlier ratifiers)
Tunisia ratified the Agreement Establishing the African Continental Free Trade Area (AfCFTA) in June 2021, becoming one of 44 countries to ratify by that date (out of 55 AU member states). The AfCFTA creates the world's largest free trade area by number of participating countries, covering 1.3 billion people and a GDP of approximately $3.4 trillion. Phase I covers trade in goods (tariff reduction) and trade in services. Phase II covers investment, intellectual property, competition, and e-commerce. Tunisia hopes to use the AfCFTA to diversify its export markets beyond Europe (currently ~70% of trade), targeting sub-Saharan Africa's consumer markets, particularly for processed foods, pharmaceuticals, textiles, and ICT services.
Key provisions
  • Tariff reduction: 90% of tariff lines phased to zero over 5–10 years (LDC treatment: 15 years)
  • Sensitive goods: 7% of tariff lines; longer liberalization schedules
  • Excluded goods: 3% (limited exclusions for security or development reasons)
  • Rules of origin: negotiated per sector; generally 25–35% local value addition
  • AfCFTA Secretariat in Accra, Ghana manages dispute resolution and implementation
  • Services schedules: Phase II negotiations ongoing; Tunisia targeting professional services and ICT
CommercialConstitutional

Organic Law (Projet de loi organique n° 07/2026) — Ratified by ARP April 7, 2026Hosting Agreement for the African Centre of Excellence for Inclusive Markets (AIMEC)

Underlying agreement signed: July 14, 2022 (sidelines of the 41st AU Executive Council session, Lusaka) | Ratified by ARP plenary: April 7, 2026 (92 for, 3 against, 3 abstentions) | AIMEC officially launched in Tunis: October 14, 2025
Organic law ratifying the 2022 headquarters agreement between Tunisia and the African Union Commission establishing the seat of the African Centre of Excellence for Inclusive Markets (AIMEC) in Tunis. AIMEC is an AU Commission institution supporting the development of inclusive, integrated markets across Africa — fostering the participation of vulnerable groups in production and consumption, advancing Sustainable Development Goals, and promoting innovation in economic policy. The law establishes the legal framework governing the Centre's activities in Tunisia and guarantees its administrative independence. During ARP committee review, Trade Minister Samir Abid was questioned over a perceived deficit of Tunisian diplomatic representation within the Centre's governance. Following ratification, Youth and Sports Minister Sadok Mourali received AIMEC's executive director, Dr. Youssef Ahmed Kardfani, on April 10, 2026, to discuss deepening cooperation on youth economic inclusion. (Run 77 addition — a genuine gap in the international-treaties watchlist that 76 prior runs had missed entirely, cross-verified across African Manager, WebManagerCenter, Business News, and La Presse de Tunisie.)
ConstitutionalCommercial

ILO Convention C193 — Decent Work in the Digital Platform Economy (Run 99 addition)

Adopted: 114th International Labour Conference, Geneva, June 28, 2026 (406–8–36) | Tunisia's vote: in favor | Status: not yet ratified domestically
Not yet Tunisian domestic law — a newly-adopted ILO convention Tunisia voted to support, distinct from ratification. The 114th International Labour Conference adopted Convention No. 193 extending core labor protections (occupational safety and health, fair compensation, protection from violence and harassment, data-privacy safeguards, algorithmic-transparency/review rights, and social-security access) to digital-platform workers, described by the ILO as a "decisive step" ensuring technological innovation respects worker rights. Tunisia's Minister of Employment and Vocational Training, Issam Lahmar, announced at the same conference that Tunisia is developing a governance-framework study with the ILO covering an estimated 150,000–250,000 platform workers across roughly 60 platforms in 15 sectors — a considerably broader figure than the domestic UGTT study's narrower 60,000-worker estimate for delivery/care/moto-transport platforms specifically (see the companion Labor Law section entry). No domestic ratification instrument, implementing bill, or ARP text has been filed as of this run; watch for a ratification bill and the ILO governance-framework study's completion. (leconomistemaghrebin.com, letemps.news, both June 28–29, 2026.)
