🚢 Transport Law

Maritime Commercial Code — Law No. 62-13 of April 24, 1962(as amended) (Run 44 correction)

Enacted: April 24, 1962 | In force: July 1, 1962 | Amended: 1972, 1990, 1998, 2004-3
Run 44 correction: prior runs cited "Law No. 62-36 of March 27, 1962" — no such law could be verified, and it appears to have been a fabricated citation. NATLEX (ILO's legislative database) directly confirms the actual founding instrument as Law No. 62-13 of April 24, 1962, "portant promulgation du Code de commerce maritime," entering into force July 1, 1962; corroborated by droit-afrique.com/FAOLEX and the Ministry of Transport's own consolidated text. Comprehensive statute governing merchant shipping, maritime contracts, and maritime liability: registration/flagging of vessels, maritime liens and mortgages (ranked above ordinary privileges), carriage of goods incorporating the 1924 Brussels Convention (Hague Rules) — carrier liability capped at 400 dinars per package since a 1990 decree (up from 100). Law No. 2004-3 (January 20, 2004) replaced the ship-ownership/registration articles. Maritime ports managed by OMMP (Office de la Marine Marchande et des Ports).
TransportCommercial

Merchant Marine Organization Law — Law No. 95-33 of April 14, 1995

Enacted: April 14, 1995 | In force: 1995
Governs professions in the Tunisian merchant marine: maritime officers, engineers, and seafarers. Establishes training, certification, and competency requirements for seafarers (implementing the STCW Convention — Standards of Training, Certification and Watchkeeping). Sets working conditions, wages, and social protection for crew members. Regulates the Tunisian ship register and conditions for flagging vessels. Enforced by the OMMP and the Merchant Navy General Secretariat (created by Decree 2019-144).
TransportLabor

Maritime Ports Code — Law No. 2009-48 of July 8, 2009

Enacted: July 8, 2009 | In force: 2009 | Replaced: Law No. 99-25 (commercial ports) and Law No. 2002-47 (fishing ports)
Unifies the legal regime for Tunisia's commercial and fishing ports into a single code, repealing the previously separate 1999 commercial-ports law and 2002 fishing-ports law. Governs port administration, concessions, safety, and access; administered by OMMP (Office de la Marine Marchande et des Ports), the same authority referenced under the Maritime Commercial Code above, of which this ports-specific code had not previously had its own standalone entry.
TransportAdministrative

Civil Aviation Code — Law No. 99-58 of June 29, 1999

Enacted: June 29, 1999 | In force: 1999 | Implementing law: 98-110 (OACA)
Governs civil aviation in Tunisia: aircraft registration, airworthiness certification, pilot licensing, air navigation rules, airport operations, and air carrier liability. The Office of Civil Aviation and Airports (OACA — Office de l'Aviation Civile et des Aéroports), created by Law No. 98-110 of December 28, 1998, manages 7 international airports (Tunis-Carthage, Djerba-Zarzis, Sfax-Thyna, Monastir-Habib Bourguiba, Tozeur-Nefta, Tabarka, Gafsa-Ksar). Tunisia is an ICAO member. National carrier: Tunisair (state-owned). Domestic aviation partially opened to private carriers.
Key provisions
  • Aircraft registration: Tunisian nationality mark TS-
  • OACA: national aviation authority; safety, security, airspace management
  • Air carrier liability: aligned with Montreal Convention (ratified)
  • Airport concessions: governed by OACA and Ministry of Transport
  • Private carrier licensing: open skies principles for international routes (EU Horizontal Agreement 2021)
TransportCommercial

Road Traffic Code — Law No. 99-71 of July 26, 1999 (as amended) (Run 50 correction)

Enacted: July 26, 1999 | Adopted by Chamber of Deputies: July 6, 1999 | Published: JORT No. 61, July 30, 1999 | In force: 6 months after publication | Amended: 2001-101, 2003, 2014, 2019
Run 50 correction: prior runs cited an enactment date of "August 23, 1999" — this could not be verified, and two independent sources (jurisitetunisie.com's promulgation-law page and a matching targeted web search) instead confirm July 26, 1999 as the date of Law No. 99-71, adopted by the Chamber of Deputies on July 6, 1999 and published in JORT No. 61 of July 30, 1999; treat July 26 as the correct date absent a primary JORT text showing otherwise. Governs road traffic, vehicle registration, driver licensing, traffic offenses, and penalties in Tunisia. Sets speed limits (50 km/h urban, 90 km/h interurban, 110 km/h highways), blood alcohol limits (0.3‰ — stricter than EU), seat belt and helmet requirements, and mobile phone restrictions. Amended by Law No. 2001-101 of October 22, 2001, among later amendments; the 2014 amendment introduced a points-based driving license system and increased penalties for serious traffic offenses, and the 2019 amendment strengthened sanctions for drunk driving and speeding. Road safety authority under the Ministry of Transport. Pending reform: a "violence routière" (road violence) reform package — automatic driving-license withdrawal for speeding 10 km/h or more over the limit, expanded radar/camera enforcement, and a zero-tolerance regime — was approved as a draft bill by the Council of Ministers on July 28, 2025 (not, as sometimes reported, an already-enacted law of that date) and forwarded to the ARP; as of the most recent confirmed reporting (December 2025) it remained under ARP examination, with no JORT promulgation number identified as of June 2026.
Transport

Land Transport Law — Law No. 2004-33 of April 19, 2004

Enacted: April 19, 2004 | In force: 2004
Regulates land transport of passengers and goods in Tunisia. Governs the licensing of transport operators (buses, taxis, trucks, coaches). Establishes the Land Transport Observatory. Sets vehicle technical inspection requirements and roadworthiness standards. Regulates freight transport contracts (lettre de voiture). Governs special transport (hazardous goods, heavy loads, exceptional convoys). National carrier TRANSTU operates urban buses and metro in Tunis; regional bus networks operated by SRTG companies.
TransportCommercial

⚡ Energy & Natural Resources

Hydrocarbons Code — Law No. 99-93 of August 17, 1999 (amended 2002, 2004, 2008, 2017)

Enacted: August 17, 1999 | In force: 1999 | Last amended: Law 2017-41 of May 30, 2017
Primary legal framework for oil and gas exploration and production in Tunisia. Governs concession agreements, production sharing contracts, and service contracts. State oil company ETAP (Entreprise Tunisienne d'Activités Pétrolières) holds mandatory minimum participation. The 2017 amendment required parliamentary approval of new hydrocarbon contracts and introduced transparency obligations aligned with EITI standards. Tunisia has been an EITI member since 2016. Pending reform (added Run 70): a restricted ministerial council chaired by then-Head of Government Kamel Maddouri examined a draft law amending the Code on February 7, 2025, recommending the text be finalized (incorporating remarks raised) before submission to the full Council of Ministers, in order to open new investment areas including renewable energy alongside traditional hydrocarbon exploration. A June 26, 2026 report (Webmanagercenter) renewed calls for urgent revision amid declining domestic hydrocarbon production, and a July 3, 2026 IACE report — corroborated across African Manager, La Presse de Tunisie, and Tustex — classifies this reform among five priority codes (with Investment, Water, and Environment) "technically finalized and validated by the Council of Ministers" but "never transmitted to the ARP," meaning no bill had reached parliamentary committee as of this update. The 1999 Code, as amended through 2017, remains in force unchanged.
Key provisions
  • Prospecting permit: maximum 1 year
  • Exploration permit: maximum 5 years (with 2 extensions of 2 years each)
  • Production concession: maximum 30 years (renewable)
  • ETAP mandatory participation: minimum share in all concessions
  • CIT rate: reduced to 50% if ETAP holds ≥40% stake (vs. standard 75% otherwise)
  • Art. 59: Mandatory compliance with environmental, health and safety standards
  • 2017 amendment: parliamentary approval required for contracts; EITI alignment
EnergyCommercial

Mining Code — Law No. 2003-30 of April 28, 2003 (as amended)

Enacted: April 28, 2003 | In force: 2003 | Amended: 2010
Governs exploration and exploitation of non-hydrocarbon mineral resources (phosphates, iron ore, lead, zinc, fluorite, salt, sand, limestone). Key provisions: 3-year exploration licenses; 30-year mining concessions; 1% royalty on production value payable semi-annually; 25% CIT on mining profits with a 5-year tax holiday from production start. Enforced by the National Office of Mines (ONM — Office National des Mines). Tunisia is the world's 5th largest phosphate producer; Compagnie des Phosphates de Gafsa (CPG) is the principal public mining entity. Pending reform: the Ministry of Industry called in July 2025 for accelerated finalization of a mining code revision framed around reinforcing state sovereignty over natural resources; as of mid-2026 no revised code had been adopted.
Key provisions
  • Exploration license: 3 years, renewable
  • Mining concession: 30 years, renewable
  • Royalty: 1% of production value (semi-annual payments)
  • Tax holiday: 5 years from production commencement
  • CIT rate: 25% on mining profits
  • Environmental rehabilitation bond: mandatory for concession holders
  • Artisanal mining rights: recognized in designated areas
EnergyCommercial

Electricity and Gas Law — Law No. 62-8 of April 3, 1962 (and Law No. 2002-28 of March 25, 2002)

STEG created: April 3, 1962 | Market opening: Law 2002-28
Law 62-8 created the Société Tunisienne de l'Electricité et du Gaz (STEG) as the national monopoly utility for electricity generation, transmission, distribution, and natural gas distribution. STEG remains a state monopoly for the transmission grid and distribution network. Law No. 2002-28 (March 25, 2002) partially opened electricity generation to private Independent Power Producers (IPPs) under power purchase agreements (PPAs) with STEG, while maintaining STEG's grid monopoly. Supervised by the Ministry of Industry and Energy.
EnergyCommercial

Renewable Energy Law — Law No. 2015-12 of May 11, 2015 (amended by Law 2019-47)

Enacted: May 11, 2015 | Amended: 2019 (business climate law) | New consolidating Code transmitted to committee: April 1, 2026
Establishes three authorization regimes for electricity production from renewable sources: (1) concession regime for large projects (>30 MW); (2) authorization regime for medium projects (1–30 MW) selling to STEG under feed-in tariff PPA; (3) self-consumption regime for own-use generation. National target: 35% of electricity from renewables by 2030. The 2019 Investment Climate Law expanded self-consumption to allow inter-site wheeling by companies. ANME (National Agency for Energy Conservation) coordinates renewable energy development. Recent application: the Conseil National des Régions et des Districts (CNRD) approved, on May 13, 2026, five concession-regime agreements (TND ~1.6 billion combined) for utility-scale photovoltaic plants in Sidi Bouzid, Gafsa, and Gabès governorates — the El Khabna plant (198 MW) plus four 100 MW plants — with all output to be injected exclusively into the STEG grid under the law's concession-regime PPA framework. Pending consolidation — new "Code des Énergies Renouvelables" (added Run 94): a Council of Ministers meeting held December 24, 2024 ordered completion ("parachèvement") of a unified Renewable Energy Code intended to gather this law's dispersed provisions into a single coherent framework; deputy Yosri Baoueb confirmed on Express FM (April 1, 2026) that the draft text had just been transmitted to the ARP's competent parliamentary commission, alongside a parallel Hydrocarbons Code project, both framed as part of an "energy sovereignty" push amid rising hydrocarbon-import costs and worsening energy deficits. Its most contested provision reserves 51% of renewable-energy project ownership for Tunisian actors, justified by Baoueb as a defense against foreign consortiums outpacing domestic operators (citing the 600 MW Tunisia–Italy ElMed interconnection as a capacity-absorption risk). A July 3, 2026 IACE report separately names this Renewable Energy Code, alongside the Foreign Exchange Code and Investment Code, as one of three reforms it urges be enacted by end of 2026 — distinct from (and not one of) the four other IACE-flagged codes (Investment, Water, Environment, Hydrocarbons) already noted as "validated by the Council of Ministers but never transmitted to the ARP" elsewhere in this document; no committee vote or report has been confirmed since the April 1, 2026 transmission.
EnergyEnvironmental

📊 Public Finance & Budget

Organic Budget Law — Law No. 2019-15 of February 13, 2019

Enacted: February 13, 2019 | In force: 2019 | Replaced: Organic Law No. 67-53 of December 8, 1967 (as amended, including by Law No. 2004-42 of May 13, 2004)
Correction (Run 65): this entry previously cited Law No. 2004-42 of 2004 as the operative organic budget law; that law was in fact only an amendment to the original 1967 organic law (No. 67-53), and both have since been fully superseded by Law No. 2019-15 of February 13, 2019, confirmed against the Finance Ministry's official text. Law No. 2019-15 defines the current constitutional and legal rules for preparation, adoption, execution, and control of Tunisia's state budget, formally introducing performance/program-based budgeting (budgétisation par objectifs) alongside the traditional Title I/Title II expenditure classification, plus multi-year budget programming and expanded rules for execution control, results evaluation, and the budget settlement law (loi de règlement). Organizes the annual Finance Law (loi de finances) process: Cabinet submits draft to parliament by October 15; parliament must adopt by December 31.
Key provisions
  • Finance Law submitted to parliament by October 15 each year
  • Parliamentary vote required by December 31; if not, prior-year budget extended by decree
  • Title I: recurrent expenditures (staff, goods, services, debt service)
  • Title II: development expenditures (public investment)
  • Special accounts (comptes spéciaux du Trésor) regulated separately
  • Court of Accounts certifies final state accounts (loi de règlement)
AdministrativeFinancial

Court of Accounts — Law No. 68-8 of March 8, 1968 (as amended, most recently Law 2019-56)

Enacted: March 8, 1968 | In force: 1968 | Major reform: Law 2019-56
Tunisia's supreme audit institution (Cour des Comptes / ديوان المحاسبات). Audits central government, public enterprises, and local authority accounts. Issues binding rulings (arrêts) imposing financial liability (débet) on public accountants for unauthorized expenditures. Issues annual public report on state budget execution presented to parliament. Audit opinions required before adoption of final budget accounts (loi de règlement). The 2019 reform modernized audit methods and strengthened transparency of findings. Also plays a role in asset declaration review. Update (Run 103): the Court's 32nd annual report, presented to President Kaïs Saïed on January 9, 2026 by First Vice-President Fadhila Gargouri, found roughly TND 1.07 billion in cumulative losses across eleven audited public entities — national carrier Tunisair alone (over TND 316 million) and the Office of Maritime Commerce and Ports (nearly TND 291 million) among the largest — attributing them to salaries, bonuses, and allowances paid without legal basis and weakened internal-control mechanisms; Saied publicly demanded prosecutions and recovery of the funds.
AdministrativeFinancial

Public Debt Management — Law No. 97-42 of July 28, 1997 and annual Finance Laws

Framework law: July 28, 1997 | Debt limits: set annually in Finance Law
Governs the state's borrowing authority and public debt management. The General Treasury of Tunisia manages the debt portfolio. External borrowing requires parliamentary authorization in the annual Finance Law. Domestic instruments: Treasury bills (BTC — 13/26/52 weeks), medium-term Treasury bonds (OAT). Tunisia has issued Eurobonds and Sukuk (Islamic bonds) on international capital markets (2012, 2015, 2020, 2021). IMF extended credit facility (2016–2020, revised 2023). Public debt reached approximately 80% of GDP by 2024.
FinancialAdministrative

Presidential Decree No. 2026-108Afreximbank US$500 Million Financing Facility

BCT–Afreximbank facility signed: June 16, 2026 | Presidential decree published: JORT, June 8, 2026 | BCT board approval: March 18, 2026
Under the Public Debt Management framework (Law No. 97-42) and the external-financing authorization in the Finance Law 2026, the Central Bank of Tunisia (BCT), acting on the state's behalf, concluded a US$500 million (≈TND 1.471 billion) financing facility with the African Export-Import Bank (Afreximbank) to help cover the FY2026 budget's external financing needs, including strategic imports of energy and food products. Signed by President Kais Saied as Presidential Decree No. 2026-108, ratifying the BCT board's March 2026 decision. Part of a broader financing relationship under which Afreximbank has extended roughly TND 5.15 billion to Tunisia over four years.
FinancialAdministrative

Draft Laws n° 38/2026 & n° 39/2026 (pending)State Guarantees for STEG IBRD Loans (~US$430 Million)

Underlying loan agreements signed: November 3, 2025 | Finance & Budget Committee approval: June 28, 2026 | Status: awaiting plenary vote as of July 2, 2026
Two companion government bills authorizing state guarantees for International Bank for Reconstruction and Development (IBRD/World Bank) loans to the Société Tunisienne de l'Électricité et du Gaz (STEG). Draft Law n° 38/2026 guarantees a US$400 million loan extended directly to STEG; Draft Law n° 39/2026 guarantees a complementary US$30 million loan channeled through the IBRD in its capacity as implementing entity of the Clean Technology Fund (CTF). Both finance a STEG energy-sector modernization program aimed at improving the utility's operational efficiency, financial performance, and governance, against a backdrop of STEG's reported TND 7.356 billion total indebtedness and over TND 6 billion in unpaid receivables (per a June 27, 2026 La Presse report). The ARP Finance and Budget Committee approved both texts on June 28, 2026; they were transmitted to the ARP plenary for final adoption but had not been put to a plenary vote as of this update. Distinct from the unrelated "combating financial exclusion" bill (Draft Law n° 023/2024 — see entry below), which some secondary sources conflated with these same bill numbers; the 38/2026 and 39/2026 numbering is independently confirmed for the STEG guarantees via the ARP's official bill registry and Arabic-language press (لجنة المالية، مشروع قانون عدد 38 و39 لسنة 2026).
FinancialEnergyPending

2026–2030 National Development Plan (Bill)

Filed under Constitution Arts. 75 & 77 | ARP standing-committee examination: June 29 – July 2, 2026 (concluded) | Status: pending plenary vote as of July 3, 2026
The economic and social planning framework law for 2026–2030, formally deposited at the ARP as draft law n°42/2026 on June 17, 2026 following its adoption by the Council of Ministers. Structured as a single-article bill — the Assembly votes yes/no/abstain on the plan as a whole rather than amending individual provisions, per committee rapporteur Saber Jlassi — it was examined jointly by all Assembly of People's Representatives standing committees beginning June 29–30, 2026. Committee discussion noted the prior five-year plan's execution rate stood at under 40%, a benchmark cited by critical deputies alongside the new plan's more ambitious targets. Targets raising the average annual growth rate to 4.2% over the period (versus 2.4% in the prior five-year period), reaching 5.1% by 2030, and raising investment from roughly 15.5% to 20% of GDP via a public-investment project portfolio of approximately TND 102 billion. A June 30, 2026 joint hearing with Minister of Economy and Planning Samir Abdelhafidh preceded committee deliberations, followed by a second joint ARP audition of the Minister on July 1, 2026 to gather further committee input (Tunisian media noted the plan's limited treatment of the media/audiovisual sector as a specific point raised in that session, and a July 1, 2026 report noted several deputies voicing doubts about the feasibility of the plan's growth and investment targets, with the Finance Committee separately flagging concerns over the underlying economic and financial assumptions). Separately on July 1, 2026, the National Union of Tunisian Journalists (SNJT) publicly criticized the plan by name for omitting a dedicated media-sector strategy, calling for the assembly to formally recognize media as an economic and cultural sector, incorporate press-freedom legislation, and include the sector in the plan's monitoring mechanisms. The plan's headline targets include lowering the poverty rate to under 15%, a portfolio of roughly 21,100 public-investment projects funded at approximately TND 102 billion, and raising the renewable-energy share of electricity production to 35% by 2030; committee coverage also noted that of 40,748 development proposals submitted by local councils, 34,715 were retained in the plan's regional components. Once adopted, it is expected to serve as the legal reference framework underpinning the national AI strategy and the NDC 3.0 climate financing plan already noted elsewhere in this document. Update (Run 71): ARP President Ibrahim Bouderbala announced July 2, 2026 that the joint standing-committee examination had formally concluded — independently reported by both Le Temps News and African Manager (both July 3, 2026) — with a consolidated synthesis report now being drafted for submission to the ARP Bureau, which will schedule the plenary session before the text proceeds to the Conseil National des Régions et des Districts under Art. 77; no plenary date has yet been announced. Bouderbala used the same occasion to publicly call for accelerating adoption of the separately-tracked Foreign Exchange, Water, Hydrocarbons, and Investment codes (see those entries). Not yet enacted — the most active legislative process in Tunisia as of this update.
FinancialConstitutionalPending

🌾 Agriculture & Fisheries

Fisheries Code — Law No. 94-13 of January 31, 1994 (as amended 2002, 2014)

Enacted: January 31, 1994 | In force: 1994 | Amended: 2002, 2014
Comprehensive statute governing fishing activities in Tunisian territorial waters and the EEZ. Commercial fishing vessels require licenses from the Ministry of Agriculture. Sets fishing quotas, prohibited fishing methods (dynamite, poison, trawling within 3 nm of coast), protected marine species, and seasonal closures. Establishes the National Fisheries Office (ONP — Office National des Pêches) for market organization and port management. Tuna and swordfish fishing regulated under ICCAT commitments. Aquaculture (fish farming) licensing governed by supplementary 2006 decree.
AgricultureEnvironmental

Plant Health Law — Law No. 99-24 of February 23, 1999; Veterinary Law — Law No. 2005-35 of May 25, 2005

Plant Health: February 23, 1999 | Veterinary: May 25, 2005
The Plant Health Law governs protection of crops from pests and diseases, phytosanitary border inspections, and pesticide registration and use (administered by INAP — National Institute for Plant Protection). The Veterinary Law regulates animal health surveillance, veterinary practice licensing, slaughterhouse standards, and control of notifiable animal diseases. Both laws implement Tunisia's WTO Agreement on Sanitary and Phytosanitary Measures (SPS Agreement) obligations for agricultural trade.
AgricultureHealth

Agricultural Development Law — Law No. 71-72 of December 31, 1971 and Law No. 2000-30 of March 20, 2000

Development Areas: 1971 | Irrigated Perimeters: March 20, 2000
The 1971 law empowers the Ministry of Agriculture to designate agricultural development areas with state support for irrigation and land improvement. The 2000 law governs management of public irrigated perimeters (périmètres irrigués publics — PIPs): water pricing through GDA user associations (Groupements de Développement Agricole), infrastructure maintenance, and cost recovery. Emergency water restrictions (2023 drought) were issued under the Water Code's provisions with inter-ministerial coordination.
AgricultureLand

Organic Agriculture Law — Law No. 99-30 of April 5, 1999

Enacted: April 5, 1999 | In force: 1999 | Amended: 2009
Establishes the legal framework for organic farming, certification, labeling, and inspection in Tunisia. Organic products must be certified by an accredited certification body and labeled with the national organic mark. Controls use of synthetic pesticides, fertilizers, and GMOs on certified organic land. Administered by the Ministry of Agriculture and the Office for Organic Farming Development (BIO). Tunisia exports significant volumes of certified organic olive oil and dates to EU markets.
AgricultureCommercial

Law No. 67-41 of June 24, 1967 (as amended)Agricultural Cooperatives

Enacted: June 24, 1967 | In force: 1967 | Amended: 1982, 2001, 2020
Establishes the legal framework for agricultural cooperatives (coopératives agricoles) in Tunisia. Agricultural cooperatives are governed by a hybrid regime combining principles from general cooperative law and specific agricultural provisions. Types include: production cooperatives, service cooperatives (input supply, marketing), and credit cooperatives (Caisses de Crédit Agricole Mutuels). Registration with the Ministry of Agriculture and the Office of Agricultural Credit Cooperatives (ODCO) is required. Cooperatives benefit from tax exemptions on profits and reduced registration fees. The Tunisian Cooperative Union (UTAP — Union Tunisienne de l'Agriculture et de la Pêche) represents cooperatives at national level. The 2020 amendment strengthened governance requirements and introduced digital voting for cooperative assemblies.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–10: Definition of agricultural cooperative; democratic governance (one member, one vote)
  • Art. 11–20: Types: production, marketing, supply, credit cooperatives
  • Art. 21–28: Registration requirements; statutes; minimum capital
  • Art. 29–36: Distribution of surplus to members proportional to participation
  • Art. 37–44: Tax advantages: exemption from corporate tax; reduced stamp duties
  • Art. 45–52: Dissolution and liquidation procedures
AgricultureCommercial

Law No. 2001-94 of August 7, 2001Pastoral and Communal Lands Code

Enacted: August 7, 2001 | In force: 2001
Regulates the use and management of collective pastoral lands (terres collectives de parcours) and communal grazing areas in Tunisia, particularly relevant in the arid and semi-arid regions of central and southern Tunisia. These lands — covering approximately 5 million hectares — are traditionally managed by tribal communities. The code establishes a management framework balancing customary pastoral rights with sustainable land management obligations. Creates Pastoral Development Associations (associations de développement pastoral) for each defined rangeland unit. Prohibits unauthorized subdivision, sale, or permanent agricultural conversion of collective pastoral lands without Ministry of Agriculture approval. Provides mechanisms for resolving boundary disputes between pastoral communities.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–8: Definition of collective pastoral lands; state ownership with usufruct rights to communities
  • Art. 9–16: Pastoral Development Associations (PDA) — formation, governance, mandate
  • Art. 17–24: Seasonal grazing circuits; carrying capacity limits per zone
  • Art. 25–30: Prohibition of unauthorized permanent crop installation; penalties
  • Art. 31–36: Water point management in pastoral zones
  • Art. 37–42: Dispute resolution between communities; role of Ministry of Agriculture
AgricultureLand

Law No. 2009-11 of February 11, 2009Food Safety and Quality Control

Enacted: February 11, 2009 | In force: July 2009 | Implementing decrees: 2010–2012
Framework law on food safety and quality control throughout the food chain — from primary production to retail. Establishes risk-based approach to food controls, aligned with Codex Alimentarius standards. Creates the National Authority for Sanitary Control and the Environment for Products (ANCSEP — Agence Nationale de Contrôle Sanitaire et Environnemental des Produits). Mandates HACCP (Hazard Analysis and Critical Control Points) systems for food businesses above a certain scale. Requires mandatory traceability of food products. Imposes pre-market authorization for food additives, colorings, and flavorings. Regulates import controls on food products at port of entry, including veterinary and phytosanitary inspections.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–8: Scope and definitions; farm-to-fork principle
  • Art. 9–16: ANCSEP mandate; inspection powers; laboratory network
  • Art. 17–24: Mandatory HACCP for medium and large food businesses
  • Art. 25–32: Food labeling requirements: ingredients, allergens, origin, expiry date
  • Art. 33–38: Import controls; border inspection posts; phytosanitary certificates
  • Art. 39–44: Market surveillance; product recall procedures; rapid alert system
  • Art. 45–50: Administrative sanctions; criminal penalties for dangerous food (up to 5 years)
AgricultureHealthCommercial

Law No. 2026-5 of March 11, 2026Regularization of Distressed Agricultural Debts

Adopted by ARP: March 10, 2026 | Promulgated: March 11, 2026 | Provisions apply through: December 31, 2026
Requires public and private banks to reschedule the full principal and initial contractual interest of classified/distressed agricultural debts (BCT class 4 and above, held before September 30, 2025) owed by farmers and agricultural companies, over up to 7 years including a 1-year grace period. Mandates automatic removal of the resulting negative banking classification once a rescheduling agreement is approved, restoring debtors' access to new financing. The Central Bank of Tunisia (BCT) was required to issue implementing conditions within 15 days of publication. Excludes debts connected to unresolved corruption or money-laundering proceedings. Enacted to address accumulated financial distress in the agricultural sector that had left many farmers and agri-businesses unable to settle liabilities or obtain new credit.
AgricultureFinancial

🏛️ Tourism & Cultural Heritage

Tourism Organization Law — Law No. 2012-49 of July 2, 2012 (Code du Tourisme)

Enacted: July 2, 2012 | In force: 2012 | Replaced: Decree-Laws No. 73-3 & 73-4 of October 3, 1973 (ratified by Laws No. 73-58 & 73-59 of November 19, 1973) and their implementing classification Decree No. 73-510 of October 30, 1973
Governs all tourism activities in Tunisia: classification and licensing of hotels (1–5 stars), travel agencies, tour operators, holiday villages, and tourist guides. Hotels classified by the Ministry of Tourism with annual inspection. The National Tourism Office (ONTT — Office National du Tourisme Tunisien) promotes tourism internationally. Investment incentives for hotel renovation under the Investment Law 2016. Tourism zone master plans govern coastal development. Replaced the sector's original 1973 framework — Decree-Law 73-3 (ratified by Law 73-58) controlled tourist-establishment management/operation, Decree-Law 73-4 (ratified by Law 73-59) controlled their construction, and Decree No. 73-510 set hotel classification standards (itself amended in 1983 and 1986, later updated by Decree No. 2007-457 of March 6, 2007, prior to this 2012 Code's full recodification). 2025: Draft Law No. 93/2025 proposed 30% social tourism quota, generating hotel federation opposition.
TourismCommercial

Cultural Heritage Protection Law — Law No. 94-35 of February 24, 1994

Enacted: February 24, 1994 | In force: 1994 | Amended: 2013
Classifies and protects Tunisia's movable and immovable cultural heritage: archaeological sites, historic monuments (medinas, mosques, ribats, Roman amphitheaters), traditional crafts, and museum collections. Unauthorized excavation, construction near classified sites, and export of antiquities are criminal offenses. The National Heritage Institute (INP — Institut National du Patrimoine) manages classified sites. Tunisia has 8 UNESCO World Heritage Sites (Carthage, Medina of Tunis, El Jem Amphitheatre, Dougga, Ichkeul National Park, Kerkouane, Kairouan, Sousse Medina). Implements the 1972 UNESCO World Heritage Convention (ratified 1975).
Key provisions
  • Classification of monuments, sites, and historic districts by ministerial order
  • Protection zones (périmètres de sauvegarde) around classified sites
  • Prohibition on unauthorized excavation — criminal penalties
  • Export of classified antiquities prohibited; unlawful export criminalized
  • INP manages museums and archaeological parks
  • International cooperation with UNESCO, ICOMOS, and bilateral partners
TourismEnvironmental

Crafts Law — Law No. 2009-25 of May 23, 2009 (Loi relative à l'artisanat)

Enacted: May 23, 2009 | In force: 2009
Defines and protects traditional Tunisian crafts (artisanat). Establishes the National Office of Tunisian Crafts (ONAT — Office National de l'Artisanat Tunisien). Creates the "artisan" professional status with access to specific microfinancing (BTS loans). Protects geographical craft designations (e.g., "Tapis de Kairouan," "Poterie de Nabeul," "Chéchia de Tunis"). Quality certification and export promotion administered by ONAT. The crafts sector employs over 300,000 artisans and is a significant contributor to tourism revenue.
TourismCommercial

Cinema and Cultural Industries — Law No. 93-40 of April 26, 1993 and Ministerial Orders

Cinema framework: April 26, 1993 | Ongoing regulatory updates | Reform bill: Proposition de loi n° 2024-03, committee review ongoing as of June 2026
Governs Tunisian cinema production, distribution, and exhibition. Establishes the Centre National du Cinéma et de l'Image (CNCI) which administers production subsidies, co-production agreements, and film classification. Tunisia maintains an active film industry with international co-productions. The broader cultural sector is governed by the Ministry of Cultural Affairs, which funds the national theater (TNT), opera (OCT), symphonic orchestra, and public libraries network. Cultural associations are registered under Decree 88 of 2011. Pending reform: Proposition de loi n° 2024-03, a dedicated statute to organize, structure, and develop the cinema industry, has been under review by the ARP's Tourism, Culture, Services, and Handicrafts Committee. Correction: the June 24, 2026 session was described in committee reporting as "continuing the study" (mouwasalat dirasa) of the bill rather than a completed article-by-article finalization as previously tracked; no committee report has been confirmed as finalized. Not yet referred to plenary as of this run. Update (Run 39): independent of this pending reform, the sector's existing subsidy mechanism was reinforced through ordinary budget legislation — Article 61 of the Finance Law 2026 (Law No. 2025-17, see Tax & Fiscal section) earmarks approximately TND 100 million per year for the CNCI's production-support fund, a concrete funding increase that took effect January 1, 2026 ahead of, and separate from, the still-pending organizational reform bill above.
TourismPress

Law No. 2026-8 of May 13, 2026Status of the Artist and Artistic Professions

Adopted by the ARP and published in JORT: May 13, 2026 | In force: 2026
New law defining and regulating the legal status of artists and artistic professions, adopted after more than a decade of debate and lobbying by cultural-sector unions — entirely absent from this artifact despite 39 prior research runs and the existing cinema-industry entry above covering the adjacent film-subsidy framework. Establishes formal recognition and professional-card registration for artists across disciplines, and notably extends recognition to digital-arts practitioners and questions of AI-assisted creative work — one of the only Tunisian statutes to date touching generative-AI authorship issues, albeit narrowly, within the cultural sector rather than as general AI regulation (compare the still-pending Personal Data Protection & AI bill in the Digital & ICT section, which remains the primary vehicle under consideration for broader AI governance).
AdministrativeLaborTourism

Law No. 88-95 of August 2, 1988 (as amended)National Archives, Libraries, and Documentary Heritage

Enacted: August 2, 1988 | In force: 1989 | Amended: 2002, 2018
Establishes the legal framework for the Tunisian national archives, public libraries, and documentary heritage preservation. Creates the National Archives of Tunisia (Archives Nationales de Tunisie — ANT) as the authority responsible for collecting, preserving, and providing access to historical state archives. Mandates transfer of public documents from ministries and agencies to the ANT after statutory retention periods (typically 30 years for classified documents). Establishes the National Library of Tunisia (Bibliothèque Nationale de Tunisie — BNT), receiving legal deposit copies of all publications printed in Tunisia (2 copies of each work). Governs public library network (bibliothèques de lecture publique) in each governorate. The 2018 amendment introduced digital archiving standards and authorized digitization programs for rare manuscripts, especially the Zeitouna manuscript collection (one of the largest Islamic manuscript collections in North Africa).
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–8: National Archives mandate; ANT organizational structure under Ministry of Culture
  • Art. 9–16: Document retention schedules; mandatory transfer to ANT after retention period
  • Art. 17–22: Public access to archives: 30-year rule for ordinary documents; 60-year rule for sensitive documents; 100-year rule for personal data
  • Art. 23–28: Legal deposit obligation for all Tunisian publishers (books, periodicals, maps, audiovisual)
  • Art. 29–34: National Library structure; special collections (Arabo-Islamic manuscripts)
  • Art. 35–40: Public library network; municipal libraries under regional cultural authorities
  • 2018 amendment: Digital preservation strategy; mass digitization of fragile and rare items
TourismEducation

🏆 Sport & Youth

Sport Law — Organic Law No. 95-11 of February 6, 1995(as amended) (Run 44 correction)

Original: February 6, 1995 | Amended: reportedly 2004 and 2006 (grew from 24 to 28 articles)
Run 44 correction: prior runs cited an amendment by "Law No. 2014-17 of April 14, 2014" — no such law could be verified in any source searched, and it appears to have been a fabricated citation; drop it rather than repeat it. Multiple independent secondary sources (a university thesis on Tunisian sports associations, kapitalis.com) instead describe the law as "modified and supplemented on 3 occasions, once in 2004 and a second time in 2006," growing from 24 to 28 articles — the exact JORT numbers for these amendments could not be confirmed from available sources and should be verified against a primary text before being cited as fact. Governs the organization of sport in Tunisia: national sports federations (a federation groups specialized associations within one or more disciplines), sports clubs, ministerial model-statutes and dissolution power over mismanaged federation boards, minimum 20% of public subsidies earmarked for youth training, and the amateur/non-amateur (professional) practice framework. Complemented by Law No. 94-104 of August 3, 1994 (physical-education and sports-activity organization: national sports council, Comité Olympique Tunisien, school/university and disabled-sports federations), which itself replaced Law No. 84-63 of 1984. Tunisia adheres to the World Anti-Doping Code; the Tunisian Anti-Doping Agency (LTDLA) is the national anti-doping authority.
SportAdministrative

Youth Policy — Law No. 2002-51 of May 13, 2002 and National Youth Strategy 2022

Framework law: May 13, 2002 | National Youth Strategy: 2022
Framework governing youth policy, youth centers (maisons de jeunes — over 300 nationwide), summer camp programs, and youth civic engagement initiatives. The Ministry of Youth and Sports coordinates implementation. Tunisia has a young population (median age ~31); youth unemployment remains a structural challenge (30%+ rate for 15–24). The 2022 National Youth Strategy targets youth entrepreneurship, digital skills, combating irregular emigration ("harraga"), and volunteer service. The Startup Act (2018) is the principal legislative tool for youth entrepreneurship.
SportSocial

Anti-Doping Regulations — Law No. 2015-50 of December 21, 2015

Enacted: December 21, 2015 | In force: 2016
Establishes Tunisia's national anti-doping legal framework, implementing the World Anti-Doping Code (WADA). Creates the Tunisian Anti-Doping Control Agency (LTDLA — Laboratoire Tunisien de Contrôle du Dopage et des Analyses). Mandates testing programs for national and international competitions, out-of-competition testing, and therapeutic use exemptions (TUEs). Sets sanctions aligned with WADA rules: 2-year ban for first offense, 4 years for aggravated violation. Implements UNESCO International Convention against Doping in Sport (ratified 2006).
SportCriminal

Draft Organic Law on Sports Structures (pending)

Approved in principle: Council of Ministers, November 29, 2025 | Under review: ARP Education, Vocational Training, Scientific Research, Youth & Sports Commission (since March 5, 2026) | Status: not yet enacted as of July 2, 2026
A government-drafted organic law, reported at 211 articles across 7 chapters, that would repeal and replace the Sport Law (Organic Law No. 1995-11 of February 6, 1995, above). Presented to the Council of Ministers on November 29, 2025 by Youth and Sports Minister Sadok Mourali, under Prime Minister Sarra Zaafrani Zenzri, after revision based on inter-ministerial and comparative-law input. Stated objectives: democratizing governance of sports structures, guaranteeing transparency, modernizing sports-club management, and improving financing mechanisms and the sustainability of public sports services. Reported provisions include Art. 13 (limiting federation/club executive-bureau officers to three terms) and Art. 120 (authorizing conversion of sports clubs into professional sports companies). Under examination by the ARP's Education, Vocational Training, Scientific Research, Youth and Sports Commission since a March 5, 2026 session, with expert hearings on sports law and sports arbitration continuing into spring 2026. At a May 15, 2026 hearing, sports-law experts (Fethi Mouldi, Maher Snoussi) publicly warned the draft grants the supervising ministry excessive powers — including appointing members of clubs' ethics/governance boards and an approval right over international representation — that could conflict with international sports-body autonomy requirements (risking non-recognition of Tunisian federations) despite the bill's stated "independence" objective; concerns were also raised about compliance burdens on small amateur clubs. Update (Run 30): following further expert testimony (including the Tunisian Olympic Committee, the Paralympic Committee, and a sports-law association), the commission has moved past the hearing stage into drafting its committee report — a modest procedural step beyond the May 15 hearing checkpoint — though no committee vote or plenary date has been confirmed. Not yet enacted as of this run.
SportPending

Proposition de loi n° 83/2025 (pending)Combating Violence in and around Stadiums

Filed: July 2025 | Under review: Education Commission (ARP) | Status: not yet enacted as of July 1, 2026
A parliamentary bill creating a dedicated criminal framework for violence at sporting events, filling a gap left by the Sport Law (No. 1995-11) and the general Penal Code. Covers acts of violence in stadiums, sports facilities, and surrounding gathering areas nationwide. Proposes: 6 months' to 5 years' imprisonment for acts of violence at sporting events; a minimum 5,000 TND fine for throwing projectiles or using flares/pyrotechnics; stadium bans of at least 5 years (life for repeat offenders); and 5 to 10 years' imprisonment for carrying or using weapons or dangerous objects at sports events. Would create a national committee to monitor stadium violence, with representatives from the Ministry of Youth and Sports, the Ministry of the Interior, the Tunisian Football Federation, civil-society associations, and supporters' groups. Under review by the ARP's Education Commission as of mid-2026; not yet enacted.
SportCriminalPending

A single-source claim that the ARP Security Commission was considering a "whistleblower reward" bill was re-encountered and, consistent with the Run 71 finding, correctly withheld as an already-investigated denied rumor. No new standalone entries were added and no existing entry required substantive revision (263 total remains accurate), but a structural audit caught a stat-counter bug: Run 94's entry-merge had correctly reduced the actual card count to 263 while leaving the header's "Laws Documented" figure at the pre-merge value of 264 — corrected here. The July 8, 11, and 13, 2026 dates still had not arrived as of this run's cutoff and remain the highest-priority triggers for the next run. Runs 96 through 101 closed further narrow gaps outside the pending-bill watchlist while re-confirming the watchlist unchanged across each run's cutoff (see the v4.96–v4.101 changelog notes above for specifics, including Run 99's new Digital Platform Workers/ILO Convention 193 entries and Run 101's "affaire de l'appareil secret" update). Run 102 re-confirmed the Foreign Exchange Code, Personal Data Protection/AI bill, and Development Plan bill watchlist items unchanged, re-verified all three trigger dates (July 8, 11, 13, 2026) as still not yet arrived, and investigated four candidate leads (a Tataouine municipal-waste-company wage dispute; STEG/World Bank and Tunisia–Oman/Kuwait bilateral-agreement committee referrals) that were each found to fall below this project's inclusion threshold — ordinary labor unrest without an accompanying bill, or routine treaty ratifications not yet enacted — and were correctly left undocumented (see the v4.102 changelog note above). No new gaps were closed this run; 265 total remains accurate. The July 8, 11, and 13, 2026 dates remain the highest-priority triggers for the next run. Run 103 cross-checked ten suspected coverage-gap candidates surfaced by a fresh research pass (Organic Budget Law, Social & Solidarity Economy Law, Tourism Law, Sports Law, Exchange Control Law, Leasing Law, Crowdfunding Law, a "Code Rural," Vocational Training Law, Public Accounting Code) directly against the live artifact and found eight already accurately captured, one ("Code Rural") correctly confirmed not to exist, and one — the Crowdfunding entry — carrying a fabricated citation ("Decree-Law No. 2020-170") corrected to the verified Law No. 2020-37 of August 6, 2020 (see the v4.103 changelog note above). It also closed a genuine gap entirely outside the pending-bill watchlist: a wholly new standalone Affaire Taïeb Rached — "Cassations Sans Renvoi" entry (Judicial Organization section) documenting the first prosecution of a Court of Cassation president in Tunisian history, missed by all 102 prior runs, plus refinements to the Court of Accounts entry (the Court's January 2026 32nd annual report) and the Entry and Residence of Foreigners entry (a July 2, 2026 Amnesty International report quantifying the UNHCR-registration suspension's effect). No watchlist item crossed into a plenary vote, enactment, or committee-report conclusion since Run 102's same-day cutoff. The July 8, 11, and 13, 2026 dates remain the highest-priority triggers for the next run. Run 104 re-confirmed the Foreign Exchange Code and Constitutional Court organic law unchanged and closed no new gaps beyond a refinement noting the RNE's July 2, 2026 mandatory-online-filing announcement on the Commercial Register Law entry (see the v4.104 changelog note above). Run 105 likewise found no JORT-published legislation dated after Run 104's cutoff and no watchlist-item movement, and closed no new gaps — a targeted structural gap-scan of nine further regulatory areas (consumer credit/microfinance, Competition Council, INPDP's own statute, ANSI/cybersecurity, Code des Collectivités Locales, Consumer Protection Law, e-signature law, Arbitration Code, Insurance Code) confirmed each is already covered by an existing entry (see the v4.105 changelog note above). The Foreign Exchange Code committee's own "Monday" meeting — now dated July 6, 2026 — joins the July 8, 11, and 13, 2026 dates as the nearest-term, highest-priority triggers for the next run to check first. Run 106 re-confirmed the watchlist unchanged (the July 6 committee meeting had not yet occurred as of its cutoff) and closed one further gap entirely outside the pending-bill watchlist: a wholly new standalone Passports & Travel Documents Law entry (Law No. 75-40 of 1975 — see Nationality & Immigration section), documenting Articles 38/39/41's border-crossing offenses and their use against Tunisian Council for Refugees founder Mustapha Djemali and colleague Abderrazek Krimi, a case missed by all 105 prior runs (see the v4.106 changelog note above). Run 107 re-confirmed all twenty-one tracked watchlist items unchanged via a fresh WebSearch-only sweep (Tavily still returned HTTP 402) and closed no new gaps — every lead surfaced, including the Hmaidi/Smara appellate rulings and the biologists' union's June 30 suspension vote, was independently verified already captured at equal or greater precision by existing entries (see the v4.107 changelog note above). Run 108 likewise re-confirmed the watchlist unchanged and found that none of the July 6, 8, 11, or 13, 2026 trigger dates had yet occurred as of its cutoff; every candidate lead surfaced (the Development Plan's committee-review completion, the Investment Code's continuing hearings, and the Hmaidi/Smara/Mabrouk/Chahed/Ben Gharbia/Turki/Mekacher July 1–3 appellate rulings) was independently verified already captured at equal or greater precision, including the Ben Gharbia acquittal-on-appeal detail (see the v4.108 changelog note above). Run 109 confirmed the stadium-violence bill (n° 83/2025) remains unchanged and already fully captured, but closed two genuine gaps outside this watchlist: a wholly new plastic-bag reduction bill (see standalone Environmental Law entry — filed the same day as this run's cutoff, so its committee-referral status is a new item to watch) and Article 83 of the already-enacted Finance Law 2026's Tunisians-residing-abroad tax/customs benefits, previously undocumented despite the artifact's existing separate entry for the National Council for Tunisians Residing Abroad's own reform bill (see the v4.109 changelog note above). The July 6, 8, 11, and 13, 2026 dates remain the highest-priority triggers for the next run, in that order, alongside checking whether the new plastic-bag bill has been referred to committee. Run 110, researching on the same calendar day as Run 109's cutoff, confirmed none of those four dates had yet occurred and closed no new gaps — a 26-search sweep found every watchlist item's status unchanged and no new JORT-published legislation, court ruling, or treaty ratification (see the v4.110 changelog note above); the recurring "Personal Data Protection enforcement began July 11" marketing false positive was again correctly withheld. Run 111, still within the same July 5, 2026 window, likewise found the watchlist unchanged and no trigger date yet arrived, discarded an Algeria/Tunisia cinema-law mislabeling trap and an unresolved Investment Code voting-status conflict rather than force either into the record, and closed one narrow implementation-level gap outside the watchlist — a July 1, 2026 "zero paper" administrative-digitalization milestone added to the Electronic Procedures in Administrative Transactions entry (see the v4.111 changelog note above). Run 112, also within the same July 5, 2026 window, re-confirmed the watchlist unchanged (JORT’s own index still shows no July 2026 issues published) and discarded a Forestry Code database-timestamp trap, confirming the Mining Code and Forestry Code were both already covered rather than genuine gaps; it closed one narrow refinement outside the watchlist — a January 2026 FTDES "Forests and Climate"/REDD+-financing reform-advocacy note added to the Forestry Code entry (see the v4.112 changelog note above). The following pending items are tracked within their relevant sections above and should be watched for resolution (enactment, plenary vote, or abandonment) in future runs, rather than duplicated here:
  • The new Foreign Exchange Code (Draft Law 115/2025, 91 articles) — committee chair Maher Ktari has called continued Central Bank of Tunisia consultation "essential" before final adoption (per a June 23, 2026 report), with the BCT still not having formally responded as of that date; a July 1, 2026 report on the separate Development Plan debate found deputies still publicly urging "acceleration" of this Code's adoption, so Ktari's pre-recess target should be treated as unconfirmed rather than on track. The related exchange-violations amnesty bill (No. 58/2025 — see standalone entry) remains with the Finance Committee with reporting suggesting the two tracks are being harmonized; watch for whether they merge into a single text or advance separately.
  • The 2026–2030 National Development Plan bill — debate continued July 1, 2026 with a second audition of Economy Minister Samir Abdelhafidh, at which the plan's 40,748 proposed projects and feasibility/financing assumptions drew renewed deputy skepticism; text now transmitted to all permanent committees for joint review before a synthesis report goes to the ARP Bureau to schedule the plenary vote, then transmission to the Conseil National des Régions et des Districts.
  • The pending Personal Data Protection & AI organic bill and the Associations Decree reform — both remain under active ARP Rights & Freedoms Commission review with no confirmed outcome to the lapsed June 24, 2026 report deadline; as of a June 4, 2026 update, the commission was still awaiting written ministry opinions on data protection, and on associations was awaiting government feedback with civil-society hearings still unscheduled — status essentially stalled since Run 38. Note: the Data Protection bill's article count is reported inconsistently across primary sources (132 vs. 123 articles) and remains unresolved — do not "correct" one figure to the other without a primary JORT/ARP text. Separately (Run 39 addition), see the civil-society-suspensions entry below for Amnesty International/HRW reporting on ~25 associations administratively suspended since mid-2025 under the still-unreformed 2011 decree.
  • The Draft Organic Law on Audiovisual Communication (99 articles, filed February 25, 2025, to replace HAICA) — no plenary movement confirmed; the May 20, 2026 jurisdictional dispute between the Rights & Freedoms Commission and the General Legislation Committee over which body hears the bill remains the last confirmed status, with no independent confirmation that the announced June 2026 hearings occurred.
  • The Investment Code reform bill — approved in principle by the Restricted Ministerial Council on December 31, 2025; the ARP Strategic Planning Commission (chaired by Saber Jelassi) remained at the article-by-article voting stage as of a May 31, 2026 report, with no committee vote or referral to plenary confirmed since. (Added Run 70) A July 3, 2026 IACE report separately classifies this reform as "validated by the Council of Ministers but never transmitted to the ARP" — possibly describing the government's own track as distinct from the committee-stage MP-initiated proposition n°26/2024 noted in the standalone entry; not yet reconciled.
  • A reported but largely unconfirmed new Code de l'eau (Water Code) replacement — its seven-chapter structure is now sourced to Minister Ezzeddine Ben Cheikh's February 25, 2025 Conseil ministériel restreint presentation (traced Run 51), but no draft has been filed with the ARP and no publicly available draft text exists as of this run; the 1975 Code (Law No. 75-16) remains the operative statute. (Sharpened Run 70) now corroborated across three July 3, 2026 sources as "validated by the Council of Ministers but never transmitted to the ARP" — an executive-stage bottleneck, not a drafting one.
  • (Added Run 70) The Hydrocarbons Code amendment (see standalone Law No. 99-93 entry) — examined by a restricted ministerial council February 7, 2025, renewed as an "urgent" priority by a June 26, 2026 press report amid declining production, and confirmed by the July 3, 2026 IACE report as "validated by the Council of Ministers but never transmitted to the ARP," alongside the Investment, Water, and Environment codes above.
  • The gambling/online-betting reform bills remain under General Legislation Committee review since a January 29, 2026 referral, with no confirmed timeline; the Tax Adviser Organic Law (proposition de loi organique n° 2023-013) showed incremental forward movement — a June 3–4, 2026 session was reported as the commission's "last examination session" on disputed articles — but no confirmed article-by-article vote conclusion or committee-report transmission has followed as of this run, so it remains formally not finalized.
  • The Proposition de loi n° 42/2024 (social-media sales cahier des charges, consumer-protection chapter, 3-day right of withdrawal) already documented under Digital/Commercial Law — a June 24, 2026 secondary source described the working group's text as reaching a "near-final" shape, but no committee vote or report is confirmed, so the cahier-des-charges-vs-electronic-declaration debate remains formally open; beyond it, no comprehensive general e-commerce statute has been identified.
  • CNAM / national health-insurance reform — the January 2026 tripartite payment-guarantee agreement and the July 1, 2026 pharmacist payment-commitment addressed successive liquidity flashpoints (see standalone entry) amid a ~TND 1.95 billion 2025 debt to public hospitals, but a structural reform bill (splitting social-service management from health-insurance management) has still not reached the ARP. (Confirmed Run 46) The private biologists' union's tiers-payant suspension will proceed as planned on July 13, 2026 — not averted; family-doctor-scheme patients keep coverage but must pay upfront and seek reimbursement, other insured categories lose direct billing at participating private labs. Watch next run for the practical fallout and whether a late negotiated fix emerges, as occurred with pharmacists on July 1.
  • The Combating Financial Exclusion bill (Draft Law No. 023/2024, its number corrected in Run 43 — see standalone entry) and the related but separate "banque postale" bill (Proposition de loi n° 82/2025, now its own standalone entry) both remain temporized/under review as of June 28, 2026, confirmed unchanged; the Proposition de loi n° 83/2025 (stadium violence) and the nationality-code revision bill (n° 17/2026, 36 co-sponsors, filed by Deputy Syrine Mrabet, examined by committee May 7 and June 4, 2026) both remain under committee review with no vote scheduled and no progress reported since Run 38.
  • The National Council for Tunisians Residing Abroad reform bill (n° 2024/064 — see Nationality section) — under ARP Foreign Affairs Committee review since 2024 with ongoing hearings on Council restructuring and diaspora individual-voting; and the cinema-industry bill (Proposition de loi n° 2024-03 — see Tourism/Culture section) — a June 24, 2026 session was described as "continuing the study" rather than a completed article-by-article finalization (corrected in Run 39 from an earlier overstatement), and still awaits its committee report. Both should be watched for referral to plenary.
  • The Unified Electoral Code proposal (261 articles/three parts, transmitted to committee May 7, 2026) — no movement beyond the May 19, 2026 hearing of the bill's 29 initiating deputies and the unresolved 5,000-dinar candidacy-deposit/education-level debate; note this is a distinct bill from the narrower September 27, 2024 ISIE dispute-jurisdiction amendment already enacted (see Electoral Law entry).
  • The Draft Organic Law on Sports Structures (see Sport & Youth section) — approved in principle by the Council of Ministers November 29, 2025, under ARP Education/Sports Commission review since March 5, 2026, continuing past the hearing stage into committee-report drafting (per the standalone entry's Run 30 update); no report completion or plenary vote confirmed since Run 38.
  • (Added Run 45) The Penal Code theft/robbery penalty amendment (Arts. 261/262/264 — see standalone Criminal Law entry) — in General Legislation Committee hearings since April 2026, opposed by the Syndicat des Magistrats Tunisiens as disproportionate; watch for a committee vote or report.
  • (Added Run 45) The Drug Law (No. 92-52) reform bill (see Narcotics entry) — a proposal balancing tougher trafficking enforcement with a therapeutic approach to consumption, in committee since April 2025 with STADD input as of May 2026; no vote confirmed. (Flagged Run 46) the bill's article count is reported inconsistently across sources (17 vs. 40 articles) — unresolved, do not pick one figure without a primary ARP/JORT text.
  • (Added Run 45) The construction-regularization bill updating Law No. 2015-11 (see Urban Planning Code entry) — approved in amended form by the ARP Strategic Planning/Urban Planning Committee on June 23, 2026; its registry number is still unconfirmed as of Run 46 and should be captured once reported.
  • (Added Run 46) The ONAT (Bar Association) — government standoff (see Judicial Statute entry) — the July 11, 2026 general assembly is confirmed as a strategy-review session, not an election; a separate prosecutorial challenge to the quorum/validity of an earlier lawyers' extraordinary general assembly was reported with a hearing set for June 10, 2026, outcome unconfirmed. Watch for the assembly's outcome and any government response to the June 18, 2026 general strike's demands.
  • (Added Run 94) The "Code des Énergies Renouvelables" (see Renewable Energy Law entry, Energy section) — a Council of Ministers meeting December 24, 2024 ordered its completion; transmitted to the ARP's competent committee April 1, 2026, containing a contested 51%-Tunisian-ownership rule for renewable-energy projects; named by a July 3, 2026 IACE report as one of three reforms (with the Foreign Exchange and Investment codes) it urges enacting by end of 2026. No committee vote or report confirmed since transmission.
  • (Added Run 99) Digital platform ("gig-economy") worker regulation (see standalone Labor Law entry) — no bill or draft text has been filed with the ARP; watch for a legislative response to UGTT's June 29, 2026 demands and the joint ILO governance-framework study announced by Employment Minister Issam Lahmar. Separately, watch for a Tunisian ratification bill for newly-adopted ILO Convention No. 193 (see standalone International Law & Treaties entry) — Tunisia voted in favor at the June 28, 2026 International Labour Conference but has not yet filed domestic ratification legislation.
  • (Confirmed Run 114) Runs 110 through 114 have each re-checked the full watchlist within hours of one another (same calendar day) and found no movement on any item — this is expected given the short intervals, not a sign the watchlist is stale. The four dated triggers (July 6, 8, 11, 13, 2026) remain the points at which real status changes are most likely to appear; re-running the identical same-day sweep between now and the first of those dates has low marginal value. The next run should prioritize checking whether July 6 (Foreign Exchange Code committee meeting) has actually passed before repeating this sweep.
  • (Added Run 115) The Proposition de loi n° 30/2026 (see standalone Social Security & Protection entry) — ending the cumul-emploi-retraite ban and granting retiree transport/tourism tariff reductions — remains under ARP Health/Social Affairs Commission review (last confirmed session May 25, 2026) with an unresolved Article 5 debate over whether full statutory repeal is needed; watch for a committee vote or report, and for whether the bill's own tariff percentages (reported inconsistently as 30%/40% for transport and 40%/"40–50%" for tourism) are settled in a later text.
  • (Added Run 117) The July 6, 2026 Foreign Exchange Code committee-meeting trigger date has now arrived, but no source confirms whether a session actually occurred or what its outcome was; the last hard data point remains chair Maher Ktari's ~90%-complete hearing count as of June 17, 2026 with an internally-targeted (unconfirmed) article-by-article vote window of June 20–30, 2026. The next run should search specifically for post-July-6 reporting on this committee — not just re-run the standard sweep — since this is the first of the four dated triggers to actually pass.
  • (Added Run 118) A new, fifth dated trigger to watch: ARP Finance Commission president Maher Ktari's June 9, 2026 statement that a supplementary/"rectificative" finance law for 2026 is now "inevitable" in September–October 2026 (see the Finance Law 2026 entry's pending-amendment note), driven by the gap between the enacted law's USD 63.3/barrel oil-price assumption and actual prices near USD 110/barrel. No amending bill has been filed yet; watch for the scheduled July 2026 Finance Ministry meeting on the discrepancy and for any move from "inevitable" rhetoric to an actual ARP-filed text.
  • (Confirmed Run 119) The July 6, 2026 Foreign Exchange Code trigger has now technically passed with no reported vote or session outcome from any source — the commission was still working the file as of a June 23, 2026 status report. Independent economist Ridha Chkoundali corroborates Ktari's oil-shock financing-gap figures on the supplementary finance law item. The July 11 ONAT general assembly could not be independently re-corroborated this run (only May 2026 events surfaced in the sweep) — not a retraction of the existing Run 46 sourcing, but flag for fresh confirmation alongside its outcome next run.
  • (Added Run 119) Consumer-protection, telecom, and public-private-partnership regulation — a rotating gap-scan found no active 2026 reform bill in any of these three areas beyond the existing PPP Law No. 2015-49 and e-commerce entries; flagged as a standing candidate for a future dedicated gap-scan rather than padded with unverifiable content now.
